If it stepped on the table<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Not in the public domain nor in its owner’s.</i> and by that the glass broke<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Damages by “foot” not in an authorized domain.</i>. He pays full damages fort the table and half damages for the glass. If there were two tables on top of one another<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Not in the public domain nor in its owner’s.</i>. If it stepped on the upper one and the lower one was broken<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Damages by “foot” not in an authorized domain.</i>. He pays full damages fort the upper table and half damages for the lower. If the table was lying in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Where one has the right to lead one’s animals and payment for damages by “foot” are excluded.</i>, [the animal] stepped on it and it ricocheted, went and broke into a private domain<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Where full payment is due for damages.</i>. What do you follow, the stepping or its breaking? If you say, after stepping<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Substitution of עַל by prothetic אַ is Babylonian usage.</i>, he is free [from paying] for its stepping on it; if you say, after its breaking, then he is guilty for the breaking<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 17b-18a" href="/Bava_Kamma.17b-18a">Babli, 17b/18a</a>, after a lengthy discussion decides that in case of damage caused by a chain of events, the location of the trigger event determines liabilities. It also notes that rules of payment of less than full damages for events not classified as “horn” are traditional, rather than biblical.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Chickens<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The usual Babylonian Amoraic and modern term for “chicken”, based on Aramaic/Syriac, is תַּרְנְגוֹל. But almost all Mishnah mss. and also the early Babli prints in this Mishnah have תרנוגל, mirroring the original Sumerian <i>turlugallu</i>.</i> are notorious<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Its owner has to pay full damages.</i> for breaking things in their usual walk. If a thread<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Something thin, root דל.</i> was bound to its foot or it was scraping<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Arukh reads חדס, from Arabic خدش “to tear with one’s nails; to scrape”.</i> and broke vessels, he pays half damages.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Chickens are notorious,” etc. Rav Huna said<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Arukh reads חדס, from Arabic خدش “to tear with one’s nails; to scrape”.</i>, if it<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The thread on the chicken’s foot. The chicken’s owner can be faulted for insufficient watchfulness.</i> became tied by itself. But if he tied it<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner tied the thread himself, perhaps to identify the chicken, and the thread caused damage, this is no longer a case only of insufficient watchfulness. The owner has to pay full damages under the heading of “pit”.</i>, he pays full damages. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 17b" href="/Bava_Kamma.17b">Babli 17b</a>.</i>: “If chickens scraped dough or fruit, or they pecked, he pays full damages<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This is damage of “foot”, for which they are notorious.</i>. If they scraped dust on dough or on fruit, one pays half of the damages<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">This is uncommon damage.</i>. If they were picking at a rope<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta adds correctly: connected to a pail of a cistern.</i> and the pail was separated<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">It fell into the cistern and cannot be recovered.</i> or broke, he pays full damages. If one<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">One pail on another; cf. Notes 30 ff.</i> fell on another; for the uppermost he pays full damages; for the lower one half damages; it is not comparable to a pit at one place which causes damage at another place<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">And therefore it cannot be characterized as “pit” which pays full damages in all cases.</i>.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, since it usually falls only into this cistern, it is as if it were his pit. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 17b" href="/Bava_Kamma.17b">Babli 17b</a>.</i>: “If chickens fell in a garden, broke vegetables, and cut rosemary<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This translation is tentative; it follows <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Gittin 68b" href="/Rashi_on_Gittin.68b">Rashi, <i>Giṭṭin</i> 68b</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Berakhot 53b" href="/Berakhot.53b"><i>Berakhot</i>53b</a>, Rashi explains: spikenard. Loew, <i>Flora</i> I, p. 10 wants to explain: stems (based on the Tosephta which switches the verbs referring to <i>ḥilfin</i> and vegetables.) At other places (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Kelim 17:17" href="/Mishnah_Kelim.17.17">Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 17:17</a>), the Gaonic commentary identifies חלף with Arabic خلاف “willow”; sometimes it means حلفة “Alfalfa grass”.</i>, he pays full damages. Symmachos said, for cutting he pays full damages, for breaking he pays half damages. If a chicken flew from one place to another and did damage with its body, he pays full damages. By the wind between its wings, he pays half damages; Symmachos says, full damages. If it blew at vessels and broke them, he pays full damages<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">For indirect damage, caused by the turbulence generated by the chicken’s wings, he pays half damages. But damage caused by the bird directly blowing at something is like damage caused by touch of its body.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How is the tooth notorious to eat what is fitting for it? A domesticated animal<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Cattle, sheep, or goats.</i> is notorious for eating fruits and vegetables. If it ate a garment or vessels, he pays half damages<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This leaves open the question of the owner’s responsibility if a plant eating animal eats meat.</i>. When has this been said? In the domain of the person sustaining the damage. But on public property<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since a shepherd has the right to lead his flock on a public road, anybody who puts a vessel into the public domain, even if it creates no nuisance, must assume that it will be broken. Therefore, nothing is due.</i> he is free; but if it benefited, he pays what it benefited<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle, applicable to all kinds of damages.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How is the tooth notorious,” etc. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The abbreviation Resh Laqish for Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is Babylonian; in the Yerushalmi it is a sign of an intrusion of Babylonian spelling.</i> said, it<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The animal’s owner does not have to pay if his animal ate food in the public domain. If it ate vessels or garments, the damage is classified as “horn” and the owner has to pay half damages unless the animal has been certified as a notorious eater of vessels or garments, when full damages are due. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 20a" href="/Bava_Kamma.20a">Babli, 20a</a>, this opinion is attributed to Samuel and R. Joḥanan. Since in money matters the Babli recognizes Samuel and R. Joḥanan as authorities, the Babli endorses this opinion.</i> refers to the first part. Rebbi Joḥanan said, it refers to all<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Even for vessels and garments no payment is due if they were left exposed on a public thoroughfare. In the Babli, this is the opinion of Rav and R. Simeon ben Laqish. Since the Yerushalmi recognizes R. Joḥanan over R. Simeon ben Laqish, it endorses this opinion.</i>. The argument of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish seems inverted. There, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: If it stood still and ate from heaped produce, it<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In reality not the animal is responsible but its owner.</i> is responsible<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish should have qualified his statement; even animals feeding in the public domain will cost their owners if their behavior deviates from the norm.</i>. And here, he says so? They said, there in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia; but here, in his own name. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish’s word implies that if a walking [animal] kicks one which is lying down<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 20a" href="/Bava_Kamma.20a">Babli 20a</a>; for an animal to lie down and ruminate on a thoroughfare is deviant behavior.</i>, is it<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In reality not the animal is responsible but its owner.</i> free [from payment]. Rebbi Joḥanan’s word implies that if a walking [animal] kicks one which is lying down, it<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In reality not the animal is responsible but its owner.</i> is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">He rejects the notion that deviant behavior of one party justifies the other. In the Babli, R. Joḥanan is presumed to agree with R. Simeon ben Laqish in this case.</i>. Not only if the walking [animal] kicks one which is lying down or one which is lying down kicks the walking one. If a walking [animal] kicks a walking one? Rebbi Immi said, Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, only if a walking [animal] kicks one which is lying down, it<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In reality not the animal is responsible but its owner.</i> is free [from payment]. But in the case of one lying down against one walking or one who was walking against one walking, it<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In reality not the animal is responsible but its owner.</i> is obligated<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 20a" href="/Bava_Kamma.20a">Babli 20a</a>; for an animal to lie down and ruminate on a thoroughfare is deviant behavior.</i>. Rebbi Hoshaia stated: It<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In reality not the animal is responsible but its owner.</i> always is free. What is Rebbi Hoshaia’s reason; is there no “horn” in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">He rejects the notion that deviant behavior of one party justifies the other. In the Babli, R. Joḥanan is presumed to agree with R. Simeon ben Laqish in this case.</i>? Rav said, if it stood still and ate from heaped produce, [it is obligated]<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The addition is the reading common to E and the Rome fragment edited by A. H. Freimann (<i>Tarbiz</i> 6, 56–63). Rav (and Ilfai, quoted later in the paragraph) hold that the exemption for “tooth” granted in the public domain only holds if the road is used as road, not as place for grazing. If the shepherd allows the animals of his herd to stand for grazing, he is responsible for any damage done. “Heaped produce” is grain arranged in an orderly heap, a sign of illegal use of the public domain for private use.</i>. Since the leniency for “tooth” is in case it ate walking from the standing<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">If cattle, sheep, or goats are driven as a herd, they may eat from what was stored in the public domain.</i>; the restriction for “horn” is in case the walking animal kicked the one standing still<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the herder of the animal standing still should have driven it away.</i>. Ilfai said: If it stood still and ate from heaped produce, it<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In reality not the animal is responsible but its owner.</i> is obligated. Since the leniency for “tooth” is that it ate walking {from the standing}<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">E and Vatican mss. read: מְהַלֶּכֶת מִן הַמְהַלֶּכֶת “the walking animal ate from another walking animal”. This refers to Ilfai’s statement in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 20a" href="/Bava_Kamma.20a">Babli, 20a</a>: “An animal in the public domain which stretched out its neck and ate from upon another animal is obligated;” a statement explained away in the Babli. The change has to be rejected as an attempt to harmonize the Yerushalmi with the Babli.</i> [from the walking]; the restriction for “horn” is in case the walking animal kicked the walking one<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">While Ilfai agrees with Rav on the rules of “tooth”, he disagrees with him on “horn” and holds that for “horn” injuries the owner of the agressive animal always has to pay.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How does he pay for what it benefited from? If it ate on the public square, he pays for what it benefited from<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">At a place open for public use, the animal’s owner pays only the price of animal feed even if it ate human food.</i>. On the borders of the public square<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">The space is not for use; its status is of domain open neither for the person responsible for causing the damage nor the person suffering it.</i>, he pays for what it damaged. From the door of a store<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner of the store encroached on the public domain to exhibit his wares.</i>, he pays for what it benefited from; inside the store he pays for what it damaged.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “How does he pay for what it benefited from,” etc. Rav said, if it bent its neck and ate, he pays for what it damaged<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">This is a direct continuation of the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 2:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.2.3.2">Halakhah 3</a>. The exemption of “tooth” only applies to an animal grazing in walking; not to one turning out of its way to feed. The same opinion is held by Rav in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 21a" href="/Bava_Kamma.21a">Babli, 21a</a>; there, the authoritative opinion is that of Samuel who exempts all feeding in the public domain.</i>. But did we not state: “From the door of a store, he pays what it benefited from; inside the store he pays for what it damaged”? How is that? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it that a donkey carried lambs and when it passed by, they bent their necks and ate; from the door of a store, he pays what they benefited from; inside the store he pays for what they damaged. Rav said, if it ate barley, he pays for straw<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Probably, not straw without grain is meant but עָמִיר, entire stalks with the kernels, as formulated in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 20a" href="/Bava_Kamma.20a">Babli, 20a</a>.</i>. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state and disagree? <sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta 1:7</a>; partially quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 20a" href="/Bava_Kamma.20a">Babli, 20a</a>.</i>“Therefore, if it ate wheat which is bad for it, it is free. If it changed and ate, he pays full damages. What means ‘if it changed and ate, he pays full damages’? If somebody had his chest hanging on his back and a cow stretched out its neck and ate from it, [its owner] pays full damages<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since the remaining grain is no longer fit for human consumption.</i>. If a large container was at the entrance to a store, partially outside and partially inside; if a cow stretched its neck and ate from it, he pays full damages<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since the remaining grain is no longer fit for human consumption.</i>.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> For a dog or a kid goat which jumped from a roof and broke vessels one pays full damages<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the vessels were in the public domain.</i> because they are notorious. If a dog stole a hot pitta<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">חררה is a piece of dough being baked over an open fire.</i> and went to a stack of sheaves; while it was eating the hot pitta it ignited the stack. For the pitta [its owner] pays full damages, for the stack<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Which is lost by indirect action of his property.</i> he pays half damages.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “For a dog or a kid goat which jumped from a roof,” etc. There are Tannaïm who state: “jumped”. There are Tannaïm who state: “fell”. Rebbi Joḥanan said, so is the Mishnah: “jumped”. But “fell” does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">One cannot say that dogs or kid goats are notoriously apt to fall from roofs; therefore, their owners either are free or at most pay half damages. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 21b" href="/Bava_Kamma.21b">Babli disagrees, 21b</a>.</i>.
“A dog stole a hot pitta.” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, only if he lit every single ear of grain<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 22a" href="/Bava_Kamma.22a">Babli, 22a</a>, explains that R. Simeon ben Laqish holds that damage by fire is like any other damage caused by a person’s property. Therefore, the fire not directly caused by the dog should not lead to liability of the dog’s owner.<br>The Vatican fragment reads נעשה במצית את האור. This is a conflation of the Leiden text with a reading נַעֲשֶׂה כְמַצִית אֶת הָאוֹר “he is considered <i>as if</i> he had lit every stalk separately”, that every stalk creates a new obligation.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, he is like a person who shoots an arrow from one place to another<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">This is action from a distance; the owner is liable even if his property only shot the arrow or started the fire.</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Tevelai said, a Mishnah supports Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: “If a kid goat was bound to it and a slave was close by and was burned, he is obligated. If the slave was bound and the kid goat close by, he is free.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.7.1">Mishnah 6:7</a>. If a person set fire to a haystack, he has to pay for the hay and everything inside. If an animal was bound in the haystack, a slave was close to it, and both were burned, the arsonist has to pay for the animal; he is not responsible for the slave, who was not bound and could have saved himself. But if the slave was bound and the animal free, then the arsonist is a murderer. Nobody is both executed and pays for the same crime; even if he cannot be convicted for lack of eyewitnesses to the crime he cannot be made to pay (<i>Terumot</i> 7:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.2-19"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1</a>). Therefore, he cannot be made to pay for the animal.</i>” But<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The argument advanced by R. Isaac bar Tevelai is not spelled out. The counter argument is presented: If the arsonist were personally liable only for the first stalk which he lit then by the time the fire reached the bound slave it no longer was his fire and there is no reason why he should not be made to pay for the animal. Only R. Joḥanan’s position is consistent with the Mishnah.</i> if you say that he is not like a person who shoots an arrow from one place to another, then for the first ear he is guilty of a capital crime; for the remainder he should be liable for repayment. Rebbi Yose said, you also understand that from: “If his cattle set fire to a stack of sheaves on the Sabbath, he is obligated. But if he set fire to a stack of sheaves on the Sabbath, he is free.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.10.1">Mishnah 3:12</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.10"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Note 44</a>.</i>” But if you say that he is not like a person who shoots an arrow from one place to another, then should he not become obligated since for the remainder he should be liable for repayment<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The argument advanced by R. Isaac bar Tevelai is not spelled out. The counter argument is presented: If the arsonist were personally liable only for the first stalk which he lit then by the time the fire reached the bound slave it no longer was his fire and there is no reason why he should not be made to pay for the animal. Only R. Joḥanan’s position is consistent with the Mishnah.</i>. A person did put out a hot water bottle<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since making fire on the Sabbath is a capital crime, he cannot be sued for civil damages. But if his animals start a fire, no crime is involved, and the owner is fully responsible in a civil suit.</i> outside<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>foris</i>, “outside, in the open”. The Escurial text and the Vatican fragment offer a second reading פירון (cf. Latin <i>forum</i> “outside place, public place; market”), and a parallel text חד בר נש אמלי אסרטא “a person filled the thoroughfare”.</i>. A donkey passed by and broke it. The case came before Rebbi Isaac bar Tevelai, who said to him: He does not owe you anything. Not only that but if [the donkey] suffered damage, [you] would be obligated for its damages<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Your merchandise has the status of “pit in the public domain”.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Which one is tame and which one is notorious? Notorious if there was testimony against it for three days. Tame if it behaved during three [days], the words of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In his opinion, a vicious animal can regain the status of tame.</i>. Rebbi Meїr says, notorious if there was testimony against it three times<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is read as including testimony about three different incidents in one day, or on three widely spaced occasions. The notorious animal regains a status of tame only if it regularly can be touched by children. In <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i>(<i>Neziqin</i> 10), this interpretation is attributed to R. Yose.</i>; tame if children touched it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Which one is tame and which one is notorious,” etc. What is Rebbi Jehudah’s reason? “From yesterday and the day before.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:36" href="/Exodus.21.36"><i>Ex.</i> 21:36</a>; a notorious bull is one who was known to gore “yesterday and the day before.” <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 23b" href="/Bava_Kamma.23b">Babli 23b</a>.</i>” How does Rebbi Meїr confirm “from yesterday and the day before”? He explains it by separate gorings<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome fragment: כהפליג נגיחות, “if it spread out its gorings”.</i>, that if it was let out the first day and gored, the second day and it did not gore, the third day it gored, it is not declared a notorious bull unless it gored on three consecutive days<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 24a" href="/Bava_Kamma.24a">Babli, 24a</a>, R. Meïr is credited with the argument that if three different gorings on three different days make a bull notorious then three gorings on the same day certainly will have the same effect. The plural “bulls” in the next sentence negates this interpretation in the Yerushalmi which might follow the <i>Mekhilta</i> and attributes that argument to R. Yose (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.2.6.1">Note 86</a>).<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 37a" href="/Bava_Kamma.37a">Babli, 37a</a>, notes that a bull can be declared notorious on alternate days if it was observed to gore on days 1,3,5 and was tame on days 2,4.</i>. If it was let out the first day and gored bulls, the second it gored dogs, the third it gored pigs: would it be declared a notorious bull for three different kinds on three days<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered, probably because it never arose in practice.</i>? If it was let out the first day and gored, the second day it was not let out, the third day it was let out and gored, we come to the disagreement between Rav Ada bar Aḥawa and Rav Huna, who disagreed<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 68a" href="/Niddah.68a">Babli, <i>Niddah</i> 68a</a>, the opinion attributed here to R. Ada is Rav’s and that of Rav Huna is Levi’s. Since the Babli follows Rav but the opinion of Rav Ada is discredited in the Yerushalmi, the Talmudim come to opposite conclusions.</i>: A menstruating woman<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The text here is incomprehensible; one has to insert two words from the parallel Babli text. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:19" href="/Leviticus.15.19"><i>Lev.</i> 15:19</a> decrees that a menstruating woman is impure for seven days; then she may immerse herself in a <i>miqweh</i> and is pure. But if she has a disccharge “many days outside her period”, she is declared זָבָה “suffering from flux”, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:25-30" href="/Leviticus.15.25-30"><i>Lev.</i> 15:25–30</a> decrees that after she is healed she has to undergo a period of purification and a Temple ceremony to regain purity. Since “days” mean a minimum of two, “many days” mean a minimum of three (cf. H. Guggenheimer, <i>Logical problems in Jewish tradition</i>, in: Ph. Longworth, ed., <i>Confrontations with Judaism</i>, London 1966, pp. 171–196.) Therefore it is clear that one deals here with a menstruating woman who checks herself <i>on the seventh day</i> and finds herself impure. She therefore cannot become pure at the end of the seventh day but falls under the severe rules of the sufferer from flux only if she has a discharge during three consecutive days.</i> checked herself the first day and found herself impure. The second day she did not check. On the third day she checked and found herself impure. Rav Ada bar Aḥawa said in the name of Rav: she certainly is <i>niddah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of <i>niddah</i>, “the menstruating woman”, this has to be read <i>zavah</i>, “the woman suffering from flux” instead; cf. the preceding Note.</i>. Rav Huna said in the name of Rav: it is doubtful whether she is <i>niddah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93,</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of <i>niddah</i>, “the menstruating woman”, this has to be read <i>zavah</i>, “the woman suffering from flux” instead; cf. the preceding Note.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">She has to follow the rules of <i>zavah</i> but, if the Temple will be miraculously rebuilt overnight, she would not bring a sacrifice.</i>. Rav Huna said, I was there at the beginning, I was there at the end, and I was there in the middle when Rav was asked, and he said, it is doubtful. Rav was asked and he said, it is certain; then he reversed himself and said, it is doubtful. Rav Ada bar Aḥawa was only there when he said, it is certain.
Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Meïr for the tame animal and Rebbi Jehudah for testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Testimony before the court which declares a bull notorious. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 24a" href="/Bava_Kamma.24a">Babli, 24a</a>, agrees and declares this to be the opinion of the Tanna R. Yose.</i>, as we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">Similar texts are in the mss. of <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael</i>(<i>Neziqin</i> 10), ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 284.</i>: “A bull which gored three times on the same day is not notorious. Why does the verse say ‘from yesterday and the day before’? For if it mended its ways for three days one after the other it is judged to be tame.” We were thinking to say that practice follows Rav Huna who said following both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Practice partially follows both RR. Jehudah and Meïr.</i>. This is confirmed by Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Meïr for the tame [bull] and Rebbi Jehudah for testimony.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How does one treat the bull of the person causing the damage in the domain of the person sustaining the damage? If it gored, hit, bit, wallowed, or kicked, in the public domain he pays half of the damages. In the domain of the person sustaining the damage, Rebbi Ṭarphon said, full damages; but the Sages say, half damages<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Halakhak 1:4, Note 160.</i>.<br>Rebbi Tarphon said to them: Since in a case where He was lenient with [damage by] tooth or foot in the public domain, where one is free, He was restrictive for them in the domain of the person sustaining the damage, when full damages must be paid, and He was restrictive regarding damage by the horn in the public domain, where one has to pay half damages; is it not logical that we should be restrictive in this case in the domain of the person sustaining the damage, that full damages must be paid? They said to him, it is sufficient for the result of an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> that it should be like the premiss<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">This is an intrinsic limitation of arguments <i>de minore ad majus,</i> derived from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 12:14" href="/Numbers.12.14"><i>Num.</i> 12:14</a> (<i>Sifry Num.</i> 106; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 25a" href="/Bava_Kamma.25a">Babli 25a</a>), discussed by the author (<i>loc. cit.</i> Note 92, p. 183).</i>. Since in the public domain [one pays] half damages, also in the domain of the person sustaining the damage half damages.<br>He said to them, I do not argue horn from horn, I argue horn from foot! Since in a case where He was lenient with [damage by] tooth or foot in the public domain, He was restrictive for damage by the horn, when He was restrictive with [damage by] tooth or foot in the domain of the person sustaining the damage; is it not logical that we should be restrictive [in case of damage] by the horn? They said to him, it is sufficient for the result of an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i> that it should be like the premiss. Since in the public domain [one pays] half damages, also in the domain of the person sustaining the damage half damages.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The bull of the person causing the damage in the domain of the person sustaining the damage,” etc. to the end. <sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.4.2">Halakhah 1:4</a>.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, this follows Rebbi Ṭarphon: “In the domain of the person sustaining the damage, Rebbi Ṭarphon said, full damages; but the Sages say, half damages.”

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A human is always notorious: Whether in error, or intentionally, whether awake, or sleeping, if he blinded another person or broke vessels, he pays full damages<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Irrespective of the circumstances, he pays full restitution for all possible kinds of damages.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A human is always notorious,” etc. Rebbi Isaac said: The Mishnah if both of them were sleeping<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">In the Rome fragment: “So is the Mishnah: if both of them were sleeping.”</i>. But if one was sleeping and another one came to sleep near him, the one who came to sleep near him is notorious<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Kamma 4a:16:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Kamma.4a.16.1">Tosaphot (4a, <i>s.v.</i> כיון</a>) quotes an expanded version: In the Yerushalmi it is noted that a person sleeping is liable only if he went to sleep near vessels, but if he was sleeping when vessels were deposited near him and he broke them, he does not have to pay because others caused the damage; similarly, if one was sleeping, another person went to sleep near him, and they injured one another, the first is free and the later one has to pay.<br>Since none of the ms. sources has this extended text, it may be an example of the Ashkenazic “book Yerushalmi”.</i>.

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody puts his jar down in the public domain and another person stumbled over it and broke it, [the second person] does not have to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Even though a human is always notorious, there are cases where he does not have to pay.</i>; if he was hurt, the owner of the amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah uses interchangeably all words which denote clay vessels used for storage, whether jar or amphora.</i> has to pay his damages<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Creating an obstacle in the public domain is damage by “pit”.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody puts his jar down in the public domain,” etc. Is it not usual for a person to put things<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Both in the Rome (R) and E mss. instead of להניחן one reads להיות מבחין (E), להיות מבחן (R): “do not people usually look where they are going in the public domain?” A person walking in the street should be obligated to observe a minimum of precaution. Then why does the Mishnah give a blanket exemption to the person who breaks another person’s vessel in the publich domain? The same readings are found at the end of the paragraph as declarative statements.</i> in the public domain? Rav said, if it was filling<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This should be read as feminine, מְלַלְאָה [cf. J. N. Epstein, Tarbiz 6 fasc. 1 (1934), p. 64–65.]</i> the entire public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 27b" href="/Bava_Kamma.27b">Babli 27b</a>. If one does not stop traffic, one is permitted to put down his load in the public domain.</i>. But if it does not fill the entire public domain, is it not usual to put things in the public domain? Samuel said, either if it was filling the entire public domain or if it is at a corner<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The person who turns around the corner could not see the obstacle in front of him. In the Babli, this is attributed to R. Joḥanan; Samuel applies the Mishnah to accidents in the dark. (In R, the statements of Rav and Samuel are conflated to one short text.)</i>. Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, his is confirmed as the Galilean point of view.</i> said, even if it does not fill the entire public domain (if he took it from here and put it down there he would create a “pit”)<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">This has to be eliminated as dittography from the following paragraph; it is not in the parallel mss.</i> and even if it was at a corner; it is not usual for a person to put things<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Both in the Rome (R) and E mss. instead of להניחן one reads להיות מבחין (E), להיות מבחן (R): “do not people usually look where they are going in the public domain?” A person walking in the street should be obligated to observe a minimum of precaution. Then why does the Mishnah give a blanket exemption to the person who breaks another person’s vessel in the publich domain? The same readings are found at the end of the paragraph as declarative statements.</i> in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Anything left lying in the public domain creates a “pit”.</i>.
If it was filling the entire public domain, if he took it from here and put it down there he would create a “pit”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If the jars block the entire path, a passer-by cannot remove one of them and put it down again in the public domain since then he would create the obstacle and would be liable for any damages his “pit” would cause in the future. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 31b" href="/Bava_Kamma.31b">Babli agrees, 31b</a>, that any obstacle created in the public domain is under the rules of “pit”.</i>. But could he take a stick and break it or step over it and if it broke, it broke<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since the passer-by has the right of way, can he forcibly make himself a path without penalty, or does he normally walk and is free from paying only for incidental damage.</i>? Let us hear from the following: If a bull mounted another<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 28a" href="/Bava_Kamma.28a">Babli, 28a</a>: “If a bull mounted another <i>to kill him</i>”.</i> and the [latter’s] owner came to draw away his own, or [the bull] slipped away, and [the attacker] fell down and died, he is free from payment. If he pushed [the attacker] which then fell down and died, he must pay<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the passer-by is not allowed to break the jars to clear the way for himself.</i>. Could he<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Could the owner of the attacking bull sue the owner of the defending animal for the value of the dead bull, claiming that even if his bull had killed the other’s animal he could have paid the damages.</i> not tell him, if you had not interferred, I would have paid your damages? But he could have answered, If I had not interferred, you would have paid me half the damage<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The attacking bull is under the rules of “horn”; the owner of the defending animal never could have hoped to recover his entire loss.</i>! Think of it, if it was notorious? It is not in your power to drag me through the courts<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Since damages caused by a notorious animal can only be collected by a suit, the owner of the defending animal is justified in his action; the owner of the attacking bull has no claim against him.</i>.
Somebody put down an amphora in the public domain and then another person came and put another one close to the first. If the first person comes to take his own and would move [the second] and put it down at another place, he would create a “pit”<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">If the jars block the entire path, a passer-by cannot remove one of them and put it down again in the public domain since then he would create the obstacle and would be liable for any damages his “pit” would cause in the future. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 31b" href="/Bava_Kamma.31b">Babli agrees, 31b</a>, that any obstacle created in the public domain is under the rules of “pit”.</i>. But could he take a stick and break it or step over it and if it broke, it broke<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since the passer-by has the right of way, can he forcibly make himself a path without penalty, or does he normally walk and is free from paying only for incidental damage.</i>? Let us hear from the following: If a bull came to mount another<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 28a" href="/Bava_Kamma.28a">Babli, 28a</a>: “If a bull mounted another <i>to kill him</i>”.</i> and the [latter’s] owner came to draw away his own, if he drew him away before it could mount, and [the attacker] fell down and died, he is free from payment. If he pushed [the attacker] which then fell down and died, he must pay<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the passer-by is not allowed to break the jars to clear the way for himself.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, we can hear from this that if one saw a flood threatening in his irrigation canal, before the flood reached his field he is permitted to divert the water to another place<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Which would damage the property of other farmers whose fields are irrigated by the same canal.</i>; after the flood reached his field he is not permitted to divert it to another place<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This is like pushing the agressive bull off its victim, which makes the victim’s owner liable for damage to the agressive bull.</i>. If the <i>chrysargyros</i><sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Χρυσάργυρος, “alloy of gold and silver; tribute of gold and silver”, a Roman tax imposed by Constantine I, repealed by Anastasios I. The tax was imposed on professions and forced artisans into guilds. A certain sum was collected from each profession in a taxing district [cf. A. Dessau, <i>Finanzen des alten Rom,</i> Handwörterbuch der Staatswissenschaften, Jena 1900, vol. 3, pp. 949–955]. Before the repartition of the tax, the taxpayer might indicate that certain people exercised the same profession as himself without being registered as members of the guild. By this act, he increased the pool of taxpayers and reduced his own share of the tax load. But after repartition of the tax, such a denunciation would lead to the others having to pay tax without his own obligation being reduced; that is forbidden.</i> was imposed, before [the collector of] <i>chrysargyros</i> arrived, one is permitted to say, X is following my profession, Y is following my professon. Once the [the collector of] <i>chrysarguros</i> arrived, it is forbidden. It is permitted to bribe the quartermaster<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">אכסניי פרכא, from Greek ξενία “hospitality, lodging”; πάροχος, Latin <i>parochus</i> (pure Latin term <i>copiarius</i>) “purveyor; furnisher of conveniences to travelling magistrates and officers in Roman provinces”. If the expression is taken as one word, cf. ξενοπάροχος, Latin <i>xenoparochus</i>, “one who provides for strangers” (E. G.).</i> before the Roman [soldiers] arrive<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The bribe is given to spare one’s house from receiving soldiers to put up. The entire paragraph is quoted <i>in extenso</i> and commented on in the <i>Responsa</i> of Simeon b. Ṣemaḥ Duran, vol. 3, # 46.</i>; after the Roman [soldiers] arrive it is forbidden.
A person put down a stone in the public domain, another person put down another stone next to the first, a third person stumbled over the first and struck the other<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">He is injured by the second stone but would not have been injured had he not stumbled over the first.</i>. Who has to pay damages, the first or the second person? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.1">Tosephta 6:1</a>; quoted also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 53a" href="/Bava_Kamma.53a">Babli, 53a</a>.</i>: “A bull pushed another ox which then fell into a pit. The owner of the bull has to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Under the rules of “horn”, full damages if the ox was notorious, half damages if it was tame (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Kamma 53a:11:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Kamma.53a.11.1">Tosaphot 53a, s.v. בעל</a>).</i>; the ‘owner’<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The pit or any obstacle classified as “pit” is in the public domain; it is not property of the person who created the obstacle. But the verse, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:34" href="/Exodus.21.34"><i>Ex.</i>21:34</a>, calls him “owner of the pit”. In the language of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 29b" href="/Bava_Kamma.29b">Babli (29b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6b" href="/Pesachim.6b"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 6b</a>) “it is not a person’s property but the verse treats it as if it were his property.” The biblical expression is used as a technical term to denote liability.</i> of the pit is free. Rebbi Nathan says, if the attacker was notorious, each of them pays one half<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Babli, R. Nathan holds that the ‘owner’ of the pit, the place where the attacked animal died, is the primary payor and, under the rules of “pit”, has to pay 100% of the damages. Then he has regress on the owner of the attacking bull for half of his cost.</i>; if it was tame, the owner of the bull<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the reading of the text is confirmed by the Rome and Escurial mss., it seems that one has to read “pit” for “bull” and vice versa in all occurrences.</i> pays two thirds<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">In all of Talmudic literature, the expression “<i>n</i> parts” means “<i>n</i> parts of <i>n</i>+1”, following Egyptian arithmetic practice. In the statement here, all commentators, from Eliahu Fulda to S. Lieberman, claim that “two parts” means “two parts out of four”, i. e., one half. This contradicts Talmudic practice. One has to accept the statement of R. Ḥanina (in the Rome ms.: R. Isaac) that the text is corrupt and has to be corrected. Only the corrected text is mentioned in Babli and Tosephta.</i> and the ‘owner’ of the pit<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the reading of the text is confirmed by the Rome and Escurial mss., it seems that one has to read “pit” for “bull” and vice versa in all occurrences.</i> a quarter.” Rebbi Ḥanania said, so is the <i>baraita</i>: The owner of the bull<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the reading of the text is confirmed by the Rome and Escurial mss., it seems that one has to read “pit” for “bull” and vice versa in all occurrences.</i> pays three quarters and the ‘owner’ of the pit<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the reading of the text is confirmed by the Rome and Escurial mss., it seems that one has to read “pit” for “bull” and vice versa in all occurrences.</i> a quarter<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the Babli, the ‘owner’ of the pit has regress on the owner of the tame bull only for half the damages. Since the owner of a notorious bull has to pay ½, the owner of a tame bull pays ¼ of the damages to the ‘owner’ of the pit who had paid all damages to the owner of the animal which was killed.</i>. (A person put down a stone in the public domain, another person put down another stone next to the first, a third person stumbled over the first and struck the other. Who has to pay damages, the first or the second person?)<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Dittography of the corrector for the printed edition, faithfully reproduced in the Venice print.</i>
A person put down a stone in the public domain, the wind blew it onto a private domain, another person stumbled over it. Is [the first] responsible for damages<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered; it is decided in the negative since a claimant could not prove the law to be on his side.</i>?
A person put down a stone in the public domain, another person stumbled over it and made it roll into a private domain where a third person stumbled over it. Is [the first] responsible for damages<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered; it is decided in the negative since a claimant could not prove the law to be on his side.</i>?
Rebbi Joḥanan said: A person put down a stone and his load in the public domain, a second person stumbled over it while he held a flask in his hand. Whether he stumbled over the stone and hit the ground or hit the stone and stumbled over the ground, [the first] has to pay damages to the person but is free from damages to the flask<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:34" href="/Exodus.21.34"><i>Ex.</i> 21:34</a> limits damages under the category of “pit” to humans and livestock, excluding its load (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.7.1">Mishnah 5:9</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.14">Tosephta 6:14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 28b" href="/Bava_Kamma.28b">Babli 28b</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> 11).</i>. The statement of Rebbi Joḥanan implies that for “pit” damages one is free from damages to vessels. But if he hit it in walking<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">If the person was not injured by the obstacle classified as “pit”, the ‘owner’ of the pit is liable for damages. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 28b" href="/Bava_Kamma.28b">Babli 28b</a>.</i>, he<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The ‘owner’ of the “pit”.</i> has to pay even for the flask.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If his jar was broken in the public domain and another person slipped on the water or was hurt by one of its shards, he must pay. Rebbi Jehudah said, if intentionally, he has to pay; if unintentionally, he is free.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If his jar was broken in the public domain,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar said, they disagree at the moment of the accident. But after the accident everybody agrees that he must pay<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">As explained at length in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 29" href="/Bava_Kamma.29">Babli, 29a/b</a>, Rebbi Jehudah holds that in using the public domain one has to be mindful of others who use the same space. Therefore a person who runs into the scene of an accident is negligent and has no claim for damages. The majority hold that follow-up accidents are the responsibility of the person who caused the first accident. R. Jehudah will agree that if he did not remove the obstacle which the accident created, he becomes ‘owner’ of his ‘pit’ by biblical decree.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the disagreement is both at the moment of the accident and afterwards<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since the public domain is strewn with shards and other debris, these are ownerless and no responsibility can be pinned on anybody.<br>The position of R. Joḥanan is not clear in any of the Talmudim. The Rome text presents two opinions attributed to R. Johanan, the one quoted here and “they disagree at the moment of the accident, but after the accident everybody agrees that he is free.”</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan objected to Rebbi Eleazar: If he does not have to pay at the moment of the accident, is it not an inference <i>de minore ad majus</i> afterwards<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">That he should be freed from liability as explained in the preceding Note. Rome text: “is it not an inference <i>de minore ad majus</i> that afterwards he has to pay?” In S. Lieberman’s opinion (in his notes to A. H. Freimann’s edition of the Rome text), the Rome ms. represents an amalgamation of two different sources.</i>? Why should he be free at the moment of the accident? For a person declares his damaged property in the public domain as ownerless<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Accepted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 29" href="/Bava_Kamma.29">Babli, 29a/b</a>.</i>. A <i>baraita</i> supports both of them, as it was stated: “The Sages agree with Rebbi Jehudah that somebody who puts down his stone or his load in the public domain does not have to pay if another person stumbled over it, for on this condition did Joshua distribute the Land<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody has the right to fair use of public property. It is perfectly normal that one should be able to put down one’s load for a time before continuing on his journey, and other people have to be aware of this and be considerate. Private property on public grounds becomes ‘pit’ only at the moment it is left there unsupervised.<br>In general, “conditions on which Joshua did distribute the Land” refer to public uses of private property; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.7.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:7 Note 107</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:1:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.1.21"><i>Nazir</i> 7:1 Note 40</a>. This condition is not mentioned in any other source.</i>.” Therefore, if Joshua had not distributed the Land on this condition, he would have to pay. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan, if he intended use of the potsherds<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of what R. Jehudah calls “intentional accident” which makes the owner of the jar liable. If the owner of the jar intends to use the shards (e. g., as writing material), he does not declare them ownerless and the reason given for freeing him from payment does not apply.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Eleazar, if he intended to cause damage<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">If he does not clean up after the accident, he shows that he does not care about what happens to others; this explains R. Eleazar’s opinion that after the accident even R. Jehudah requires payment.</i>. Rebbi Ze‘ira and Rebbi La both say that Rebbi Jehudah agrees with the Sages that one has to pay if he declares his bull who gored in the public domain ownerless. Stationary damage cannot be compared to walking damage.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who pours out water in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Illicit use of public domain.</i> is liable for the damage if another person is hurt because of this. He who puts away a thorn, or glass, or who fences his fence with thorns, or [owns] a fence which collapsed into the public domain, is liable for the damage if other persons are hurt because of this<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is somewhat too compressed. If somebody puts away dangerous things (a thorn, broken glass) in the public domain he is liable for any ensuing damage. The same holds if he fences in his orchard by a thorny hedge and the thorns grow into the public domain; he is liable for hurt caused within the public domain. If any of his property, fence, or wall collapses into the public domain, he is liable for any damages caused.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who pours out water in the public domain,” etc. Rav Huna said, when he was falling to the ground. But if his garments were dirtied, one is liable. Rav said, even if his garments were dirtied, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since the text is confirmed by the parallel mss., it cannot be amended. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 30a" href="/Bava_Kamma.30a">Babli, 30a</a>, Rav holds that the person pouring out the water is liable only for clothing, not for injury to the person slipping in the water; Rav Huna objects. Since pouring out water is intentional, even R. Jehudah (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.2.1">Mishnah 2</a>) will agree that ‘pit’ was created at the moment of the pouring; in this case there is no difference in liability during or after the pouring. One has to conclude that the Yerushalmi reports a Babylonian tradition diametrically opposed to that reported in the Babli: Everybody agrees that for personal injury the person who is to blame has to pay; for vessels it is a dispute (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.1.8">Note 34</a>).</i>. The word of Rav implies one is not liable for ‘pit’ damages of vessels.
“If somebody hides a thorn or [a piece of] glass in another person’s wall; if the owner of the wall then tears it down and a third person is hurt, he is liable. The pious people of earlier times used to take them out<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text of the mss. and the Tosephta; it was changed by the corrector of the Venice edition to מצניעין “they buried them”.</i> into their fields, lower than three handbreadths so that the plough would not lift them<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 2:6" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).2.6">Tosephta 2:6</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 30a" href="/Bava_Kamma.30a">Babli 30a</a>. It is unclear who has to pay; probably it is the person who put the glass into the hole in the wall. In the interpretation of the Babli, he only has to pay if the wall was already weakened and one could foresee that it had to be taken down; this certainly is not the position of the Yerushalmi.</i>.”
New Paragraph. “He fences his fence with thorns.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, explain it according to everybody if it grew outwardly<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If the thorns grow into the public domain, the owner of the hedge is responsible. If the hedge is cut exactly at the border of the private domain, most authorities in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 30a" href="/Bava_Kamma.30a">Babli, 30a</a>, hold that people do not walk too close to a wall or a hedge and, therefore, the owner of the hedge is not liable for injuries occurring on his property line.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: Why do we not explain it following Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah who said, three [handbreadths] close to a domain are counted with the domain<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonian sources, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 50a" href="/Bava_Kamma.50a">Babli 50a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.4">Tosephta 6:4</a>, he is quoted as requiring any potential danger in a private domain to be at least <i>four</i> handbreadths away from the boundary. The Yerushalmi version is repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.6.2">Halakhah 5:7 Note 62</a>.</i>? He answered, why are you not satisfied with what Rebbi Joḥanan said; explain it according to everybody if it grew outwardly.
“A fence which collapsed into the public domain.” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: This is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr said, one is liable for damage caused by inaction. This is the following<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.10.4.1">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 10:4</a>, dealing with a wall or a tree close to the public domain which may constitute a danger of imminent collapse; a court order is issued to the owner to eliminate the danger.</i>: “If he was given a deadline by which to cut the tree or demolish the wall and they collapsed, if it happened within the term, he is not liable; after the term, he is liable.” How much is a term? Rebbi Hoshaia stated: 30 days<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 10:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.10.4.2-3"><i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 10:4</a>; in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 118a" href="/Bava_Metzia.118a">Babli, <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 118a</a> as statement of R. Joḥanan. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta (<i>Bava qama</i> 2:5</a>, <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 11:7): At least thirty days.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody take out his straw and his pressed olives<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">After they have been pressed for the production of olive oil and are squeezed dry. This is an illegal use of the public domain.</i> into the public domain to compost, he is liable for any damages another person might incur because of this. Anybody may take it and acquire it<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">By rabbinic convention, anything deposited illegally in the public domain becomes ownerless; it becomes the property of the first person to take it up and remove it from the public domain. The Mishnah in the Babli and most independent Mishnah mss. have this principle spelled out by Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel.</i>. If somebody turns over cow droppings in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This is an act of acquisition; he may collect the droppings but at the same time he becomes the owner of the corresponding ‘pit’.</i>, he is liable for any damages another person might incur because of this.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody take out his straw and his pressed olives,” etc. What did he acquire? The House of Yannai said, the increase in value. Samuel said, the substance<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The person who picks up the composted material from the public domain gets it all without paying according to Samuel; the House of Yannai would require him to pay the price of the original material to the previous owner. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 30b" href="/Bava_Kamma.30b">Babli 30b</a>, in the names of Rav and Ze‘iri.</i>.
Ḥizqiah said, only if he turned it over to acquire<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This now refers to cow dung. The parallel mss. and Medieval quotes all have an additional sentence: If he did not turn it over with the intention of acquiring, it was not acquired.</i>. The word of Ḥizqiah implies that movables can be acquired by turning them over<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This is rather obvious since by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5</a> all movables can be acquired by lifting; turning over seems to be impossible without lifting.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah there is a parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.12"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5, Notes 514</a> ff., in a different formulation with mostly different speakers.</i> But could they be acquired by dragging? Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, this is reasonable for pillars and hard hides<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Which cannot be lifted by a single person; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.12"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5, Note 514</a>.</i>. But soft hides one does not acquire unless one lifts them up.
Rebbi Aḥa said, would that not<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Reading with the Rome fragment לית for ליי in the text. With this paragraph, the fragment ends.</i> be obvious from the reasoning of the rabbis?<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The negative answer is only given at the end of the paragraph.</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The principle that monetary fines are barred in cases of criminal punishment is detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:11-14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.11-14"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1 Notes 51–71</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.7"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1 Note 30</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.12"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5 Notes 515–519</a>. Except for the example of the rebellious son, the passage in <i>Qiddušin</i> is a differently formulated close parallel to the text here.</i> Is the rebellious son liable for double restitution for his first theft?<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The rebellious son (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:18-21" href="/Deuteronomy.21.18-21"><i>Deut.</i> 21:18–21</a>) is stoned if convicted of financing an extravagant lifestyle by repeated theft from his parents. For a first theft he cannot be prosecuted as rebellious son; one would expect that all the rules of theft apply to him. But he cannot be prosecuted as rebellious son if he never was convicted of the first theft. Of the 200% of the stolen amount which the law on theft requires him to pay, the first 100% are restitution. But the second 100% are a fine. Can the fine be collected if in some indeterminate future the conviction might be an integral part of a capital case?</i> Or since he is warned of a capital offense should he be freed? Let us hear from the following: If somebody steals another’s wallet<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The theft occurs in the victim’s home. The thief is guilty of theft at the moment he lifts the wallet; he is guilty of desecrating the Sabbath only when he removes it from the house into the public domain.</i> and removes it on the Sabbath he is liable since he already is liable for the theft of the wallet before he comes in conflict with the holiness of the Sabbath. Is he not forewarned of a capital offense and you say, he is liable! Here<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The case of the rebellious son.</i> also, even though he is forewarned of a capital offense, you say that he has to be liable. Rebbi Yannai bar Ismael said, Rebbi Hoshaia follows his own opinion, as we have stated: He who takes out is guilty even if he did not put down<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">For the rabbis, the Sabbath is desecrated only by a complete prohibited action. Transport has three parts: Lifting an article, transporting it (which under certain circumstances is prohibited on the Sabbath) and putting the article down. For the rabbis, a person cannot be prosecuted for desecration of the Sabbath if he takes an object in a private domain, takes it out to the public domain and never stops moving until the end of the Sabbath (R. Joḥanan in <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1 2b l. 40, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 31a" href="/Ketubot.31a">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 31a</a>). R. Hoshaia disagrees. However, the reason might be that he follows Ben Azai (<i>Šabbat</i>1:1, 2c l. 41; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 31b" href="/Ketubot.31b">Babli <i>Ketubot</i> 31b</a>) who holds that walking is like standing still. Everybody agrees that standing still while carrying something is equivalent to putting it down.</i>. Similarly, he who takes out is guilty even if he did not take up with the intent to take out<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This Tanna holds that an article cannot be acquired by dragging it on the floor, but only by lifting; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 91b" href="/Shabbat.91b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 91b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 86a" href="/Bava_Batra.86a"><i>Bava batra</i> 86a</a>.</i>, for warning is possible only at the moment of his taking out. It was stated: If he was dragging it until he left<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This Tanna holds that an article cannot be acquired by dragging it on the floor, but only by lifting; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 91b" href="/Shabbat.91b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 91b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 86a" href="/Bava_Batra.86a"><i>Bava batra</i> 86a</a>.</i>, he is free since the obligations of capital crime and restitution falls on him simultaneously. Therefore, if both obligations do not fall on him simultaneously, he is liable. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it with large pouches which usually are dragged<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Which cannot be lifted by a single person; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:5:12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.5.12"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:5, Note 514</a>.</i>; one cannot infer anything from this<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">There is no indication that smaller articles can be acquired by dragging.</i>.
One objects from the Mishnah; sometimes one objects in support of Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">In his dispute with R. Hoshaia; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.4.4">Note 67</a>.</i>, sometimes one objects in support of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">It is not known to which statement or disagreement this refers. It seems that one refers to the question whether carrying from one domain to another is forbidden on the Day of Atonement since it is forbidden on the Sabbath (even though this opinion is characterized in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 14a" href="/Keritot.14a">Babli, <i>Keritut</i> 14a</a>, as “stupidity”).</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Keritot 3:4" href="/Mishnah_Keritot.3.4">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 3:4</a>; quoted partially <i>Šabbat</i> 1:1, 2c l. 43.</i>“A person may eat once and be liable for four purification and one reparation sacrifices: An impure person who ate suet which was leftover sacrifice on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Three purification offerings are due for the inadvertent eating of sacrificial meat by an impure person (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:20" href="/Leviticus.7.20"><i>Lev.</i> 7:20</a>), suet (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:23" href="/Leviticus.7.23">v. 23</a>), and leftover sacrificial meat (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:18" href="/Leviticus.7.18">v. 18</a>), a fourth for eating on the Day of Atonement (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:29" href="/Leviticus.23.29"><i>Lev.</i>23:29</a>). A reparation sacrifice is due for the unauthorized use of <i>sancta</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:15" href="/Leviticus.5.15"><i>Lev.</i>5:15</a>).</i>. Rebbi Meïr said, if it was a Sabbath and he took it out<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">He put the meat in his mouth in one domain, went out and ate it in another domain.</i> on the Sabbath. They told him, it is not from that category<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">This sin is committed by carrying, not eating.</i>.” When they objected in support of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he took it out on the Sabbath, there is no putting down<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">If the person ate in walking, the piece of meat disappears without coming to rest.</i>. Why did they say, it is not from that category<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Joḥanan, eating the piece <i>is</i> putting it down. Since for him transporting on the Sabbath is not punishable without bringing the object being transported to rest, the liability is triggered not by carrying outside but by eating. This would be in the same category as the other transgressions.</i>? When they objected in support of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Why is he not liable if he took it out on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Why could R. Meïr not mention transporting on the Day of Atonement? The E text has here an addition:<br>הוֹצִיאוֹ [בַּשַׁבָּת וְהִנִיחוֹ. וְלָמָּה אָמְרוּ אֵינוֹ הַשֵׁם, אֵימַר תִּיפְתָּר בְּשֶׁאָכְלָהּ. וַאֲכִילָה הַנָחָה הִיא. לא מִסְתַּבְּרָה וְלֹא הוֹצִיאוֹ בַּשַׁבָּת חַייָב. הֲרֵי שֶׁהוֹצִיאוֹ] בְּיוֹם הַכִּיפּוּרִים יְהֵא פָטוּר.<br>If he took it out [on the Sabbath and put it down. Why did they say: "it is not from that category"? I would say in explanation that he ate it. Is eating not putting it down? Therefore, it is only reasonable that if he took it out on the Sabbath, he be liable. Then if he took it out] on the Day of Atonement why should he not be liable?<br>The additional text is between the brackets, [ ]. It seems that the scribe of L lost the text between הוציאו and שהוציאו. But since the text does not add anything to the discussion, the addition might be a gloss that entered the text.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, Rebbi Meïr follows Rebbi Aqiba, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Section 9 8" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Section_9.8"><i>Sifra Emor Parašah</i> 9(8</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 101a" href="/Chullin.101a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i> 101a</a> (with R. Yose the Galilean instead of R. Ismael); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Keritot 2:12" href="/Tosefta_Keritot.2.12">Tosephta <i>Keritut</i>2:17</a> (attributions switched).</i>: From where that if one unintentionally worked on a Day of Atonement which fell on the Sabbath, he is liable for each one separately? The verse says, “it is Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:3" href="/Leviticus.23.3"><i>Lev.</i> 23:3</a>.</i>,” “it is the Day of Atonement.<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:28" href="/Leviticus.23.28"><i>Lev.</i> 23:28</a>. In this opinion, R. Meïr details obligation of 6 purification offerings.</i>” Rebbi Ismael said, he is liable only once<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is only one punishment in case of willful transgression, there can be only one sacrifice in case of inadvertent sin.</i>.<br>If he took it out] on the Sabbath and put it down. Why did they say: “it is not from that category”? I would say in explanation that he ate it. Is eating not putting it down? Therefore, it is only reasonable that if he took it out on the Sabbath, he be liable. Then if he took it out [on the Day of Atonement why should he not be liable?

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Two potters were walking one behind the other. The first stumbled and the second stumbled over the first; the first is liable for the damage done to the second. One came with an amphora, the other came with a beam. If the jug of the one was broken by the other’s beam he is not liable since each of them had the right to walk there<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the jug had the duty to keep his distance from any obstacle which endangered his vessel.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Two potters were walking one behind the other,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan asked: Does one give time to the first to get up<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Is stumbling alone an indication of insufficient attention which makes the stumbled person liable to damages? Or is the person who notices that he is falling down required to alert the persons following him to be careful? Or is stumbling an accident for which he is not responsible; his liability then would start only if he does not expeditously clear the way for others. Also in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 31a" href="/Bava_Kamma.31a">Babli, 31a</a>, R. Joḥanan is reported to accept the third alternative.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, it is obvious for Rebbi Joḥanan to give time to the first to stand up. But it is reasonable otherwise from the second one onwards<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">If somebody sees an accident in the road in front of him, does he have to stop faster to avoid a multiple collision?</i>, does one give time to the second to stand up? Let us hear from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">The following text to the end of the paragraph, except for some interspersed editorial comments, is closely related to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 2:8-10" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).2.8-10">Tosephta 2:8–10</a> (partially quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 31a" href="/Bava_Kamma.31a">Babli 31a</a>); these texts are used to correct a scribal error and fill a lacuna in the text.</i>“Potters were walking one behind the other. The first stumbled and fell, the next one stumbled and fell, the next one stumbled and fell. The first one pays to the second, and the second to the third. But if all fell because of the first, he pays for all of them. They are liable for damage to humans but not liable for damage to vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">By the rules of ‘pit’. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 31b" href="/Bava_Kamma.31b">Babli, 31b</a>, denies the definition of ‘pit’ to the first person who caused the mass collision.</i>. But if they (damaged) [warned]<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of Tosephta and Babli.</i> one another, none are liable. Donkey drivers were walking one behind the other. The first stumbled and fell, the next one stumbled and fell, the next one stumbled and fell. The first one pays to the second, and the second to the third. But if all fell because of the first, he pays for all of them. Donkeys were walking one behind the other.
