The first stumbled and fell, the next one stumbled and fell: even 100, all are liable.” Some Tannaïm state, none are liable. Rebbi La said, he who says ‘liable’, if their owner let them lie down. He who says ‘not liable’, if they fell, since animals are not responsible for accidents<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of an animal is not responsible for accidents caused by it. The sentence about donkey drivers was inserted only to show the difference between the drivers and their animals.</i>. “If the feet of a donkey were bad, one is not allowed to step over him. [ ]<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">With the parallels, one has to insert: “If it fell, they are permitted to step over it.”</i>” What does it mean, ‘to step over him?’ They step on his body and pass by<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">If the path is narrow and does not allow for a bypass.</i>. “If one was free, the other loaded, the loaded one has the right of way before the free one. One unloaded<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">It carries empty packaging.</i>, the other one loaded, the loaded one has the right of way before the unloaded one. If both were loaded or both unloaded, let them agree between themselves<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 32b" href="/Sanhedrin.32b">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 32b</a>, the one who gives up his right of way can demand payment from the other party.</i>. Two carriages or two ships, one empty, the other loaded, the loaded one has the right of way before the unloaded one. If both were unloaded or loaded, let them agree between themselves.” <sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Derekh Ereṣ Rabba,</i> Chapter 10; <i>Derekh Ereṣ (Maḥzor Vitry)</i> Chapter 8, end.</i>“The person entering the bath house gives precedence to<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Yields the right of way.</i> the person leaving; the person leaving the toilet gives precedence to the person entering.”
“One came with an amphora, another came with a beam.” Rebbi Ze‘ira asked: If they changed<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">One of the people suddenly changed his speed so that the other crashed into him. One might read this paragraph in connection with the next Mishnah.</i> and caused damage, what is the rule? Let us hear from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 2:9" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).2.9">Tosephta 2:9</a>; partially in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 10b" href="/Bava_Kamma.10b">Babli 10b</a>.</i>“Five people sat on a couch; another came, saton it, and it broke. He pays for all of them. But if it broke because of all of them<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">They invited the sixth; this caused the overload.</i>, they are all liable.”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the carrier of the beam was first and the carrier of the amphora last. If the jar broke on the beam, he<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The carrier of the beam.</i> is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody using the public domain is required to have eyes in the back of his head.</i>; but if the carrier of the beam stopped walking, he is liable, except that he is not liable if he said to the carrier of the amphora: stop! If the carrier of the amphora was first and the carrier of the beam last. If the jar broke on the beam, he<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The carrier of the beam.</i> is liable, but if the carrier of the amphora stopped walking, he is not liable, except that he<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">The carrier of the beam.</i> is liable if he<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">The carrier of the amphora.</i> said to the carrier of the beam: stop! The same applies if one carries fire and the other flax<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">And the flax was burned.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the carrier of the beam,” etc. Did not Rav say: If he filled the entire public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">6,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 27b" href="/Bava_Kamma.27b">Babli 27b</a>. If one does not stop traffic, one is permitted to put down his load in the public domain.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah only applies to a narrow path; on a wide road the second person is required not to walk directly behind the one carrying a dangerous load.</i>? (And this filled the entire public domain.)<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">A corrector’s addition; probably taken unthinkingly from the first paragraph of the Chapter.</i> One said, since he stopped, he is like a street corner<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The person who turns around the corner could not see the obstacle in front of him. In the Babli, this is attributed to R. Joḥanan; Samuel applies the Mishnah to accidents in the dark. (In R, the statements of Rav and Samuel are conflated to one short text.)</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Two were walking in the public domain, or one was running and one was walking, or both were running. If they caused damage one to the other, neither of them is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">A corrector’s addition; probably taken unthinkingly from the first paragraph of the Chapter.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Two were walking” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 2:11" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).2.11">Tosephta 2:11</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 32a" href="/Bava_Kamma.32a">Babli 32a</a> in the language of the Tosephta.</i>“Yose the Babylonian said: If one ran in the public domain and caused damage, he is liable. Late Friday evening he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Anybody exceeding the speed limit is liable for any damage. But late Friday night everybody is running.</i>.” They said, Yose the Babylonian is Yose ben Jehudah, is Yose the small one<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">A longer list is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 113b" href="/Pesachim.113b">Babli, <i>Pesaḥim</i> 113b</a>, bottom.</i>. Why is he called Yose the small one? Because he was the last of the pious<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">A different version in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 9:16:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.9.16.1"><i>Soṭah</i>: Yerushalmi 9:17, Note 275</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 49b" href="/Bava_Kamma.49b">Babli 49b</a>; Tosephta 15:5; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 52b" href="/Yoma.52b"><i>Yoma</i> 52b</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody split [wood] in a private domain<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">A legitimate act which requires precautions to be taken because it is intrinsically dangerous.</i> and caused damage in the public domain, in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">An illegitimate act.</i> and caused damage in a private domain, in a private domain and caused damage in another private domain, he is liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody split [wood] in a private domain,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 2:4:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.2.4.2-3"><i>Makkot</i> 2:4</a>. The text bracketed here is an addition by the corrector, probably from <i>Makkot,</i> recognizable by his standard Babli spelling. The same topic is discussed in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 32b-33a" href="/Bava_Kamma.32b-33a">Babli, 32b/33a</a>.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, if he was splitting wood in his courtyard when a worker entered to claim<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Makkot:</i> “To collect”. In any case, the person entering did not trespass.</i> his wages, and a splinter ricocheted and damaged him, he is liable. If he died he does not go into exile<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">The responsibility of the person splitting wood in his own backyard is in civil, not in criminal law. The text in <i>Makkot</i> adds: “Because it is not as in a forest,” referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:5" href="/Deuteronomy.19.5"><i>Deut.</i> 19:5</a> which describes the circumstances under which the homicide has to dwell in a city of refuge. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 33a" href="/Bava_Kamma.33a">Babli, 33a</a>, quotes the end of the same verse, describing how the blade of an ax “finds his neighbor and he dies”, to exclude one who walks into the path of danger (cf. <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 183).</i>. But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state, “he is not liable”? They do not disagree. What Rebbi Yose said, if he had seen him, but what Rebbi Ḥiyya said, if he had not seen him. If he had not seen him, once he said to him: enter, {should he not be liable? But did not Rebbi Ḥiyya state, “he is not liable”? Since he told him: enter, he has to take precautions<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:27" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.27">Tosephta 6:27</a> it is stated explicitly that the worker, who has the right to go and collect his wages, enters on his own responsibility unless invited in, at which moment the responsibility shifts to his employer.</i>. Some want to say, since he told him to enter it} becomes like the courtyard of partners, about which Rebbi Joḥanan said in the name of Rebbi Yannai<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 5:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.5.1.4"><i>Nedarim</i> 5:1, Notes 22–23</a></i>: Partners acquire from one another in a courtyard and are mutually liable for damages. But did not Rav say: If he filled the entire public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">6,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 27b" href="/Bava_Kamma.27b">Babli 27b</a>. If one does not stop traffic, one is permitted to put down his load in the public domain.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah only applies to a narrow path; on a wide road the second person is required not to walk directly behind the one carrying a dangerous load.</i>? And did this fill the entire public domain? They said, since he<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The worker, looking for his employer to collect his wages.</i> walks in the entire courtyard, it is as if he had filled the entire courtyard.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Two tame bulls which injured one another; one pays half for the excess damage<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since each owner has to pay damages to the other, in effect only the owner of the bull causing the greater damage has to pay for the excess damage his animal did cause, by the statutory rate of 50% for the tame, 100% for the notorious.</i>. If both are notorious, one pays in full for the excess damage. One tame and one notorious; the notorious over the tame pays in full for the excess damage; the tame over the notorious pays half for the excess damage.<br>Similarly, two people who injured one another pay in full for the excess damage<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Since a human always is notorious (2:10).</i>. A human and a notorious animal, or a notorious animal and a human, one pays in full for the excess damage. A human and a tame animal; the human over the tame pays in full for the excess damage; the tame over the human pays half for the excess damage. Rebbi Akiba says, also a tame animal which injured a human pays the excess damage in full<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21" href="/Exodus.21"><i>Ex.</i> 21</a> do not apply to damages caused to humans.</i>.<br>A bull worth a mina<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">100 denars.</i> which gored a bull worth 200 and the cadaver is not worth anything; he<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the dead bull. It is presumed that the aggressor was tame and the damage be paid from its body (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:5</a>).</i> takes the bull. A bull worth 200 which gored a bull worth 200 and the cadaver is not worth anything; Rebbi Meïr said, about this case it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35"><i>Ex.</i> 21:35</a>.</i>: “They shall sell the living bull and split the proceeds.” Rebbi Jehudah said to him, you upheld “they shall sell the living bull and split the proceeds,” but you did not uphold <sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35"><i>Ex.</i> 21:35</a>.</i>“and also the cadaver they shall split.” What is this? That is a bull worth 200 which gored a bull worth 200 and the cadaver is worth 50 <i>zuz</i><sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian half-šeqel, <i>zūz,</i> is identified with the Roman denar.</i>, in which case each owner takes half of the living bull and half from the cadaver<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).3.3">Tosephta 3:3</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> 12.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A bull worth a mina” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The text of this <i>baraita</i> is similar to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 2:5" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).2.5">Tosephta 2:5</a>, but the latter is not an edition of the text underlying the Yerushalmi.</i>“A bull worth a mina which gored a bull worth a mina; weakened (it) in the amount of 50 <i>zuz</i>; he takes the bull, for he gets only half the amount of the injury and therefore, if the bull died or was weakened, he only has that bull<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence assumes that the first bull be tame. The first bull kills the second; the first is weakened by the fight and now is worth only half the value of the bull it killed. The owner of the dead bull takes the living one as exact payment.</i>. A bull worth a mina which gored a bull worth 200 and weakened it in the amount of 50 <i>zuz</i>; then the second [bull] couter-attacked and injured it to the amount of three gold [denars]. The first pays half a gold denar to the second<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">One must read with the Tosephta: “The <i>second</i> pays half a gold denar to the <i>first</i>.” The first bull damaged the second in the amount of 50 silver denar. The second damaged the first for 3 gold denar = 75 silver denar. The excess loss to be compensated for is 25 silver denar = 1 gold denar. If the bulls were tame, half a gold denar would be due.</i>. A bull worth 200 which gored a bull worth 200. It increased in value and now is worth 400; but if it had not gored it, it would now be worth 800 <i>zuz</i>. If it improved before the trial it is judged as at the time of the damage<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the second bull can only claim the diminution of value immediately after the attack, which certainly is less than the 200 denar value of the bull at that moment, not the 400 denar of future loss.</i>. If it was weakened, it is judged as at the time of the trial.” Rebbi La said, so is the <i>baraita</i>: The agressor improved; if it improved before the trial it is judged as at the time of the damage; if it weakened, it is judged as at the time of the trial<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">When expert testimony may be taken.</i>.
What is Rebbi Meïr’s reason? The verse says, <sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35"><i>Ex.</i> 21:35</a>.</i>“they shall sell the living bull and split its proceeds.” How does Rebbi Jehudah support <sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35"><i>Ex.</i> 21:35</a>.</i>“and also the cadaver they shall split”? From here that each one loses half the damages<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 34a" href="/Bava_Kamma.34a">Babli 34a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).3.3">Tosephta 3:3</a>; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 12; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.3.2">Halakhah 1:3</a>, end. The verse proves that half damages for ‘horn’ are paid exclusively from the proceeds of the sale of the attacking animal.</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person may be liable for the action of his bull but not liable for his own action, not liable for the action of his bull but liable for his own action: If his bull puts to shame, he is not liable;<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">An animal cannot insult a human; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.2.2">Chapter 1, Note 17</a>.</i> but if he put to shame, he is liable. If his bull blinded the eye of his slave or knocked out his tooth, he is not liable; but if he himself blinded the eye of his slave or knocked out his tooth, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:26-27" href="/Exodus.21.26-27"><i>Ex.</i> 21:26–27</a> decree only that a slave gain his freedom if he is mistreated by his owner personally.</i>. If his bull injured his father or his mother, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">As for injury to any third person.</i>; but if he himself injured his father or his mother, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Since he committed a capital crime (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:15" href="/Exodus.21.15"><i>Ex.</i> 21:15</a>) and nobody has to pay if he is tried for his life (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.2-15"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Notes 3–73</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.2-19"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1</a>).</i>. If his bull set fire to a stack of sheaves on the Sabbath; he is liable, but if he set fire to a stack of sheaves on the Sabbath, he is not liable since he will be tried for a capital crime<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">Since he committed a capital crime (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:15" href="/Exodus.21.15"><i>Ex.</i> 21:15</a>) and nobody has to pay if he is tried for his life (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.2-15"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Notes 3–73</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.2-19"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The ms. has the text placed correctly after Halakhah 11 but with a wrong header, “<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.10.2">Halakhah 9</a>”. In the <i>editio princeps,</i> the paragraph was then wrongly inserted after <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.8.2">Halakhah 8</a>; it refers to Mishnah 12.</i>: “Two tame bulls,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">The parallels are <i>Šabbat</i> 2:2 (5a l. 31), <i>Erubin 10:12</i> (26c l. 47), <i>Pesaḥim</i>6:1 (33b l. 34); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 34b" href="/Bava_Kamma.34b">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 34b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 106a" href="/Shabbat.106a"><i>Šabbat</i> 106a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Beitzah 12b" href="/Beitzah.12b"><i>Beṣah</i> 12b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yevamot 16b" href="/Yevamot.16b"><i>Yebamot</i> 16b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 62b" href="/Sanhedrin.62b"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 62b</a>.</i> Ben Pedaia said: Spoilers are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">“Liable” and “not liable” here mean “guilty” and “not guilty” under the laws of the Sabbath. In the first introductory paragraph to the laws of the Sabbath (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 16:29" href="/Exodus.16.29"><i>Ex.</i> 16:29</a>) only leaving the camp was forbidden. In the Ten Commandments (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 20:10" href="/Exodus.20.10"><i>Ex.</i> 20:10</a>), “all work” is forbidden without a definition of what constitutes “work”. The missing definition is deduced from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 35" href="/Exodus.35"><i>Ex.</i> 35</a>. That Chapter starts with a repetition of the Sabbath prohibitions and then proceeds with the instructions for the building of the Tabernacle. One concludes that the work necessary for building the Tabernacle is the work forbidden on the Sabbath (<i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Wayyaqhel</i>). Then in v. 33 the work of Beṣalel and Aholiab, the builders of the Tabernacle, is defined as מְלֶכֶת מַחֲשָׁבֶת “thinking work”. The only work which constitutes a punishable desecration of the Sabbath is intentional constructive work. Therefore, any purely destructive work is not punishable.</i> except for one making a fire and one causing a wound. Rebbi Joḥanan said, one making a fire only if he needs the ashes; one causing a wound only if he needs the blood<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Without exception, destructive work without positive effects is not punishable.</i>. But did we not state: “His bull”<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah here.</i>? His bull needs ashes<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 35a" href="/Bava_Kamma.35a">Babli explains, 35a</a>, an intelligent bull which has a wound on its back sets a fire because it needs the ashes to wallow in them to ease its pain.</i>.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a bull was pursuing another bull which was damaged. This one says, your bull did the damage, but the other one says, it was hurt by a rock; the burden of proof is on the claimant<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Without proof that stands up in court he cannot collect any damages.</i>.<br>If two [bulls] were pursuing another [bull]; each of them says: your bull did the damage; neither of them is liable. But if they belonged to one owner, he is liable for both of them.<br>If one was large, the other small. The person sustaining the damage says that the large one did the damage<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since for claims of damages caused by a ‘tame’ animal the amount which can be recovered is limited by the value of the attacking animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.9.3">Note 131</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:5</a>), the person suffering the loss is interested in raising that limit.</i> but the person causing the damage says that the small one did the damage, the burden of proof is on the claimant.<br>If two were damaged, one large and one small, and the [bulls] doing the damage were two, one large and one small. The person sustaining the damage says that the large one damaged the large one<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Since for claims of damages caused by a ‘tame’ animal the amount which can be recovered is limited by the value of the attacking animal (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.9.3">Note 131</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.5.1">Mishnah 1:5</a>), the person suffering the loss is interested in raising that limit.</i> and the small one the small; but the person causing the damage says that the small one did damage the large one and the large one the small. [Or] one tame and one notorious, the person sustaining the damage says that the notorious one damaged the large one<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">In order to recover his entire loss without limitation.</i> and the tame one the small; but the person causing the damage says that the tame one did damage the large one and the notorious one the small. The burden of proof is on the claimant.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person may be liable for the action of his bull,” etc. Rebbi Yudan asked: How is the court trial<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">This really is a continuation of examples to Mishnaiot 13–16 applying the principle that “the burden of proof is on the claimant”, even if the resulting judgment be logically inconsistent.</i>? Let us hear from the following: “Two persons threw two pebbles and broke two jugs, one full of wine and one full of oil. Each of them said, I broke the one full of wine. Both of them pay for the wine. One empty and one full; each of them said, I broke the empty one. Both of them pay for the empty one. If they broke one amphora. Each of them said, you broke it. Neither of them is liable.

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A bull which gored four or five bulls, one after the other<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A tame bull went on a goring spree before it could be declared notorious in court. The damage which it caused has to be liquidated by the rules of the tame bull; i. e., the bull has to be sold and from the proceeds half the damages have to be paid.</i>, should pay for the last of them<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Meïr, a bull which gored becomes the property of its victim’s owner. The original owner has a claim to the excess of the sale price over the claim for restitution.<br>Assuming that the bull was worth <i>a</i>; it gored <i>n</i> bulls, worth <i>a</i><sub>1</sub><i>, a</i><sub>2</sub><i>,</i> … <i>,a</i><sub>n</sub>. The owner of the last bull claims <i>a</i><sub>n</sub>/2 from the owner of the bull gored as the penultimate. If <i>a</i> - <i>a</i><sub>n</sub>/2 &gt; 0, the owner of that bull satisfies himself up to the amount <i>a</i><sub>n-1</sub>/2. If <i>a</i> - <i>a</i><sub>n</sub>/2 - <i>a</i><sub>n-1</sub>/2 &gt; 0, the preceding owner can satisfy himself up to <i>a</i><sub>n-2</sub>/2, and so on until all of the proceeds <i>a</i> are used up. In practically all cases, the owner of the attacking bull ends up with nothing.</i> If there is anything left, it should revert to the penultimate. If then there is anything left, it should revert to the one before the penultimate. The later one is always advantaged, the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the original owner and the owner of the victim become co-owners of the attacking bull. The last owner takes <i>a</i>/2; the owner of the penultimate victim then is a 50% owner of the remaining <i>a</i>/2, and so on. In this way, the owner of the <i>j</i>-th bull gored gets <i>a</i>/2<sup class="footnote-marker">n-j</sup><sup class="footnote-marker">+1</sup>; the owner of the attacking bull is left with the same amount as the one given to the owner of the first victim, <i>a</i>/2<sup class="footnote-marker">n</sup>.<br>If the values of the bulls are distinct, the last owner takes <i>a</i><sub>n</sub>/2, the one before him<br>min(<i>a</i><sub>n</sub><sub>-1</sub>/2, <i>a</i>/2-<i>a</i><sub>n</sub>/4)<br>and so on.</i> says, if a bull worth 200 gored a bull worth 200 and the carcass is not worth anything, each one takes a mina. If then it gored another bull worth 200, the last one takes 100 and the preceding each take 50. If then it gored another bull worth 200, the last one takes 100, the preceding one takes 50 <i>zuz</i><sup>4</sup><i class="footnote">Silver denars.</i>, and the two first ones each a gold denar<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">25 silver denars.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A bull which gored four or five bulls,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The basic explanation of this paragraph is given by Nachmanides, <i>Milḥamot Hashem,</i> to Mishnah 4:1.</i> Rebbi Yannai said, Rebbi Meïr attacks him<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The arguments given to the owner of the bull gored last against the owner of the bull gored as the penultimate.</i> from two sides: As you take it, if it is yours<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.1.1">Note 2</a>.</i>, give it to me. If it is not yours<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">But you are part owner as claimed by R. Simeon.</i>, hand it over to me that I may satisfy my claim. But did not Rebbi Eleazar say, the guarding of damages parallels the guarding of acquisitions<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The property rights given by the Torah to the owner of the dead bull is one of ownership, not possession (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1, Note 352</a>). Therefore, the potential owner of the bull cannot be liable for the damages done by an animal not in his actual possession.</i>? <sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 26b" href="/Bava_Kamma.26b">Babli, 26b</a>, this is labelled as R. Aqiba’s opinion.</i> Rebbi Jehudah told him, it was delivered to him for his damages but it remains a pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since he becomes owner for monetary gain, he is obligated for damages as if he were a paid trustee. The second argument attributed to R. Meïr really is valid only if the penultimate owner actually took possession of the offending bull.</i>.
What is between them? Rebbi Joḥanan said, dedication is between them. In Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion, the first [owner]<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In this paragraph, one assumes that the bull gored only once. The first owner is the original one; the second is the the owner of the dead bull.</i> may dedicate<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The second owner is co-owner, but as long as he did not actually take possession of the bull, the first can pay him off as mortgage-holder and be both owner and in possession. Therefore, only he may dedicate since nobody can dedicate what is not in his possession (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.5.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5, Notes 105–106</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 5:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).5.1">Tosephta 5:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 33b" href="/Bava_Kamma.33b">Babli 33b</a>).</i>. In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion, [only] both together may dedicate it<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">While the bull is still in the possession of the first, ownership belongs to the second. They can only act together. The same reasoning applies to the following examples.</i>. If it went up in price. In Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion, it increased for the first. In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion, it increased for the second. If it was rented out, in Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion, the rent belongs to the first. In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion, [the rent] belongs to both of them. If it improved<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:9:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.9.2">Chapter 3, Note 130</a>.</i>, in Rebbi Jehudah’s opinion, the improvement belongs to the first. In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion, the improvement belongs to both of them.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Simeon stated the same. Just as Rebbi Jehudah said, he acquired, so Rebbi Simeon said, he acquired<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The owner of the injured animal becomes part owner with the owner of the attacker.</i>, only that Rebbi Jehudah says, he acquired the living and the dead<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.10.1">Chapter 3, Note 135</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 3:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).3.3">Tosephta 3:3</a>. The owner of the attacker also becomes part owner of the carcass.</i>; but Rebbi Simeon says he only acquired the (dead)<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">This certainly should read: the living. R. Simeon only discusses the case that the carcass be without value; this skirts the issue of ownership of an injured animal.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.10.4.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 10:4</a>. The text and its relation to the corresponding one in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 10:3:2-4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.10.3.2-4.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 10:4</a> is explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.5.2">Notes 73–75</a>.</i> There, we have stated: “Similarly, if three who invested together lost or gained they would split in this manner.” Rebbi Abun said, the statement looks reasonable if they bought a precious stone because he can say to him, without my ten denars you could not have bought anything. But anything that usually is split {smaller units that can be bought with less capital} one adds together and splits {proportionally to the capital invested}. Rebbi Eleazar says, even things that usually are split [are divided evenly], because he can say to him, you have a lot of merchandise and you have difficulty selling it. I have little merchandise and turn it over rapidly and make as much as you do. So far if his merchandise was here. What if his merchandise was in Rome? He can say to him, by the time you went to Rome, I turn mine over rapidly here and make as much as you do.
It was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Ketubot (Lieberman) 10:4" href="/Tosefta_Ketubot_(Lieberman).10.4">Tosephta <i>Ketubot</i> 10:4</a>.</i> Three who invested together and suffered theft, absorb [the loss] in the same way<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The distribution algorithm described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.10.4.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 10:4 (Note 62</a>).</i>. But was it not stated: If those [precious] stones were stolen, half for each one<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Each of the investors absorbs the same amount of loss.</i>. Rebbi Shammai said, tetradrachmas are large<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should interchange the positions of “large” and “easy”. Money can be divided easily and exactly; precious stones are a one or nothing affair as noted in the preceding paragraph.</i> and it is possible to subdivide exactly but stones are easy<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should interchange the positions of “large” and “easy”. Money can be divided easily and exactly; precious stones are a one or nothing affair as noted in the preceding paragraph.</i> and it is impossible to know which part in it belongs to whom. Because of the doubt, each one takes proportional to his investment. What did you see to say that we deal with theft, maybe one deals only with what is left<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Which is money and therefore easily distributed.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, even if you say that we deal with theft, would not equity be hurt? Think of it, if one invested 50 and the other 150. If 50 was stolen, would not the one with 50 not lose anything<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">This is difficult to understand. There are 150 left and the sum total of the claims is 200. By the method of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 10:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.10.4.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 10:4</a>, the first 50 are claimed by both investors, the remaining 100 only by the second. Therefore, the first gets 25, the second 125; each one loses 25. If the loss were distributed proportional to the investment, the first investor would take 37.5, the second 113.5. It is a matter of opinion which method be more equitable.</i>?
<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 10:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.10.4.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 10:4, Notes 76–79</a>.</i> There, we have stated: “If [the ox] gored an ox worth 200, the last one takes 100, the one before him 50, and the two first ones each a gold denar.” Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Ze‘ira: The same holds for earnings. Rebbi Yose said, the statement of Rebbi Ze‘ira disagrees with Rebbi Eleazar. Rebbi Mani said, I asked this before Rebbi Yudan. He said to me: does Rebbi Eleazar not agree if they contracted between themselves that each can take according to his contribution? Oxen are as if contracted. He turned around and said this before Rebbi Yose, who answered him, they disagree explicitly: Rebbi Eleazar said, if nothing was said, they split evenly; Rebbi Ze‘ira said, if nothing was said, each takes according to his contribution.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A bull which is notorious for its kind but not notorious for other species, notorious for humans but not notorious for animals, notorious for small ones but not notorious for large ones, pays full damages for what it is notorious for, but half damages for what it is not notorious for. They asked before Rebbi Jehudah: If it was notorious on the Sabbath but not on weekdays? He answered, it pays full damages on Sabbaths but half damages on weekdays. When does it revert to be tame? If it changed its ways for three Sabbath days<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. Jehuda’s opinion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 2:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.2.6.1">Mishnah 2:6</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A bull which is notorious for its kind,” etc. After it killed one human, is it not notorious<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Any animal which killed “a man or a woman” has to be killed (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex</i>. 21:28</a>). How can such an animal ever become notorious after three times if it is killed after the first time?</i>? Rav said, if it killed three Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Who are not subsumed under the appellation “man or woman”. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 41a" href="/Bava_Kamma.41a">Babli 41a</a>, in the name of Rav Sheshet.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, if it pursued three times and it was estimated that every pursuit was for goring<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For endangering humans, an animal may become notorious for trying three times.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.2">Halakhah 2:6, Notes 90–96</a>.</i> If it was let out the first day and gored bulls, the second it gored dogs, the third it gored pigs: would it be declared a notorious bull for three different kinds on three days? If it was let out the first day and gored, the second day it was not let out, the third day it was let out and gored, we come to the disagreement between Rav Ada bar Aḥawa and Rav Huna, who disagreed: A menstruating woman checked herself the first day and found herself impure. The second day she did not check. On the third day she checked and found herself impure. Rav Ada bar Aḥawa said in the name of Rav: she certainly is <i>niddah.</i> Rav Huna said in the name of Rav: it is doubtful whether she is <i>niddah.</i> Rav Huna said, I was there at the beginning, I was there at the end, and I was there in the middle when Rav was asked, and he said, it is doubtful. Rav was asked and he said, it is certain; then he reversed himself and said, it is doubtful. Rav Ada bar Aḥawa was only there when he said, it is certain.
Rav Jeremiah said in the name of Rav: Practice follows Rebbi Meïr for the tame animal and Rebbi Jehudah for testimony.
We wanted to ask: what is the reason of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">He accepts that a bull may be notorious only for the Sabbath. Is it reasonable to expect animals to change their behavior according to a human calendar?</i>? Because it sees us dressed<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">E reads מניין נקיין, a misspelling for מָנִין נְקִיִין “clean garments”. Quoted in Hebrew translation by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Kamma 37a:5:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Kamma.37a.5.1">Tosaphot 37a <i>s.v.</i> הרי</a>; explaining that the bull does not recognize its master and attendants wearing Sabbath clothing.</i> cleanly, it changes its behavior.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The bull of an Israel which gored a bull of Temple property or the bull of Temple property which gored a bull of an Israel are not liable since it was said “his neighbor’s bull”<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35"><i>Ex.</i> 21:35</a>.</i>, not a bull of Temple property.<br>The bull of an Israel which gored a bull of a non-Jew is not liable; the bull of a non-Jew which gored a bull of an Israel pays full damages, whether tame or notorious.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The bull of an Israel which gored a bull of a non-Jew,” etc. Rav said, “He saw and permitted Gentiles<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Habakkuk 3:6" href="/Habakkuk.3.6"><i>Hab.</i> 3:6</a>. The usual translation is: “He saw and made peoples jump” (by an earthquake).</i>,” He permitted the Gentiles’ money<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 38a" href="/Bava_Kamma.38a">Babli, 38a</a>, a statement of R. Abbahu, applies to those Gentiles who violate the Noahide commandments. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 13:2" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.13.2"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 13(2</a>); <i>Tanḥuma Buber Šemini</i> 10; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Devarim Rabbah 1" href="/Devarim_Rabbah.1"><i>Deut. rabba Eqeb</i> 1</a> (some mss.).</i>. Ḥizqiah said, “He appeared from Mont Paran<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 33:2" href="/Deuteronomy.33.2"><i>Deut.</i> 33.2</a>. The list of peoples living around Sinai is traditionally interpreted as a list of those to whom the Torah was offered first but who declined, and only Israel did accept (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 2b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.2b">Babli <i>Avodah zarah 2b</a>; Sifry Deut.</i>343; <i>Pesiqta dR. Cahana Berakhah,</i> ed. Buber 199b; <i>Tanḥuma Berakhah</i> 4 (Buber 3); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:2" href="/Deuteronomy.32.2"><i>Pseudo-Jonathan Deut.</i> 32:2</a>); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:2" href="/Deuteronomy.32.2"><i>Midrash Tannaïm Deut.</i> 32:2</a>.</i>,” He appeared in front of the Gentiles; Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, He made them poor<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This statement possibly is fragmentary; cf. the formulations in the sources indicated in the preceding two Notes.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said, following their laws<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">It is stressed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:1" href="/Exodus.21.1"><i>Ex.</i> 21:1</a> that the laws of that Chapter is <i>before them;</i> it applies only to intra-Israelite lawsuits. An application to suits involving Gentiles is illegitimate (<i>Sifry Deut.</i> 16; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 1). Gentile law does not recognize payment for half the damages.</i>. Rebbi La said, this was not said referring to this topic, but to what Rebbi Ḥiyya stated<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 4:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).4.2">Tosephta 4:2</a>.</i>: If a Gentile’s bull gored another Gentile’s bull, even though they accepted to be judged by the laws of Israel, he is liable for full damages whether tame or notorious. On that it was said that Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, following their laws.
<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 38a" href="/Bava_Kamma.38a">Babli 38a</a>; <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 344; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:2" href="/Deuteronomy.32.2"><i>Midrash Tannaïm</i>(<i>Midrash Haggadol Deut.</i> 32:2</a>).</i> It happened that the [Roman] government sent two officials to study Torah with Rabban Gamliel. They learned from him Bible, Mishnah, Talmud, practice, and homilies. At the end, they told him: All of your teachings are beautiful and commendable except two things which you say: “A Jewish woman shall not act as midwife to a Gentile, but a Gentile woman may act as midwife for a Jewish woman; a Jewish woman shall not nurse the child of a Gentile, but a Gentile woman may nurse for a Jewish woman with her consent; what was robbed from a Jew is forbidden but from a Gentile it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, a Jew could be a fence for goods stolen from a Gentile. In <i>Midrash Haggadol:</i> “what was robbed from a Jew is forbidden but <i>what was lost by</i> a Gentile is permitted.” The entire sentence is missing in the Babli.</i>.” At that moment did Rabban Gamliel decide that what was robbed from a Gentile be forbidden because of desecration of the Name. “The bull of an Israel which gored a bull of a non-Jew is not liable,” etc. But we shall not inform the government of this. Nevertheless, by the time they reached the Tyrian ladder they had forgotten everything.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a bull of a sane person gored the bull of a deaf-mute, insane, or underaged person, he is liable. If a bull of a deaf-mute, insane, or underaged person gored the bull of a sane person, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since these persons cannot appear in court, they cannot be sued.</i>. If a bull of a deaf-mute, insane, or underaged person gored, one<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah in the Babli: “The court”.</i> appoints a guardian for them and receives testimony about them<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">About the animal, to have it declared notorious. Once the animal is declared notorious, the guardian will have to pay damages from the estate of his wards.</i> in the presence of the guardian<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since testimony in general may not be taken in the absence of the parties. In the case of the goring bull, it is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:29" href="/Exodus.21.29"><i>Ex.</i> 21:29</a> that testimony must be “in presence of the owner’ (Midrash Haggadol <i>ad loc</i>.).</i>. If the deaf-mute became hearing, the insane normal, the underaged of age, it returns to be tame, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that “tame” or “notorious” is not a property of the animal but a qualification of the legal status of its owner. If the owner became a person in law, his status would have to be determined anew.</i>; Rebbi Yose said, it remains in its prior state. A bull of the stadion<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Trained for bullfights.</i> is not to be killed since it is said: “if it gores,” not that others train it to gore.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a bull of a sane person,” etc. So is the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah should not be read as freeing only the underaged deaf-dumb insane person from liability.</i>: The bull of a deaf-mute, the bull of an insane, the bull of an underaged.
Did we not state: “If a bull of a deaf-mute, insane, or underaged person gored, one appoints a guardian for them and receives testimony about them in the presence of the guardian”<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah seems self-contradictory. Either incompetent persons are not liable or they have to be represented by guardians whose duty it will be to indemnify the victims of the animals of the incompetent. The question is also asked in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 39a" href="/Bava_Kamma.39a">Babli, 39a</a>. It is answered there but here left unanswered. One may not assume that the treatment of the Babli is valid for the Yerushalmi. It is more likely that the question is not answered because the Mishnah has a straightforward interpretation: The incompetent are not liable, but it is the duty of the court to intervene and appoint guardians responsible for future control of the dangerous animals (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:36" href="/Exodus.21.36"><i>Midrash Haggadol Ex.</i> 21:36</a>).</i>? So far if testimony was taken in the presence of the owner and he delivered to the guardian. If testimony was taken in the presence of the guardian and he delivered to the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This text seems to be devoid of sense. One may adopt the emendation of <i>Pene Moshe</i> and switch the objects: “So far if testimony was taken in the presence of the <i>guardian</i> and he delivered to the <i>owner.</i> If testimony was taken in the presence of the <i>owner</i> and he delivered to the <i>guardian</i>?” This text refers to the disagreement between R. Meïr and R. Yose. For the latter, a notorious beast always remains notorious. For R. Meïr the designation of “notorious” lapses if the animal is handed over to the grown-up owner by the guardian. What would be his opinion if an owner of a notorious beast developed a mental illness and his estate were handed to a guardian by the court? Does he also hold that the designation of “notorious” lapses in this case?</i>? Let us hear from the following: <sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 40a" href="/Bava_Kamma.40a">Babli 40a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).5.4">Tosephta 5:4</a>.</i>“If somebody borrowed it with the understanding that it was tame but it turned out to be notorious, the owner pays half of the damages and the borrower pays half of the damages<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The owner has to pay full damages for the notorious animal. Since he failed to inform the borrower, he has regress on him only for half the damages since the borrower is responsible to watch the animal to make sure it causes no damage as “tame”. (For the different versions of the Tosephta, cf. S. Lieberman in <i>Tosefta kiFshutah.</i>)</i>.” Rebbi Eleazar said, this is Rebbi Yose’s, since Rebbi Yose said, it remains in its prior state. But if this is Rebbi Yose’s, he<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since for R. Yose the status of the animal does not change with a change of control, the borrower should be liable for the entire damage.</i> should pay in full. If he borrowed it with the understanding that it was tame<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Same explanation given in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 40a" href="/Bava_Kamma.40a">Babli, 40a</a>.</i>. But if he borrowed it with the understanding that it was tame, he should not have to pay anything. If he knew that it was apt to gore<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">But the borrower was not informed that the animal had been declared notorious by action of the court.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 5:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).5.4">Tosephta 5:4</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 44b-45a" href="/Bava_Kamma.44b-45a">Babli 44b/45a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 98b" href="/Bava_Kamma.98b">98b</a>.</i>: “If it killed while at the borrower’s, who handed it back to the owners, before judgment was rendered he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Since he handed back a bull, he does not have to replace it even though after judgment the bull will not be worth anything.</i>, after judgment was rendered<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">That the bull has to be killed and its carcass forbidden for usufruct (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>).</i> he is liable. Rebbi Jacob said, even after judgment was rendered but before it was stoned, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Since he returned the bull; its changed legal status is not apparent.</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph has an exact correspondence in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:4:2-5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.4.2-5.4"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:4</a> (47a l. 15), Notes 111–115. The next paragraph is a reformulation of the topic discussed there, Notes 100–101, 116–122.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, as a matter of principle one<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The court, if the father had failed to appoint a guardian before his death.</i> does not appoint guardians for orphans to their detriment, but only to their profit; but if they are detrimental, they are detrimental. Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina said, neither as a matter of principle nor as a reaction, neither for profit nor for detriment. A Mishnah disagrees with Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: “One appoints a guardian for them” There is a difference about an ox, lest it continue to cause damage.
If a bull of orphans and guardians went out and did damage, what is the rule? Rebbi Joḥanan said, [the damage is paid] from the orphan’s property. For if you say from the guardians’, nobody ever will agree to become a guardian. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, from the guardians’. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina follows Abba Shaul and Rebbi Joḥanan the rabbis, as it was stated there: “A guardian appointed by the orphans’ father shall be made to swear; if he was appointed by the court he shall not be made to swear,” for the court will check him out. “Abba Shaul says, it is the other way around,” a guardian appointed by the orphans’ father shall not be made to swear; it would be as if he would take a pledge from him. If he was appointed by the court he shall be made to swear, since a person will do much to be called trustworthy.
Abba bar Rab Huna said: This<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The statement which excepts the bull trained to fight from retribution; the Mishnah puts all the blame on its trainers.</i> implies that somebody who hit another person’s animal which as a consequence went and did damage is responsible for the damage caused<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Even though in general one does not allow suits for indirectly caused damages.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A bull which gored a human who then died, if it is notorious [the owner] pays weregilt<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:30" href="/Exodus.21.30"><i>Ex.</i> 21:30</a>.</i>, if tame he is not liable for weregilt; in both cases they have to be killed<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:29" href="/Exodus.21.29">29</a>.</i>. The same holds for a son or a daughter<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">There is no difference whether the victim was adult or underage. The statement is only necessary since the verse, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:31" href="/Exodus.21.31"><i>Ex.</i> 21:31</a>, stresses that the same rules apply to underage as to adult victims.</i>. It it gored a male or female slave, [the owner] pays 30 tetradrachmas<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:32" href="/Exodus.21.32"><i>Ex.</i> 21:32</a>.</i>, whether he was worth a mina or was worth only a gold<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This reading is also found in some Babli mss., Alfasi, and the Naples print of the Mishnah. In other texts: “one (silver) denar”.</i> denar.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A bull which gored a human,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 41b" href="/Bava_Kamma.41b">Babli 41b</a>; cf. <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 10 (Horovitz-Rabin p. 283); <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i>21:28, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Midrash Tannaïm</i>(<i>Midrash Haggadol Ex.</i> 21:28</a>, ed. Margaliut p. 484.)</i>: “But the owner of the bull is free<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>.</i>,” free from half the weregilt, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Aqiba said to him, Rabbi, is he not destined for the severity of the stoning place<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">As the formulation of the Babli makes explicit, since any damages caused by a tame animal are paid from its body and a bull which killed a human is stoned and its carcass forbidden for usufruct, there is nothing from which either damages or fine might be paid.</i>? He answered him, I said this only for one which intended to kill an animal but killed a human<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The bull was attacking another animal when a human intervened and was killed. In that case, the bull is not condemned to be stoned and is not forbidden for usufruct.</i>, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A list of similar situations as given later in the paragraph.</i> Before he objects to Rebbi Eliezer, should he not object to himself<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Below, R. Aqiba is quoted as holding that the verse frees the owner of a tame bull from paying for the death of a slave. He should have told himself that his interpretation is impossible.</i>? Rebbi Miasha said, explain it if he transgressed and slaughtered it<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.8.1">Mishnah 9</a>, the bull’s meat becomes forbidden only when it is stoned. If the owners slaughter it immediately after the attack, before the court had time to intervene, the meat is valuable and its proceeds are available to cover damages.</i>. But some want to say, from what<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Reading מִן “from” instead of מאן “who” (I. Lewy).</i> we learn from Rebbi Eliezer who said, I said this only for one which intended to kill an animal but killed a human, a Gentile but killed an Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">It is stressed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:1" href="/Exodus.21.1"><i>Ex.</i> 21:1</a> that the laws of that Chapter is <i>before them;</i> it applies only to intra-Israelite lawsuits. An application to suits involving Gentiles is illegitimate (<i>Sifry Deut.</i> 16; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 1). Gentile law does not recognize payment for half the damages.</i>, still birth but killed a viable [child]<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Killing a fetus or a newborn which is not viable is forbidden but not prosecutable as murder; the same holds for killing a <i>ṭerepha,</i> a terminally ill person.</i>, he is not liable. How does Rebbi Aqiba explain “the owner of the bull is free”<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>.</i>’<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Below, R. Aqiba is quoted as holding that the verse frees the owner of a tame bull from paying for the death of a slave. He should have told himself that his interpretation is impossible.</i>? Free from paying for a slave. Does not Rebbi Eliezer agree that he is free from paying for a slave? “If weregilt is imposed on him,<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:30" href="/Exodus.21.30"><i>Ex.</i> 21:30</a>.</i>” the verse speaks of the notorious<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the notion of weregilt is not applicable to the tame bull; the exemption needs no verse.</i>. Does not Rebbi Aqiba agree that he is free from half the weregilt? It is said here, “the bull shall be stoned<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:29" href="/Exodus.21.29"><i>Ex.</i> 21:29</a>. This verse imposes weregilt for the killing of a free person by a notorious bull.</i>,” and it says there, “the bull shall be stoned.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:32" href="/Exodus.21.32"><i>Ex.</i> 21:32</a>. This verse imposes a fine for the killing of a slave.</i>” Since about “the bull” mentioned there, the verse speaks of the notorious, so also about “the bull shall be stoned” mentioned here, the verse speaks of the notorious<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, no fine is imposed for the killing of a slave by a tame bull.</i>. But is it not written: “The bull by stoning shall be stoned<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>.</i>”? It is better that “the bull shall be stoned” shall be interpreted following “the bull shall be stoned” rather than that “the bull shall be stoned” should be interpreted following “the bull by stoning shall be stoned”<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, no fine is imposed for the killing of a slave by a tame bull.</i>.
Rebbi Yasa said, I heard something<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>baraita.</i></i> from Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac in this matter, but I do not remember what it was. Rebbi Ze‘ira told him, maybe it was the following: At the start the discussion refers to the tame animal. It added weregilt for the notorious. Then it added damages for both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a> discusses the tame bull which kills a human. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:29" href="/Exodus.21.29">Verses 29–32</a> deal with the notorious bull which is killing. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:35" href="/Exodus.21.35">Verses 35–36</a> then deal with both kinds of bulls as damaging goods.</i>. I could think that as it added damages, it added the payment for the slave<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Since the payment for killed slaves is not treated as weregilt but as payment of damages to the owner for the loss of his slave, might it not be trated as part of the rules for payment of damages?</i>; the verse says “but the owner of the bull is free<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>.</i>;” free from paying for a slave. Rebbi La said<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">A different but parallel argument is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 42b" href="/Bava_Kamma.42b">Babli, 42b</a>.</i>, everywhere you are more restrictive for a slave than anything else since even if he is scabbed one pays thirty tetradrachmas; I would have said also (for his father and his mother)<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has ro read: “a male or female slave”.</i> the same; the verse says “but the owner of the bull is free<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>.</i>;” free from paying for fetuses<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">There is an obvious lacuna here which is filled by E: “But the owner of the bull is free;” <i>free from paying for a slave. It was stated in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah: “but the owner of the bull is free,”</i> free from paying for fetuses.<br>The scribe’s error was induced by the repetition of the same text. For the statement of R. Eleazar ben Azariah, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.5.2">Note 71</a>.<br>The payment for fetuses refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:22" href="/Exodus.21.22"><i>Ex.</i> 21:22</a>: If quarelling people unintentionally hit a pregnant woman who then has a miscarriage, they have to indemnify her husband. The argument in the text presupposes the statement later in the text: “ ‘people’ but not bulls.” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:22" href="/Exodus.21.22"><i>Midrash Haggadol</i> to <i>Ex.</i> 21:22</a>) This exempts the owner of a notorious bull from payment if it causes a miscarriage. Therefore, there is no reason to think that the owner of a tame bull should pay. Why should R. Eleazar b. Azariah have to mention it?</i>. Everywhere you are more restrictive for a notorious than for a tame one, would you be more restrictive here for a tame one than for a notorious? But it must be so: Men who act unintentionally are liable; bulls who act unintentionally are not liable. Does this mean that if they acted intentionally, they were liable? The verse says, “but the owner of the bull is free<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>.</i>;” free from paying for a slave<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to read here: “for fetuses”.</i>. Another Tanna stated: “But the owner of the bull is free<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>.</i>;” free from paying for a slave<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to read here: “for fetuses”.</i>. But is it not written: “If people quarrel<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:22" href="/Exodus.21.22"><i>Ex.</i> 21:22</a>.</i>”, people but not bulls. Rebbi Ḥaggai said, so is the <i>baraita</i>: Men who act unintentionally are liable; bulls who act unintentionally are not liable. Does this mean that if they acted intentionally, they were liable? The verse says, “but the owner of the bull is free<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a>.</i>;” free from paying for a slave<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to read here: “for fetuses”.</i>. But is it not written: “When they quarrel<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:22" href="/Exodus.21.22"><i>Ex.</i> 21:22</a>.</i>,” “when they brawl<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:18" href="/Exodus.21.18"><i>Ex.</i> 21:18</a>, specifying the payments due for intentionally inflicted injuries.</i>”. Is not quarrel the same as brawl<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">There is missing the corresponding rhetorical question “and fight the same as quarrel”? It is in E. The arguments are used to impose the payments mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:18" href="/Exodus.21.18"><i>Ex.</i> 21:18</a> for intentional injuries on the unintentional injuries mentioned in 21:22, and vice-versa (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 9:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.9.5.3"><i>Nazir</i> 9:5, Notes 183–184</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 9:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.9.3.2-3"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 9:3</a>, 27a l. 58; <i>Midrash Haggadol</i>21:22).</i>? Since there the act was intentional, so here the act was intentional; or since here the act was unintentional, so here the act was unintentional. What about this<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Which of the two contradictory arguments is to be accepted? Neither.</i>? As Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, at the start it treats the tame animal, etc.
From where damages for a slave<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">If a slave was injured by an animal but was not killed. May his owner sue the animal’s owner for damages.</i>? The verse says, “or it gores a son, or it gores a daughter, …, and if a slave<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:31" href="/Exodus.21.31">Verses <i>Ex</i>. 21:31</a>,32 treat attacks on minors and on slaves in parallel. It is implied that any payments due for an injured child are due for a slave.</i>, etc.” Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba and Rebbi Samuel bar Isaac. One said, the addition may not be more than the main thing<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Since for a slave killed the payment is 30 tetradrachmas, payments for an injured slave can be at most 30 tetradrachmas. This opinion is not found in the Babli.</i>. But the other said, he has to pay the entire damages.<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">All payments due for a free person (medical costs, lost wages, payment for pain and suffering) are due for a slave without limitation. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 43b" href="/Bava_Kamma.43b">Babli, 43b</a>, attributes this opinion to R. Samuel b. R. Isaac.</i>

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A bull which was rubbing itself against a wall which then fell on a human, [or which] intended to kill an animal but killed a human<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The bull was attacking another animal when a human intervened and was killed. In that case, the bull is not condemned to be stoned and is not forbidden for usufruct.</i>, a Gentile but killed a Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">It is stressed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:1" href="/Exodus.21.1"><i>Ex.</i> 21:1</a> that the laws of that Chapter is <i>before them;</i> it applies only to intra-Israelite lawsuits. An application to suits involving Gentiles is illegitimate (<i>Sifry Deut.</i> 16; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 1). Gentile law does not recognize payment for half the damages.</i>, stillbirth but killed a viable child<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Killing a fetus or a newborn which is not viable is forbidden but not prosecutable as murder; the same holds for killing a <i>ṭerepha,</i> a terminally ill person.</i>, is free from prosecution.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A bull which was rubbing itself against a wall,” etc. “A man who kills any human soul,<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:17" href="/Leviticus.21.17"><i>Lev.</i> 21:17</a>.</i>” to include a hit which is potentially deadly. Some Tannaïm state, even if it is not deadly<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 78a" href="/Sanhedrin.78a">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 78a</a>, explains that the verse can be read either as “a man who kills a human totally” or as “a man who kills anything of a man.” In the first version, if one attacker wounds a person and a second one finishes him off, the second attacker is the murderer even if the wounds inflicted by the first are potentially lethal. In the second version, the heirs of a person killed by a gang have indemnity claims against all gang members taking part in the attack even if none of them can be prosecuted for first degree murder.</i>. Rebbi La said, even if it is deadly but a third person came and killed, the killer is guilty<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">A tannaïtic statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 20 1" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_20.1"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 20(1</a>).</i>. “And it killed a man or a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:29" href="/Exodus.21.29"><i>Ex.</i> 21:29</a>.</i>,” just as a man lets his sons inherit his injury claims, so a woman lets her sons inherit her injury claims. But does not a man inherit from his wife<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">How can her children (or in the absence of children, her paternal relatives) inherit anything from her if she is survived by her husband?</i>? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, so did Rebbi Hoshaiah, the father of the Mishnah, explain: One teaches here about weregilt which is due after death<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">(<i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon b. Ioḥai</i>21:29, p. 181). The biblical right of inheritance by the husband, based on <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:11" href="/Numbers.27.11"><i>Num.</i> 27:11</a>, excludes future claims (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 42b" href="/Bava_Kamma.42b">Babli 42b</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 8:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.8.5.4"><i>Bava batra</i> 8:6 Note 129</a>). Since there is no weregilt for a living person, the husband cannot inherit.</i>.
If somebody sells a bull to another person and it turns out to be goring. Rav said, it is an acquisition in error, but Samuel says, he can tell him: I sold it to you to be slaughtered<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rav, the buyer can force the annulment of the sale and receive his money back. According to Samuel, since a bull can be sold either for meat or for work, it is up to the buyer to specify for which use he is buying since for livestock there do not exist generally valid criteria of quality. (<i>Ševi‘it</i> 5:8, Notes 70,71; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 46a" href="/Bava_Kamma.46a">Babli 46a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 92a" href="/Bava_Batra.92a"><i>Bava batra</i> 92a</a>).</i>. “He shall give weregilt for his person.<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex..</i> 21:30. It is not clear to whom the verse refers in mentioning “his”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:10:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.10.2"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:10, Note 151</a> and all sources quoted there, in particular <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 40a" href="/Bava_Kamma.40a">Babli 40a</a>.</i>” Some Tannaїm state: The attacker’s person. Some Tannaїm state: The person suffering the damage. Following him who said, the person suffering the damage, [assume] the first one injured him fatally and then the second came and kept him in confusion<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The bull of owner A injured a person who was prevented by the bull of owner B to seek immediate medical attention; the person then died.</i>. If you say that damages are fully required<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Damages are due even though weregilt was paid.</i>, the first one pays damages and the second pays the weregilt. If you say that damages are not fully required<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">There is no claim for damages if there is one for weregilt.</i>, the first one pays weregilt but the second is not liable. Following him who said, the attacker’s person, if you say that damages are fully required, the first one pays weregilt and the second is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Since the action of B’s bull was strictly defensive, he is not an attacker.</i>. If you say that damages are not fully required, neither one is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">If neither of them can be held criminally liable, neither of them can be held financially liable.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A bull belonging to a woman, a bull belonging to orphans<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Minors for whom no guardian was appointed.</i>, a bull under a guardian<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Property of orphans.</i>, a wild bull, a bull of Temple property, a bull belonging to a proselyte who died without heirs, these are subject to the death penalty<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If they killed a person.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says that a wild bull, a bull of Temple property, a bull belonging to a proselyte who died without heirs<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Whose estate is ownerless; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.9"><i>Qiddushin</i> 1:1, Note 30</a>.</i>, are not subject to the death penalty since they have no owners.
