<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A bull belonging to a woman, a bull belonging to orphans,” etc. What is Rebbi Jehudah’s reason? “It was testified to before its owners and they did not watch it;<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:29" href="/Exodus.21.29"><i>Ex.</i> 21:29</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 4:6" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).4.6">Tosephta 4:6</a>. The verse speaks of the notorious bull; one seems to understand that no ownerless bull can be brought to court.</i>” those have no owners who could be responsible for damages. Rebbi Hoshaia stated: In matters of damages<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Damage claims are possible only against humans. One must refer to a bull who did damage before its owner dedicated it to the Temple, or was property of a proselyte while the latter was still living.</i>, Rebbi Meïr declares liable but Rebbi Jehudah declares not liable. What about weregilt? Rebbi Pedat in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Everybody agrees that he is liable for weregilt. But some say, Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai: Just as they disagree for damages so they disagree for weregilt<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">The language of the Tosephta seems to support R. Joḥanan. The formulation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 44b" href="/Bava_Kamma.44b">Babli, 44b</a> (in the name of Rav Huna and a <i>baraita</i>) is inconclusive. (The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 44a" href="/Bava_Kamma.44a">Babli, 44a</a>, reports a similar disagreement, between Rav and Samuel, referring to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.6.1">Mishnah 7</a>.)</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah asked before Rebbi Ze‘ira: How does one actually act? He said to him, following Rebbi Hoshaia that in matters of damages, Rebbi Meïr declares liable but Rebbi Jehudah declares not liable; therefore, in matters of weregilt everybody agrees that he is liable.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one dedicates a bull which is led out to be stoned, it is not dedicated, and if it was slaughtered, its meat is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:28" href="/Exodus.21.28"><i>Ex.</i> 21:28</a> reads in part: “The bull shall be stoned; its meat shall not be eaten.” If the bull is killed by stoning, it is not ritually slaughtered and automatically its meat is forbidden as carcass meat. The specific mention of the prohibition of the meat is interpreted to mean that it becomes forbidden the moment sentence is pronounced. If the owner wants to save something of the value of the bull, he has to slaughter it immediately after it killed [<i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> 10; <i>dR. Simeon ben Ioḥai</i> 21:28, p. 178; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.2-16">Yerushalmi <i>Pesaḥim</i> 2:1</a> (28c l. 34), <i>‘Orlah</i> 3:1 Note 26; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 22b" href="/Pesachim.22b">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i>22b</a>].</i>. If its owner dedicated it before sentence was pronounced, it is dedicated, and if it was slaughtered, its meat is permitted.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A bull which is led out to be stoned,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 6:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.6.2.2"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 6:2</a>(23b l.25) an exact copy, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.7.2">10:8</a>(29d l.14) a reformulation.</i>: If a bull was led out to be stoned when its witnesses were found to be false, Rebbi Joḥanan said, the first to come acquires it<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since the court declares the bull to be forbidden for usufruct, it cannot remain the property of its owner. When the prohibition of usufruct is lifted because the sentence was declared false, the bull is ownerless. If a third party grabs the bull before the owner can take it back, that third party now is the legal owner. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Keritot 24a" href="/Keritot.24a">Babli, <i>Keritut</i> 24a</a>, holds that R. Joḥanan will agree that the bull never was ownerless if the judgment was overturned on the initiative or appeal by the owner.</i>; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it was false despair<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that ownership never was annulled, only that the owner gave up hope of ever having use of the bull again. The bull remains the property of its owner who may use it once the prohibition of usufruct is lifted.</i>. Similarly, if a slave was led out to be killed when his witnesses were found to be false, Rebbi Joḥanan said, he acquired himself<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">He is free and needs no bill of manumission.</i>; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it was false despair.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If it was handed over to an unpaid trustee, or a borrower, or a paid trustee, or a lessee, they entered instead of the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">The four cases of temporary guardians of other people’s property are defined in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 6:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.6.6.1">Mishnah <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 6:8</a>. For damages caused while the animal was in the temporary control of another person, the person in control may be responsible in place of the owner. This limited liability is detailed in the second part of the Mishnah.</i>; a notorious animal pays full damages, a tame one half damages. If he tied it down with a rope and locked it in as he should, if then it broke out and caused damage he is liable whether tame or notorious<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">In the Halakhah, the Babli, <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon b. Ioḥai,</i> and most independent Mishnah texts, this is identified as R. Meïr’s opinion. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).5.7">Tosephta 5:7</a> and <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> 10, R. Meïr declares the owner of the tame bull not liable in this case, only the owner of a notorious bull. In all of these sources there is a third opinion, by R. Eliezer ben Jacob, that neither is liable.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, for the tame he is liable, for the notorious he is not liable since it was said: “if its owner would not guard it<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex..</i> 21:36. There is no corresponding verse regarding a tame bull.</i>,” but it was guarded. Rebbi Eliezer says, this one can be guarded only by the knife<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">Once a bull has been declared notorious, it has to be slaughtered immediately.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If it was handed over to an unpaid trustee,” etc. Rebbi Eleazar said, the words of Rebbi Meïr that watching for damages is on the level of an unpaid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The unpaid trustee has to pay only if anything happens because of his negligence. Therefore, it seems that R. Eleazar refers to R. Meïr’s position as articulated in Tosephta and <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael,</i> Note 127.</i>; Rebbi Jehuda said that watching for damages is on the level of a paid trustee<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">The paid trustee has to pay for any damage which it was in his power to prevent. R. Jehudah frees the person guarding a notorious bull from being liable only because of an exemption granted by the verse.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, the words of Rebbi Meïr: If one handed over a bull<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">In E and Rashba: “a tame bull”. This seems to be required by the next paragraph.</i> to an unpaid trustee, if it went off and did damage, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">The unpaid trustee has to pay only if anything happens because of his negligence. Therefore, it seems that R. Eleazar refers to R. Meïr’s position as articulated in Tosephta and <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael,</i> Note 127.</i>; if it went out and became torn<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">The bull is attacked and severely damaged by another.</i>, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">In E and Rashba: “is liable”. In Rashba’s explanation, the person who accepts the duty to watch over an animal becomes liable if the animal is damaged.<br>The text of L seems to be preferable since the unpaid trustee is freed from liability if he can swear that the damage was not caused by his negligence.</i>. To a paid trustee, if it went off and did damage, he is liable; if it went out and became torn, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that one has to read: “is liable”. The clause is missing in Rashba’s quote.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, the words of Rebbi Jehudah: If one handed over a notorious bull to an unpaid trustee, if it went off and did damage, he is liable; if it went out and became torn, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since even the paid shepherd does not have to pay for the torn, by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:12" href="/Exodus.22.12"><i>Ex.</i> 22:12</a>.</i>. To a paid trustee, if it went off and did damage, he is liable; if it went out and became torn, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Even though <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:12" href="/Exodus.22.12"><i>Ex.</i> 22:12</a> frees the paid trustee from paying for a torn animal, here he should have prevented the bull from getting out in the first place.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, was it not watched? But the Torah declared him liable. Rebbi Eleazar said, for every guarding required by the Torah, even if it was done by enclosing in an iron wall, one only qualifies by personal presence<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Mechanical devices cannot replace personal supervision. Even the unpaid trustee will be liable if he relies on a mechanical device instead of his personal supervision.</i>. Therefore one determines whether he is qualified for watching; then he is not liable, otherwise he is liable.

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A bull gored a cow and her fetus<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A dead fetus after a miscarriage.</i> was found beside her. If it is not known whether she gave birth before she was gored<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, the owner of the bull would not have to pay for the miscarriage.</i> or gave birth after she was gored, he pays half the damages for the cow<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The normal amount for damage caused by a tame bull.</i> and a quarter for the fetus<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since the owner of the cow cannot prove that the bull caused the miscarriage but neither can the owner of the bull prove that his animal did not cause it, and the bull’s attack on the cow is proved by two witnesses, the claim of damages for the fetus is “money in doubt” which is split evenly, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.1.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:1, Note 9</a>.</i>.<br>And similarly, if a cow gored an ox and her calf is found next to her, but it is not known whether she gave birth before or after she gored<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">If at the moment of her goring the calf was part of her body, it is available to be sold to cover the damages. But if it was born before the goring, it is not available to the owner of the bull. Therefore, the claim of the bull’s owner on the calf cannot exceed a quarter of its value.</i>, he pays half the damage from the cow and a quarter from the calf.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A bull gored a cow”, etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This is a slight reformulation of a passage in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.1.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:1, Notes 9–11</a>.</i> Do all cows have miscarriages? Should we follow the majority of cases? This implies that in criminal cases and in money matters one does not apply a majority argument. Rebbi Yose said, in another case they applied a majority argument in criminal cases and in money matters, as Rebbi Aḥa stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 3:6" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).3.6">Tosephta 3:6</a>.</i>“If a camel was in heat among camels and one finds one of them dead, I am saying that certainly the one in heat killed it.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In mss. and <i>editio princeps,</i> this paragraph is part of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.1.2">Halakhah 1</a>. But it clearly refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:1:1-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.1.1-3">Mishnah 2</a>.</i> And Rebbi Yannai said, so is the Mishnah: He pays half of the damage from the cow and the owner of the calves returns a quarter to the owner of the cow<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">He refers the Mishnah to the case where the rancher sold the expected calf of his pregnant cow to a third party. The owner of the bull has to be indemnified by the cow’s owner alone, but the latter has regress on the calf’s owner up to a quarter of its value.<br>The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 46b" href="/Bava_Kamma.46b">Babli, 46b</a>, has another approach: If the cow is available, he collects half his damages from the cow. If the cow is not available, he collects a quarter of his damages from the calf.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, each of them gives half<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that the owner of the bull has to collect separately, first half of his claim of half the the damages up to a quarter of the calf’s value and then the remainder from the cow’s owner.</i>. What is the difference between them? If the calves died. According to him who said, he pays half of the damage from the cow and a quarter of the damage from the calf, each one pays his part of the damage. According to him who said, he pays half of the damage from the cow and the owner of the calves returns a quarter to the owner of the cow, one pays the damage, but the other one says, I did not want these, here they are before you<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the carcass of the calf is not worthless, he may return the dead bodies and claim his money back.</i>.
“And similarly, if a cow gored an ox,” etc. Rebbi Abun bar Cahana asked before Rebbi Immi: Because it is in doubt; therefore if it were certain, each one would give half<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Why is the calf treated on an equal footing with the cow? Is not the calf here only an appendix to the cow?</i>? Would I say that a foot of a person is the entire person? Each one would give half? He said to him, the Mishnah deals with a notorious animal<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">It is difficult to accept this reading since the Mishnah clearly speaks of half damages, but it is confirmed by all quotes of this paragraph in Medieval sources.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text is in Tosephta <i>‘Arakhin</i> 3:9,10.</i>“Its foot is sold to you,” he sold half<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The side to which the foot belongs.</i>. “Its second foot is sold to you,” he sold half of the [remaining] half<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not say that he was selling the other half, something is not sold. Therefore, sale of the unborn calf can be treated as sale of a quarter interest in the cow. If the cow were notorious, the owner of the calf would be liable as holder of a quarter interest in the cow.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, all these traditions are Levi’s<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">As the Babli points out, 46a, both Mishnaiot have to be rejected. Instead of an appeal to Symmachos’s principle that “one splits money in doubt”, one rules that “the burden of proof is on the claimant” and in the absence of witnesses the owner of the injured animal has no chance to prove any claim he might have on the calf. R. Simeon ben Laqish seems to attribute to Levi the acceptance of Symmachos’s rule in these cases.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a potter put his pots in a private courtyard without permission and an animal of the owner broke them, [the owner] is not liable; if [the animal] was hurt, the owner of the pots is liable. But if he puts them there with permission, the owner of the courtyard is liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a potter put his pots,” etc. If he put his pots in a private courtyard without permission and a bull came from another place and broke them, he<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The bull’s owner.</i> is not liable. If it was hurt by them, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The potter.</i>. This implies that ‘horn’<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Probably one should read: ‘foot’.</i> is not liable in a courtyard which belongs to neither party<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.1.4">Halakhah 2</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody put his produce in a private courtyard without permission and an animal of the owner ate them, [the owner] is not liable; if [the animal] was hurt, the owner of the produce is liable. But if he puts them there with permission, the owner of the courtyard is liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody put his produce in a private courtyard,” etc. If he put his produce in a private courtyard without permission and a bull came from another place and ate them, he is not liable. If it was hurt by them, he is not liable. This implies that the ‘tooth’ is not liable in a courtyard which belongs to neither party.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody put his bull in a private courtyard without permission and the owner’s bull gored it or his dog bit it, [the owner] is not liable; if it gored the owner’s bull, he<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The bull’s owner.</i> is liable. If it fell into his<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The courtyard's owner.</i> cistern and caused it to stink, he is liable. If his<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The courtyard's owner.</i> father or son were inside<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">And were killed by the falling bull.</i>, he pays weregilt. But if he put it in by permission, the owner of the courtyard is liable; Rebbi says, in all these cases<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:2:1-4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.2.1-4.1">Mishnaiot 3–5</a>.</i>, only if he<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The courtyard's owner.</i> agreed to watch.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody put a bull in a private courtyard,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 5:10" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).5.10">Tosephta 5:10</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 5:9" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).5.9">9</a>. A very incomplete quote in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 48a" href="/Bava_Kamma.48a">Babli, 48a</a>.</i>“If somebody put a bull in a private courtyard without permission, and a bull came from another place. If it gored, hit, bit, walloped, or kicked, he pays full damages, the words of Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">For him, half damages for ‘horn’ only applies in the public domain.</i>. But the Sages say, for ‘tooth’ and ‘foot’ he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:2:2-6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.2.2-6">Halakhah 1:2</a>.</i>; for goring, or hitting, or biting, or walloping, or kicking, or pushing, a tame [bull] pays half the damages, a notorious full damages from storage.”
Rav Jehudah in the name of Samuel: Practice follows Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 48b" href="/Bava_Kamma.48b">Babli 48b</a>.</i>. Rav Ze‘ira said, practice follows Rebbi. Rebbi Jeremiah said, everybody agrees that in a house, if he tells him, “put it in,” [he implies] “and I shall watch it.” Everybody agrees that on a field, if he tells him, “put it in,” [he implies] “and you watch it.” Where do they disagree? In a courtyard. Rebbi says, a courtyard is like a field, but the Sages say, a courtyard is like a house. There, we have stated<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.3.1">Mishnah 6:3</a>. There, it is stated that if the owner gave permission, he is liable for damages; this seems to contradict R. Jeremiah’s explanation.</i>: If somebody assembles sheaves in another person’s field without permission.” [R. Isaac said,]<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of E and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.3.2">Halakhah 6:3</a>.</i> sheaves on a field are like vessels in a house<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Because of their value, they always are guarded.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it by one which has a lock<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The field is fenced and locked in.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a bull intended [to hurt] another bull but hit a woman who then had a miscarriage, he is not liable. But if a man intended [to hurt] a neighbor but hit a woman who then had a miscarriage, he pays for the children<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:22" href="/Exodus.21.22"><i>Ex.</i> 21:22</a> speaks only about quarrelling men hitting a woman, not animals attacking.</i>. How does he pay for the children? One estimates the worth of the woman before and after she gave birth<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">How much she would be worth on the slave market.</i>. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, but the value of a woman increses after giving birth!<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Before she gave birth, a potential buyer would discount the price to account for the risk of death in childbirth.</i> But one estimates how much the children would have been worth and gives [the value] to the husband; if she does not have a husband, to the latter’s heirs. If she was a freedwoman or a proselyte<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If she was pregnant when she was manumitted or converted, she has no legal father of the children. Also if a woman was married to a freedman or a proselyte who died childless, the attacker does not have to pay since the husband has no heir (but cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 49" href="/Bava_Kamma.49">Babli 49a/b</a>, <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon b. Ioḥai</i>21:22).</i>, he is not liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a bull intended,” etc. A bull which intended [to attack] another; therefore, if it intended to hit the woman is he liable? Does it make a difference whether it intended to hit the woman or intended to hit another [bull]<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:22" href="/Exodus.21.22"><i>Ex.</i> 21:22</a> speaks only of men; animals are excluded irrespective of their actions. The same argument is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 49a" href="/Bava_Kamma.49a">Babli, 49a</a>, in the name of Rav Ada bar Aḥawa.</i>? Then why was it stated here? Abba bar Rav Ḥana<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">One might wonder whether to read “Abba bar bar Ḥana”.</i> said, because he had to state the next clause: “But if a human intended [to hurt] another human but hit a woman who then had a miscarriage, he pays for the children.”
It was stated: “I am selling you a fertile slave girl, her children increase her value. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, this is buttocks<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κάθισμα “buttocks, behind; seat; sinking”. It may be that the word is used here in a slightly pornographic meaning.</i>.” It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:22" href="/Exodus.21.22"><i>Ex.</i> 21:22</a>.</i>: “If quarrelling men hit a pregnant woman and she loses her children.” <sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 8; partially quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 49a" href="/Bava_Kamma.49a">Babli, 49a</a>.</i> Abba Yose ben Ḥanan said, from the meaning of “and she loses her children” would I not know that she was pregnant? Then why does the verse say “pregnant”? It tells you that he is not liable unless he hits her at the place of her pregnancy. Therefore, if he hit her on the palm of her hand or the sole of her foot, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In E and <i>Or Zarua‘</i> (vol. 3, #236), one reads: “If he hit her on the palm of her hand or the sole of her foot, <i>on her head or any of her limbs, would we understand that he be liable? The verse says,</i> “<i>pregnant</i>” (it tells you that he is not liable …).</i>. “As the woman’s husband will put on him”. Do I understand, even if the pregnancy is not his<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">If he married a pregnant girl.</i>? The verse says, “pregnant”. It tells that he pays only to the master of the pregnancy. Rebbi Uqba<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">In the Medieval parallel sources [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Kamma 43a" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Kamma.43a"><i>Tosaphot</i> 43a</a>, <i>Or zarua‘, Yalqut Šim‘oni Qonṭeros Aḥaron</i> (Ashkenazi), <i>Rashba ad</i> 43a (Sephardi)], “R. Aqiba”, an unlikely text.</i> asked, if somebody sleeps with his mother or his sister, is that one not also the master of the pregnancy? The verse says “husband”, somebody who may be called “husband”. This excludes those who cannot be called<sup class="footnote-marker">45a</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the reading להיקרות of E, not the bland “to be” of L.</i> “husband”<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Since an incestual marriage is no marriage at all (<i>Qiddušin</i> 3:12), <i>Or zarua</i>‘ (<i>loc. cit</i>.) deduces from here that the adulterous father of a bastard fetus has a claim on the money since he could have married the woman had she not been otherwise married. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 43a" href="/Bava_Kamma.43a">Babli, 43a</a>, explicitly gives the claim to the money to the boyfriend of an unmarried pregnant woman.</i>.
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Fetuses do not gain freedom by ‘tooth and eye.’<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">A slave gains his freedom if his master severely injures one of his limbs, exemplified by “tooth and eye”; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:26-27" href="/Exodus.21.26-27"><i>Ex.</i> 21:26–27</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.10"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3, Note 380</a>.</i> One understands “eye”. Tooth?<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows E. In L, the terms are exchanged, following the biblical mention of “tooth” before “eye”. But a fetus has no tooth, only eyes. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kiddushin 24b" href="/Kiddushin.24b">Babli, <i>Qiddušin</i> 24b</a>, understands the statement as meaning that the child of a slave mother is born free if he was injured in the womb by his master.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, so is the <i>baraita</i>: Fetuses do not gain freedom by their mother’s ‘tooth and eye.’<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">This contradicts the Yerushalmi’s doctrine (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3, Notes 403–407</a>) that a slave woman and her fetus cannot have different status; it follows the minority opinion of R. Joḥanan (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:14" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.14"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3, Notes 408</a>) that there can be no partial manumission except in the case of a slave belonging to two joint owners.</i>

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli and most independent Mishnah mss., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.6.1">Mishnah 7</a> is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.6.1">Mishnah 8</a> and vice versa.</i> One who digs a pit in the public domain is liable if an ox or a donkey fell in and died. Anyone who digs a pit, a ditch, a cave, rectangular excavations, or bases for poles, is liable. Then why is it said “a pit”? Just as a pit to be life-threatening must be ten handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">About 1 yard.</i> deep, so everything to be life-threatening must be ten handbreadths deep. If it was less than ten handbreadths deep when an ox or a donkey fell in and died, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:33-34" href="/Exodus.21.33-34"><i>Ex.</i> 21:33–34</a> is not applicable.</i>, but if they were damaged, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For any obstacle put into the public domain, the person responsible is liable for full damages (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.1.2">Chapter 1, Note 3</a>).</i>.<br>One who digs a pit in a private domain and opens it to the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">If the pit is so close to the public domain that an unsuspecting passer-by might be tripped by it, it is counted as obstacle in the public domain. If the private domain is separated from the public domain or another private domain by a fence at least 10 handbreadths high, the owner can do what he pleases on his property (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 50a" href="/Bava_Kamma.50a">Babli 50a</a>).</i>, in the public domain and opens it to a private domain, in a private domain and opens it to another private domain, is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Again, if the pit is too close to the other property, it is putting an unauthorized obstacle on somebody else’s property.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who digs a pit in the public domain,” etc. It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:33" href="/Exodus.21.33"><i>Ex.</i> 21:33</a>.</i>: “If a man open a pit” and “if a man dig a pit.” One pit for damages, the other pit for death<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The verse mentions “opening” for a pit which is not deep enough to kill an animal, and “digging” for a pit deep enough to kill.</i>. Rebbi Isaac said, both the pit of death and the pit of damages were added from the same verse, but when it comes to the pit of death you say he is not liable for vessels, but when it comes to the pit of damages you say he is liable for vessels<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In the next Halakhah one deduces “an ox but not an ox with his vessels” from the language of the verse. Why does this not apply to damages in case the animal survives the fall? No answer is given; it must be that the award of damages in this case is a matter of common, not biblical law.</i>! This is not only if he dug, from where if he bought, inherited, or it was given to him as a gift? The verse says, “if a man acquire a pit.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Understanding יכרה as not derived from I כרה “to dig” but from II כרה “to buy” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 2:6" href="/Deuteronomy.2.6"><i>Deut</i> 2:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Hosea 3:2" href="/Hosea.3.2"><i>Hos.</i> 3:2</a>); in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sotah 13a" href="/Sotah.13a">Babli (<i>Soṭah</i> 13a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 26a" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.26a"><i>Roš Haššanah</i> 26a</a>) this is charaterized as Phoenician.</i>” Has one the right to abandon his damages in the public domain<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since ‘pit’ is an obstacle in the public domain, how is it possible to acquire if from another person?</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it following Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah who said, three [handbreadths]<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Reading טפחים for דברים “things” in the text.</i> close to a domain are like that domain<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">This defines what means “close to another domain” in Mishnah 8 (Note 54). In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 50a" href="/Bava_Kamma.50a">Babli, 50a</a>, he requires a distance of at least 4 handbreadths. A private domain can be bought or inherited with all its liabilities.</i>.
Rebbi Mana asked: Can the depth be considered like the height? How is that<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">As I. Lewy correctly notes, היכי דמי is pure Babli language.</i>? If about him who comes from up high, he sees it. But for him who comes from below<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In the absence of parallel texts, any interpretation of this paragraph is speculative. The explanation of Eliahu Fulda seems the most natural: The pit is dug on an incline; it does not go deeper than the surrounding valley. For an ox coming down from the hill, the pit is simply a slightly steeper declivity, but for one climbing up from the valley it is a real obstacle.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A pit dug by two partners; if the first went by and did not cover it, the second and did not cover it, the second is liable. If the first covered it, the second came and uncovered it, the second is liable. If the first covered it, the second came, found it uncovered but did not cover it, the second is liable. If he covered it appropriately<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">This will be defined in the Halakhah.</i> but an ox or a donkey fell in and died, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:33" href="/Exodus.21.33"><i>Ex.</i> 21:33</a> explicitly absolves him if the pit is covered.</i>. If he did not cover it appropriately and an ox or a donkey fell in and died, he is liable. If it fell forward because of the noise of the excavation, he is liable, backward because of the noise of the excavation, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">An animal falling backward is an unusual occurrence that cannot be foreseen; also in this case the animal fell on the ground and not into the pit.</i>. If an ox and its equipment<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Harness and plough.</i> fell in and broke, a donkey and its equipment<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Cover and saddle.</i> was torn, he is liable for the animal but not liable for the equipment. If the ox falling in was deaf-mute, insane, or young, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">These categories do not apply to animals.</i>; a son or daughter, a slave or slave-girl, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Humans are supposed to watch where they are going.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A pit dug by two partners,” etc. How is a pit of two partners possible<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since the pit is dug in the public domain, it is the sole responsibility of the digger; there can be no joint ownership.</i>? Rebbi Yannai said, explain it if they both together put in the last stone<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">They together secured the rim lest the pit collapse. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 51a" href="/Bava_Kamma.51a">Babli, 51a</a>, this explanation is ascribed to R. Joḥanan, R. Yannai’s student.</i>. And why did they not explain that each of them dug ten handbreadths<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:2:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.2.4">Halakhah 1:2, Note 107</a>.</i>? Because he wants to explain it for a pit ten handbreadths deep<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The mininum depth for which the digger is responsible if an animal is killed. The Mishnah is always formulated to apply to the borderline case.</i> of two partners.
“If the first went by and did not cover it, the second and did not cover it, the second is liable.” Rebbi Abba bar Bizna<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">An Amora of this name is not otherwise known. With all quotes in Medieval authors one should read “bar Bina (Abbina)”.</i>, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: One gives him time to cut cedars from Lebanon<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">In the interpretation of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 52a" href="/Bava_Kamma.52a">Babli (52a</a>): The first partner is freed from liability if the second had time to bring wood from Lebanon after being informed that the pit was not covered.</i>. That can be a stringency and it can be a leniency<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">It is missing the main point of a legal rule, to be equally applicable to everybody. In the Babli, Samuel therefore states that the first is freed from liability the moment the second becomes aware of the situation.</i>. Sometimes he is close to the Lebanon, sometimes he is far from the Lebanon.
“If he covered it appropriately.” How much does it have to be covered? It shall be as we have stated there: “That [it can withstand] a truck full of stones driving over it.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:11:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.11.1">Mishnah <i>Bava batra</i> 3:12</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 1:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.1.1.7"><i>Bikkurim</i> 1:1, Notes 19–20</a>.</i>”
Rebbi Yannai said, in front of him, whether forward ot behind he is (not) liable. Outside, forward he is (not) liable, behind he is [not] liable<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the case that the animal was frightened by the noise of the excavation. It is clear that in the entire statement, “not liable” has to be replaced by “liable", and vice versa. This is confirmed by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:13" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.13">Tosephta 6:13</a> which frees from liability only if the animal falls backward outside the pit; <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 11 (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 289), <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon b. Ioḥai</i> 21:33 (ed. Epstein-Melamed p. 185 l. 13).</i>.
“If an ox and its equipment<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Harness and plough.</i> fell in and broke, a donkey and its equipment<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Cover and saddle.</i> and was torn, he is liable for the animal but not liable for the equipment.” Samuel said, when it became disoriented because of the air<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The bad air exuding from the pit.</i>, but if it hit the ground he is liable. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both say, even if it hit the ground, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 53a" href="/Bava_Kamma.53a">Babli, 53a</a>, Samuel’s statement is attributed to Rav, R. Joḥanan and Resh Laqish’s to Samuel. Since in the Babli practice follows Samuel and in the Yerushalmi R. Joḥanan, both Talmudim decide in the same way.</i>: The Torah absolved from liability when it fell down. “An ox or a donkey fell in there;<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:33" href="/Exodus.21.33"><i>Ex.</i> 21:33</a>.</i>” “an ox” and not an ox and its equipment; “a donkey” and not a donkey and its equipment<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:1:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.1.8">Halakhah 3:1, Note 34</a>, and the references given there.</i>. Since it looked logical: If a pit of damages<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In the next Halakhah one deduces “an ox but not an ox with his vessels” from the language of the verse. Why does this not apply to damages in case the animal survives the fall? No answer is given; it must be that the award of damages in this case is a matter of common, not biblical law.</i>, where one is not liable for death, one is liable for vessels; would it not be logical that for a pit ten handbreadths deep, for which one is liable for death, should one not be liable for vessels?<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Neziqin</i> 11 p. 288 l. 16.</i> The verse says, “an ox or a donkey fell in there;” “an ox” and not an ox and its equipment; “a donkey” and not a donkey and its equipment.
“If the ox falling in was deaf-mute, insane, or young, he is liable”. Rebbi Eleazar said, so is the Mishnah: an ox which is deaf-mute, an insane ox<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">One should not read the Mishnah as: “An ox, a deaf-mute, an insane, or a minor,” the last three items referring to humans.</i>, [an underage ox]<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Addition from E and a Genizah fragment; also required by general Talmudic style.</i>; “a son or daughter, a slave or slave-girl, he is not liable”.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The ox and any domestic animal equally are under the rules of falling into a pit, separating from Mount Sinai<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 19:13" href="/Exodus.19.13"><i>Ex.</i> 19:13</a>.</i>, paying double restitution, returning lost property, unloading, muzzling, interbreeding, and the Sabbath. Wild animals and birds follow the same rules. Then why was it said “ox or donkey”? But the verse speaks of the actuality<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">At the time of the promulgation of the Law, these were the most frequent cases.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The ox and any domestic animal equally are under the rules of falling into a pit,” etc. Falling into a pit, “and an ox or a donkey fell in there.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:33" href="/Exodus.21.33"><i>Ex.</i> 21:33</a>.</i>” Separating from Mount Sinai, “neither animal nor man shall live.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 19:13" href="/Exodus.19.13"><i>Ex.</i> 19:13</a>.</i>” Paying double restitution, “from ox to donkey<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:3" href="/Exodus.22.3"><i>Ex.</i> 22:3</a>, the penalty for the thief found with livestock.</i>”. To return lost property, “you shall certainly return them<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:1" href="/Deuteronomy.22.1"><i>Deut.</i> 22:1</a>.</i>.” Unloading, “do remove<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex..</i> 23:5.</i>.” Muzzling, “do not muzzle an ox while threshing<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:4" href="/Deuteronomy.25.4"><i>Deut.</i> 25:4</a>.</i>.” Interbreeding, “your animal you shall not breed <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 19:19" href="/Leviticus.19.19"><i>Lev.</i> 19:19</a>.</i>.” The Sabbath, “that your ox and your donkey may rest<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:12" href="/Exodus.23.12"><i>Ex.</i> 23:12</a>.</i>.”
“Wild animals and birds follow the same rules.” <sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:4:6-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.4.6-8"><i>Kilaim</i> 8:6</a>: R. Samuel, but in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 55a" href="/Bava_Kamma.55a">Babli, 55a</a>, in the name of Samuel.</i> Samuel said, the sea goose is <i>kilaim</i> with domesticated goose. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah does not say so, but an ox and a bison are <i>kilaim</i> with one another; a donkey and a wild donkey are <i>kilaim</i> with one another<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Contradicting the rabbis in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:4:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.4.6"><i>Kilaim</i> 8:6, Notes 97–98</a>. The Genizah fragment here has an addition: “A pig and a wild boar are <i>kilaim</i>;” this is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Kilayim (Lieberman) 1:8" href="/Tosefta_Kilayim_(Lieberman).1.8">Tosephta <i>Kilaim</i> 1:8</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, Rebbi stated a complete Mishnah: “Wild animals and birds follow the same rules.<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">If two related kinds of land animals are <i>kilaim,</i> then certainly land and water fowl are <i>kilaim</i> and one does not need a specific statement for this.</i>” Rebbi Joḥanan said, and I brought this from the House of Levi<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 55a" href="/Bava_Kamma.55a">Babli 55a</a>, in the name of R. Simeon ben Laqish.</i>: A rooster with a pheasant<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Greek φασιανός.</i>, a rooster with a peacock-hen<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">טַווָס, Greek ταώς “Peacock”.</i> are <i>kilaim</i> even though they are similar one to the other.

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss., the text reads:<br>He who brings a flock into a corral and locks it as he should, but they managed to get out and caused damage, is <i>not liable. If he did not lock them in as he should, they managed to get out and caused damage,</i> he is liable.<br>It seems that this text also underlies the Yerushalmi Halakhah; the shortened version may be a scribal error.</i> He who brings a flock<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Sheep and goats are notorious for eating everything edible in their way; in this respect they can be compared to a bull known to be goring.</i> into a corral and locks them in as he should<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 55b" href="/Bava_Kamma.55b">Babli, 55b</a>, defines “locking in as one should” as a minimal standard: fencing, including the doors, which can withstand a normal wind but not necessarily a storm. There is no reason to assume that the same minimal standard is accepted in the Yerushalmi.</i>, but they managed to get out and caused damage, is liable. If they broke out in the night<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">When it could not have been noticed by the rancher.</i> or robbers broke in and they managed to get out and caused damage, he is not liable. If robbers let them out<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">As the Halakhah explains, the robbers are liable only if they led the flock out for their own purpose. The case that they broke into the corral and the flock left through the breach is covered by the preceding sentence.</i>, the robbers are liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who brings a flock into a corral,” etc. Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Yannai: In disagreement<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli refers the Mishnah to a disagreement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.9.1">Mishnah 4:10</a> about a bull which was tied down with a rope and locked in, where R. Meïr does not absolve the owner from liability but R. Jehudah does. It is asserted that the Mishnah in the version of the Babli and the independent Mishnah mss. is R. Jehudah’s, but the Mishnah in the version of the Yerushalmi is R. Meïr’s.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, it<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The long version of the Mishnah.</i> is everybody’s opinion. Rebbi La said, Rebbi Eleazar’s statement is convincing since watching the animal itself is written only for “horn”<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Only for a notoriously goring bull does <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:36" href="/Exodus.21.36"><i>Ex.</i> 21:36</a> require that the animal be under the control of a human 24 hours a day; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 55b" href="/Bava_Kamma.55b">Babli 55b</a>. It follows that for damages classified as “tooth” or “foot”, even R. Meïr may agree to the long version of the Mishnah.</i>. What about it? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, in disagreement. Rebbi Eleazar said, it is everybody’s opinion. Some say, it follows Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob who said<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In the case of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.9.1">Mishnah 4:10</a>; the text is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 5:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).5.7">Tosephta 5:7</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 4:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.4.9.1">Chapter 4 Note 127</a>.</i>, in no case is he liable<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">R. Jehudah would agree with the short version of the Mishnah.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">In case the animals escaped in the night.</i> So far if [the corral] was fenced in on four sides. If it was fenced in on three sides and breached on one side<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">As the text stands, it seems to make no sense. Nobody can claim that animals are “locked in as they should be” if they are brought to an area open on one side. E reads “[the robbers] breached”; it has to be rejected since the Genizah text supports L. One has to explain that the corral is fenced in on all four sides but while three sides are strong enough to withstand a concerted push by the flock, the fourth side is not. In that case, was the escape of the flock to be foreseen and should the rancher be liable?</i>, and [an animal] left through the breach, what is the rule? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 11:7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).11.7">Tosephta <i>Bava Meṣi‘a</i> 11:7</a>.</i>: “If his wall fell in from the noise of earthquakes<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In the Genizah text and the Tosephta: “because of an earthquake”.</i> or thunder and he rebuilt it as required, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">If he followed the local building code, but the wall collapsed again and somebody was hurt in the collapse, the builder is not liable.</i>, otherwise he is liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, the rancher is liable for damages by animals escaping through a substandard fence.</i>” The only problem is if three better built walls fell together with it<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The question remains whether a builder is responsible for damages from the collapse of a substandard wall (or fence) when the same incident also destroyed walls built to local standards.</i>.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Shevuot 3:1-2" href="/Tosefta_Shevuot.3.1-2">Tosephta <i>Šebuot</i> 3:1–3</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 55b" href="/Bava_Kamma.55b">Babli 55b</a>.</i>: Rebbi Joshua said, four persons are not required to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that damages caused indirectly cannot be recovered in court.</i> but Heaven will not forgive them until they pay up: A person who knows testimony beneficial to another but does not testify<sup class="footnote-marker">19a</sup><i class="footnote">He refuses to appear as a witness in another person’s civil case who as a consequence loses his suit. The person refusing to be a witness has the moral obligation to idemnify the other.</i> is not required to pay but Heaven will not forgive him until he pays up. A person who hires false witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In money matters. This is indirect causation since “there is no agency in criminal matters”; the responsibility of a person knowingly committing a criminal act is not diminished by his performing the act for the benefit of another person.</i> against another and collects is not required to pay but Heaven will not forgive him until he pays up. A person who bends standing grain towards a fire<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">He causes a wildfire to burn agricultural produce which but for his intervention might have been spared.</i> or who makes a breach in a fence for animals<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">He lets the animals find the breach later on their own. This case explains why robbers who break into a corral and thereby create a possibility for the animals in the corral to escape cannot be sued for the damage caused later by the animals.</i> is not required to pay but Heaven will not forgive him until he pays up.
“If robbers let it out, the robbers are liable.” Rav Hoshaia said, if they let it out as part of their robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since they took the animals as their property, they take the liability with them.</i>. But if they let it out to get lost, the robbers are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">He lets the animals find the breach later on their own. This case explains why robbers who break into a corral and thereby create a possibility for the animals in the corral to escape cannot be sued for the damage caused later by the animals.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he left it<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Even though <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.1.1">Mishnah 1</a> is formulated for a flock, Mishnah two always refers to a single animal.</i> in the sun<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If the corral has no shade, the animal can be expected to try to break out to find shade somewhere. Since the owner causes the break, he is liable for all consequences.</i> or handed it over to a deaf-and-dumb, an insane, or an underage person<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">These three categories are the paradigm of irresponsible persons. Appointing these as shepherds is the equivalent of letting the animals roam freely without supervision.</i>, he is liable. If he handed it over to a shepherd, the shepherd takes his place. If it fell<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">By accident.</i> into a garden and caused damage<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">In most Babli and Mishnah mss., “and profited”, by eating the produce around it. A “garden” is an irrigated plot.</i>, it<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the Halakhah and some Mishnah and Alfassi mss., in the entire Mishnah one reads מְשַׁלֵּם “he (the owner) pays”, instead of מְשַׁלֶּמֶת “it (the animal) pays”.</i> pays for what it profited<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The animal’s owner does not have to pay the high-priced vegetables which his animal ate but only the value of animal feed of corresponding nutritional content.</i>. If it entered in due course<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Under the control of the owner, not by accident.</i>, it pays for what it damaged. How does it pay for what it damaged? One estimates a <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">2’500 square cubits, the standard size of a field; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.9"><i>Peah</i> 2:1, Note 31</a>.</i> of the field; how much was it worth and how much is it worth<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The animal’s owner only pays the diminution in value of the crop as if the damaged parcel had been part of a standard field whose yield is sold wholesale.</i>. Rebbi Simeon says, if it ate ripe produce it pays for ripe produce<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The exact retail value of what it destroyed.</i>, whether one or two <i>se’ah</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:20" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.20">Tosephta 6:20</a>.</i>: “If a shepherd handed his flock over to another shepherd, the first is liable, the second is not liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner did not give prior authorization for the change of control; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:4:10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.4.10"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:4, Note 449</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.5"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:5, Notes 127–146</a>. The first shepherd remains liable to the owner; he might sue the second shepherd once he paid the owner. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 56b" href="/Bava_Kamma.56b">Babli, 56b</a>, makes an exception if the second shepherd is employed by the first as his helper since then one may assume an implied consent for the transfer of control.</i>” Rav and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both said, since he handed it over to a responsible person, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah insists that the owner remain liable if he hands over control to an irresponsible person, one may infer that any change of control between responsible persons implies a change of liability.</i>.
“If he handed it over to a shepherd, the shepherd takes his place. If it fell into a garden and caused damage, he<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the Halakhah and some Mishnah and Alfassi mss., in the entire Mishnah one reads מְשַׁלֵּם “he (the owner) pays”, instead of מְשַׁלֶּמֶת “it (the animal) pays”.</i> pays for what it profited.” Rav Huna<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 57b-58a" href="/Bava_Kamma.57b-58a">Babli, 57b/58a</a>, “Rav”.</i> said, if it fell on vegetables<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">“Paying for the benefit” may be more than “paying for the damage”. If the animal would have had broken bones if the fall had not been cushioned by the vegetation, the benefit to the owner is quite large.</i>. But if it stood up and grazed, he pays for what it damaged<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">If the shepherd does not immediately remove the animal from the field, the subsequent damage is not considered caused by the accident but under the shepherd’s control.</i>.
“If it entered in due course, he<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the Halakhah and some Mishnah and Alfassi mss., in the entire Mishnah one reads מְשַׁלֵּם “he (the owner) pays”, instead of מְשַׁלֶּמֶת “it (the animal) pays”.</i> pays for what it damaged.”<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">If the shepherd does not immediately remove the animal from the field, the subsequent damage is not considered caused by the accident but under the shepherd’s control.</i> Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina said, on that field itself he is not liable, but for another field he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with Rav Huna and holds that for <i>all</i> damages caused by an animal falling into a growing area by accident, only the corresponding value of animal feed is due. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 58a" href="/Bava_Kamma.58a">Babli. 58a</a>, this position is attributed to Rav Cahana.<br>In the entire paragraph, “liable” means “liable for the full value of the destroyed produce”.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both said, even if it grazed on that entire field he is not liable; he only is liable if it left the field for the public domain and from the public domain entered another field<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 58a" href="/Bava_Kamma.58a">Babli, 58a</a>, R. Joḥanan holds that a change in the status of liability occurs as soon as the person responsible realizes that the animal left the field into which it had fallen.</i>. But what is the rule if it fell into a garden and then entered another garden? In the opinion of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina who said, on that field itself he is not liable, here he will be liable<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Since he imposes full liability as soon as the animal leaves the field of the accident.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who both said, even if it grazed on that entire field he is not liable; he only is liable if it left the field for the public domain and from the public domain entered another field. But if it fell into a place which it could have entered, or it entered a place into which it could have fallen?<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In a case which could be classified in two different ways, which section of the Mishnah is applicable?</i> Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina said, that is the Mishnah; it fell into a place into which it only could have fallen<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">A genuine accident. The leniency of the Mishnah only applies to a case in which the animal fell into a plot not accessible to it by walking.</i>. But if it fell into a place which it could have entered? Each case is problematic for one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose ben Ḥanina and R. Joḥanan.</i>. One needs the first case, that he pays what it profited<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">A genuine accident. The leniency of the Mishnah only applies to a case in which the animal fell into a plot not accessible to it by walking.</i>; the other<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Even R. Joḥanan will agree that an animal falling into a field accessible to it by walking is judged by the rules of entering, not falling.</i> needs the other case, that he pays what it damaged.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:21" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.21">Tosephta 6:21</a> (partially with different tradents); <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Simeon</i> <i>ben</i> <i>Ioḥai</i> p. 195. Parts are quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 58b-59a" href="/Bava_Kamma.58b-59a">Babli, 58b/59a</a>, as Amoraic statements.</i>: “One does not estimate a <i>bet</i> <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">30 <i>bet se’ah</i> = 75’000 square cubits. At this wholesale level, the damage done by the animal would be considered minimal and the farmer deprived of just compensation.</i> because it would diminish, nor a <i>bet</i> <i>qab</i><sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">One sixth of a <i>bet se’ah</i>. In a plot of this size the damage would be complete and the farmer overpaid.</i> because it would inflate. But one estimates a <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">2’500 square cubits, the standard size of a field; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Peah 2:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Peah.2.1.9"><i>Peah</i> 2:1, Note 31</a>.</i> of the field in question, how much it was worth and how much is it worth now. Rebbi Jehudah said, when? If it ate shoots of the fig tree or plucked shoots of vines. But if it ate unripe grapes or unripe figs, one estimates them as fully grown fruits. Rebbi Jehudah said in the name of Rebbi Aqiba: If it ate fully grown produce, he pays for fully grown produce, [fruits of]<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Reading of the Genizah text.</i> trees, one estimates trees for him. Trees one estimates by the <i>bet se’ah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">In a minimal orchard, ten trees grow on a <i>bet se’ah</i> (cf. <i>Ševi’it</i> 1:2, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.1.4">Note 18</a>). One has to estimate the diminution of value in the wholesale price of the yield of ten trees.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Jehudah said in the name of Rebbi Aqiba: Produce not yet one-third ripe is estimated under the rules of trees.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">To be estimated on a field the size of a <i>bet se’ah</i>.</i>” In the House of Rebbi Yannai they said, one estimates a three-<i>qab</i> in sixty three-<i>qabs</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A damaged plot the size of half a <i>bet se’ah</i> is estimated in 30 <i>bet se’ah</i>, not 60.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina said, one estimates a stalk in sixty stalks<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 58b" href="/Bava_Kamma.58b">Babli, 58b</a>.</i>.
A person stole a hard date<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Which is barely edible.</i>. The case came before Samuel who said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Probably to his student and aide, Rav Jehudah, to determine the amount of restitution due.</i>, go and estimate for him at the top of the date palm<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The place most exposed to the sun, which produces the sweetest and most expensive dates.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, it was a preserve of unripe dates<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The treatment turned an almost wooden fruit into a delicacy for which a comparison with the sweetest kind of fresh date might be appropriate.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody stacks his sheaves on another person’s field without permission and the field’s owner’s animals ate them, he<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The field’s owner; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 5:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.5.3.1">Mishnah 5:4</a>.</i> is not liable. If it was damaged by it<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If the produce stacked there was inappropriate as animal feed.</i>, the owner of the stack is liable. But if he stacked his sheaves with permission, the field’s owner is liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.5">Halakhah 5:5, Notes 29–32</a>.</i> If somebody stacks his sheaves on another person’s field without permission.” R. Isaac said, are sheaves on a field like vessels in a house? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, explain it by one which has a lock.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who causes a fire through the hand of a deaf-mute, an insane, or an underage person<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">These are irresponsible; no liability can be imposed on them.<br>The expression “to send a fire” is a pun on the expression “sending בעירה” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:4" href="/Exodus.22.4"><i>Ex</i>. 22:4</a>), his animal, and paying for בערה “arson” in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:5" href="/Exodus.22.5">v. 5</a>; cf. Ibn Ezra <i>ad</i> <i>loc</i>.</i> is not liable in human law<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Damages by indirect arson cannot be recovered.</i> but liable in Heaven’s law<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">He cannot hope for divine forgiveness unless he pays the damages.</i>. If he causes it through a hearing person, the hearing person is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since “there is no agency in criminal matters”, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.1.4">Note 20</a>.</i>. If one person brings the fire and another one the wood, the one bringing the wood is liable. If one brings the wood and another one the fire, the one bringing the fire is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">If several ingredients are necessary, the person contributing the last ingredient makes the act possible and, therefore, bears full liability.</i>. If another person came and fanned the flames, the one who fanned is liable. If the wind fanned it<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">And without wind the fire would not have caused damage.</i>, none of them is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Since they did not directly cause the damages.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sets a fire,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">A similar discussion is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 59b-60a" href="/Bava_Kamma.59b-60a">Babli, 59b–60a</a>; but the underlying argument there seems to be different.</i>Ḥizqiah said, if he handed him<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The irresponsible person.</i> a glowing coal. But if he handed him an open flame he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In that case, he is directly responsible for the fire; it is not indirect causation.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, coal and flame are the same<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Since after handing the fire to the deaf-and-dumb, insane, or underaged, he cannot know what they are going to do with it; the causation of the damage is still indirect. R. Joḥanan will agree that if somebody hands a fire to an irresponsible person and then guides that person until the fire is set, he is the arsonist and liable for all damages.</i>. It is difficult according to Ḥizqiah, for if somebody saw another person’s glowing coal rolling by and did not extinguish it, is he not free from liability<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The passive bystander who fails to prevent a fire cannot be sued for damages. Then what is the differencebetween an irresponsible and a responsible person in this matter?</i>? They said, explain it if he handed him an ownerless glowing coal, or also an ownerless flame, and one cannot infer anything<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The responsible person will acquire the coal or burning object by picking it up and becomes liable in all respects; an irresponsible person cannot acquire.<br>E has a different version of the last sentence, which avoids the Babylonian expression אינמי:<br>תִּיפְתָּר שֶׁמָּסַר לוֹ שַׁלְהֶבֶת שֶׁל הֶקְדֵּשׁ<br>וְלֵית שׁ״מ כְּלוּם.<br>“Explain it if he handed him a flame belonging to the Sanctuary, and one cannot infer anything.”<br>Taking the Sanctuary’s property is sinful; only the adult can sin.</i>.
Some Tannaĩm state: וְלִיבָּה. Some Tannaïm state: וְנִיבָּה. He who says וְלִיבָּה. As it is written, “in the flaming fire”<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 3:2" href="/Exodus.3.2"><i>Ex</i>. 3:2</a>.</i>. He who says וְנִיבָּה, “it was in my heart like burning fire.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 20:9" href="/Jeremiah.20.9"><i>Jer</i>. 20:9</a>. A similar argument is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 60a" href="/Bava_Kamma.60a">Babli, 60a</a>.<br>It seems that the difference between the versions is simply a dialectal switch between the liquids <i>l</i> and <i>n</i>; but a biblical source is sought. In the Babli, the reference is to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 57:19" href="/Isaiah.57.19"><i>Is</i>. 57:19</a>, where the “fruit of the lips” is interpreted as “speech”. The verse from <i>Jer</i>. quoted here then points out that speech can be fiery, i. e., with one’s mouth one can fan a fire.</i>”
There<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 60a" href="/Bava_Kamma.60a">Babli 60a</a>, in the name of Rava, the representative of Galilean Halakhah.</i>, they say, the Mishnah speaks of a catastrophic wind, but in case of normal wind he is liable. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish both say, even in case of normal wind he is not liable; for sometimes it blows, sometimes it does not blow<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Wind is unpredictable; wind which is fanning a fire cannot be planned. If without the wind the fire would not have spread, the person starting the fire is not liable.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sets a fire which consumes wood, or stones, or dust, is liable since it is said: “If fire gets out of control and finds thorns, etc.”<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:5" href="/Exodus.22.5"><i>Ex</i>. 22:5</a>. The Halakhah will explain the proof from the verse.</i> If it crossed a wall four cubits high<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">A stone or brick wall which will not burn.</i>, or a public road<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">At least 16 cubits wide (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.6.2">Halakhah 6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 6:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.6.4.1">Mishnah <i>Bava</i> <i>batra</i> 6:7</a>).</i>, or a brook<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">At least 8 cubits wide (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.6.2">Halakhah 6</a>).</i>, he is not liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sets a fire which consumes wood,<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">The full verse reads: “If fire gets out of control and finds thorns, or consumes a stack of sheaves, or standing grain, or the field, the person who set the blaze shall certainly pay.” Similar discussions on what the different examples mentioned in the verse imply are in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 60a" href="/Bava_Kamma.60a">Babli 60a</a>, <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Ismael</i>, <i>Neziqin</i> 14 (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 297), <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Simeon</i> <i>ben</i> <i>Ioḥai</i> p. 196.</i>” etc. Since thorns are flammable, do I only include flammable material? The verse says, “a stack”. Since a stack of sheaves is particular in that it was harvested, do I only include harvested material? The verse says, “standing grain.” Since all of these are particular in that they are flammable, do I only include flammable material? If it swept his furrow or damaged his stone, from where? The verse says, “or the field.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">The earth constituting the field.</i>” Should it only have mentioned thorns, standing grain, and stack, but not mentioned field? In the opinion of Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.7.1">Mishnah 7</a>. Since R. Jehudah requires the arsonist to pay for everything of value stored inside the stack, nothing of value would have been excluded if “field” had not been mentioned.</i> this is acceptable since he says, he pays for everything inside. But according to the rabbis, who say that he only pays for a stack of wheat or barley sheaves, why did it mention “field”<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">To include stones and require the arsonist also to pay for the work needed to plough the field anew.</i>, why did it mention “stack”<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">To include everything stacked, even limestone assembled to be burned into lime (<i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Ismael</i>).</i>?
“If it crossed a wall four cubits high, or a public road, or a brook, he is not liable.” Rebbi Abba said, the Mishnah speaks of burning fire<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">A fire which spreads by burning the flammable material next to it, to be distinguished from wind-whipped fire which jumps over fire clearings and waterways.</i>. But if it is wind-whipped everybody agrees that he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since it can be expected to cross a fire clearing of 16 cubits and more.</i>.
