Rebbi Joḥanan said, the Mishnah speaks of wind-whipped fire, but if it is burning he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Since it can be expected to cross a fire clearing of 16 cubits and more.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody starts a fire inside his property, how far does it have to spread<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">That the spreading of the fire was not foreseeable and the person starting the fire would no longer be liable.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah says, one looks at it as if he started it at the center of a <i>bet</i> <i>kor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Which is 75’000 square cubits (Note 49). The edge length of a <i>bet kor</i> therefore is 273.86 cubits and the distance of the center from the side is 136.93 cubits. For him, the liability stops if a strip 137 cubits wide around the fire is protected against the spread of the fire. </i> Rebbi Eliezer says, sixteen cubits like a public road; and Rebbi Aqiba says, 50 cubits; and Rebbi Simeon says “the person setting the fire shall certainly pay,<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:5" href="/Exodus.22.5"><i>Ex</i>. 22:5</a>.</i>” everything depends on the setting of the fire.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody starts a fire inside his property,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:22-23" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.22-23">Tosephta 6:22–23</a>; cf. <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Ismael</i>, <i>Neziqin</i> 14 (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 297), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 61a" href="/Bava_Kamma.61a">Babli 61a</a>.</i>“It happened that a fire crossed the Jordan, because it was a firestorm<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">A large fire can create its own storm which carries the flames beyond the expected limits.</i>. How far may a fire spread<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">How wide a strip has to be cleared of flammable material to protect the person who set the fire from damage claims?</i>? Rebbi Eliezer says sixteen cubits like a public road, if there is wind thirty cubits. Rebbi Jehudah says thirty cubits, if there is wind fifty cubits. Rebbi Aqiba says 50 cubits, if there is wind a hundred cubits. It happened in Arabia that a fire jumped more than three hundred cubits. When is this? If it jumps. But if there is wood or it burns continuously<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">If the safety strip is not cleared of all flammable material.</i>, he is liable even up to a <i>mil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">2’000 cubits.</i>. If it crossed a river or a pond eight cubits wide, he is not liable.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who sets fire to a stack of sheaves which contains implements, Rebbi Jehudah says, he has to pay everything contained in it but the Sages say, he pays only for a stack of wheat sheaves or a stack of barley sheaves<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">He has to pay only for what was visible from the outside.</i>. If a goat was tied to it, and a slave was nearby<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Since the slave was not bound, he could have escaped. The arsonist is not guilty of murder; he is liable for all material damage which he may cause.</i>, and they were burned, he is liable. If a slave was tied to it, and a goat was nearby, and they were burned, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Since the slave could not have escaped, the arsonist is a murderer. Even if he cannot be criminally prosecuted for lack of eye witnesses or for other reasons, he cannot be sued for monetary damages (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.6"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1, Notes 29ff</a>., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:2-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.2-15"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Notes 3–73</a>, and sources given there.)</i>. But the Sages agree with Rebbi Jehudah that one who sets fire to a building has to pay everything contained in it since people regularly store things in houses.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who sets fire to a stack of sheaves,” etc. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Sages agree with Rebbi Jehudah about threshing tools which usually are hidden there<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 61b" href="/Bava_Kamma.61b">Babli (61b</a>) agrees in the name of Rava, the proponent of Galilean Halakhah.</i>. The argument of Rebbi Jehudah seems inverted. There<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 6:9. The parallel treatment there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.6">Halakhah 6:9 (Notes 151–154</a>) is more explicit.</i> he says except what is hidden, but here he says including what is hidden? But does not Rebbi Jehudah simply explain “stack”? Why was “stack” written? That he has to pay for all that is in it<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">The difference has nothing to do with positions of R. Jehudah, but with his interpretation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:5" href="/Exodus.22.5"><i>Ex</i>. 22:5</a>. The verse states that the arsonist has to pay for “thorns, or a stack, or standing grain, or a field.” Since even without mention of “stack” the arsonist would have to pay for the grain, the inclusion of “stack” can only mean “everything in a stack, whether recognizable from the outside or not.”</i>. The argument of the rabbis seems inverted. There they say, including what is hidden; but here they say, except what is hidden? But did not Rebbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Sages agree with Rebbi Joḥanan about threshing tools which usually are hidden there?<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">They do not disagree with R. Jehudah. Their difference is about the standards of proof required from the victims of a fire. R. Jehudah holds that the arsonist would have to disprove any claim while the rabbis hold that the claimant still has the burden of proof unless he is supported by current agricultural practice.</i> Rebbi Hoshaia said, if there are no witnesses. But if there are witnesses, everybody agrees with Rebbi (Yose)<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read: Jehudah, with Rosh (Chapter 6, #16).</i> as it was stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:24" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.24">Tosephta 6:24</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 62a" href="/Bava_Kamma.62a">Babli 62a</a>.</i>“If it was a stack of wheat sheaves covered with barley or a stack of barley sheaves covered with wheat, he pays for a stack of barley.” As the following: A person deposited a tied sack with another person, when something happened. This one said, it was full of dross, but the other said, it was full of raw silk. [ ]<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Add with Rosh and Alfasi (#125): “Rav said.”</i> He swears and collects<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The text is elliptic; it is best explained following Nahmanides (and all Spanish commentators of the Babli.) The first problem is what is the connection between the story and the question at hand; the second is why the claimant may swear and collect. Nahmanides (as quoted by Rashba <i>ad</i> <i>62a</i> and others) thinks that the sack was lost in a fire; the owner of the sack claims payment from the person responsible for the fire. Now Mishnah <i>Šebuot</i> 7:1 states that in general one only accepts an oath of a person who can absolve himself of payment by the oath. To collect money based on an oath is possible only in very few cases, one of them being the victim of a robbery. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 62a" href="/Bava_Kamma.62a">Babli, 62a</a>, it is stated that R. Jehudah equates loss by fire to loss by robbery. Since the Yerushalmi holds that the Sages mostly agree with R. Jehudah, everybody must hold that the victim of a fire may swear and collect. In the case described, since the sack was deposited tightly tied, the argument of the person with whom the sack was deposited (who may have set the fire himself), that the sack might have contained scrap metal, is not more than a conjecture and may be disregarded.<br>By the biblical law of deposits (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6-8" href="/Exodus.22.6-8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6–8</a>), the person holding the deposit has to swear that he did nothing to impair the deposit; then he is not liable. If he set the fire, he cannot swear and, therefore, should be required to pay without the other side being forced to swear. However, this only applies if the nature of the deposit was known. If the contents of the sack were not ascertained by the trustee when he accepted the sack, the rule that the person who cannot swear must pay is irrelevant.</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan asked: Can one argue for him an argument which does not apply to him<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The entire paragraph is in a slightly different formulation in <i>Šebuot</i> 7:2, 37d.<br>Since the Sages agree with R. Jehudah that for matters within general practice one believes the claimant of fire damages without proof, must the “general practice” be interpreted narrowly or may one admit, e. g., practices of rich people for a poor claimant?</i>? Let us hear from the following: Bar Ziza’s sharecropper deposited a pound<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">A Roman pound, 345 g, an enormous value in the early 3rd Century.</i> of gold with somebody. Bar Ziza and Bar Ziza’s sharecropper died; the case came before Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose. He said, does not everybody know that all Bar Ziza’s sharecrooper had was Bar Ziza’s property? It should be given to Bar Ziza’s sons. Bar Ziza had adult and underage sons. He said, the adult one should take half and when the underage ones grow up, they should receive half. Rebbi Ismael ben Rebbi Yose died; the case came before Rebbi Ḥiyya who said, that argument<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">R. Ismael ben R. Yose’s.</i> means nothing [since there are people who do not flout their wealth]<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">Missing here, added from the text of Alfasi (#125) and the parallel in <i>Šebuot</i>. In the latter text, the position of R. Ḥiyya is explicitly endorsed by R. Joḥanan who quotes <a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 13:7" href="/Proverbs.13.7"><i>Prov</i>. 13:7</a>: “There are those who pose as rich and have nothing, pose as poor and have great wealth”.</i>; it should be given to the sharecropper’s sons. The trustee told him, I already disbursed half of it. He told him, what you gave, you gave by court order and what you will give, you will give by court order. May the sharecropper’s sons say to Bar Ziza’s sons, give us what you took? They can tell them, what was done was done by court order. May the underage ones say to the adult, let us share with you? May they tell them, we found a find? Rebbi Isaac said, the case between the adult and underaged ones is compared to one who gave a gift<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Since R. Ismael ben R. Yose said that “it should be given to Bar Ziza’s sons,” all have to share equally.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a spark came from under a hammer and caused damage, he<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The person working the hammer.</i> is liable. If a camel was loaded with flax and while it walked in the public domain the flax entered a store, was lit by the storekeeper’s light, and set the building on fire, the camel’s owner is liable. If the store owner put his light outside<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">An illicit use of the public domain.</i>, the store owner is liable. Rebbi Jehudah says, in case of a Ḥanukkah light he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Where an outside display is required.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a spark is coming from under a hammer,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:28" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.28">Tosephta 6:28</a>.</i>“Rebbi Jehudah stated: In case of a Ḥanukkah light he is not liable because he acted with permission<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Where an outside display is required.</i>. But the Sages say, in any case he is liable, as, e. g., those who build huts in front of their stores on Tabernacles because it is permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the religious requirement, the use of public space for a hut is permitted under the jurisdiction of the Jewish court but it cannot supersede the right of others to unhindered access to the public domain.</i>; but if somebody else is hurt by them, he is liable.”

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The obligation to make double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The general punishment of a thief, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:3" href="/Exodus.22.3"><i>Ex</i>. 22:3</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8">8</a>.</i> is more frequent than that of paying quadruple or quintuple restitution since the obligation to make double restitution applies both to living things and to inanimate objects; but the obligation of paying quadruple or quintuple restitution only applies to oxen and sheep, as it is said: “If a man steal an ox or a sheep,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:37" href="/Exodus.21.37"><i>Ex</i>. 21:37</a> decrees quintuple payment for stolen cattle sold or slaughtered by the thief and quadruple payment for stolen sheep or goats.</i>” etc. He who steals from a thief does not pay double restitution, nor does the one who slaughters or sells after a thief pay quadruple or quintuple restitution.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The obligation to make double restitution is more frequent,” etc. From where does he get this? From “if being found it is found<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:3" href="/Exodus.22.3"><i>Ex</i>. 22:3</a>: “If the stolen goods being found arc found alive in his hand, be it ox, or donkey, or sheep, doubly shall he pay.” Since in 21:37 quadruple or quintuple restitution was ordered for selling or slaughtering stolen cattle or sheep, the mention of “donkey” extends the set of objects subject to double restitution beyond that subject to multiple restitution.</i>”. This includes not only ox, or donkey, or sheep: Since He says “alive”, it includes all living beings<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 83b" href="/Bava_Kamma.83b">Babli 83b</a>, <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Ismael</i> <i>Neziqin</i> 13, <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Simeon</i> <i>ben</i> <i>Ioḥai</i> p. 194.</i>. “The stolen goods”, to include movables. Why “ox, or donkey, or sheep”? If it were restricted to anything useful for the altar, one should have stated “ox and sheep”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since a donkey is an impure animal excluded from the altar, its mention declares the law to be a strictly profane matter.</i>. [If it were restricted to anything useful for the priests, one should have stated “ox and donkey.]<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Addition of E, confirmed by a Genizah fragment. At first look, the argument seems to prove that double restitution be required only for animals whose firstborn are either given to the Cohen (cattle and sheep, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:19" href="/Exodus.34.19"><i>Ex</i>. 34:19</a>), or for which a substitute is given to the Cohen (donkeys, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:20" href="/Exodus.34.20"><i>Ex</i>. 34:20</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:13" href="/Exodus.13.13">13:13</a>). The extended arguments in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 64a" href="/Bava_Kamma.64a">Babli, 64a</a>, and even more so in <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Simeon</i> <i>ben</i> <i>Ioḥai</i>, p. 194, note that the explicit mention of “stolen goods” shows that the list is incomplete and, therefore, no restriction is intended..</i> Rebbi Ismael stated: It excludes real estate which is not movable; it excludes slaves from whom you only have service; it excludes documents which only are for proof<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 64b" href="/Bava_Kamma.64b">Babli 64b</a>; for these only simple restitution is required. Documents which prove a claim do not in themselves represent money’s worth.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu asked: Why did we not state that double restitution is due if a claim of theft is made in a partnership<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">In any trust relation, the person making a false claim that something was stolen which he himself took has to make double restitution, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex</i>. 22:8</a>.</i>, where quadruple or quintuple restitution does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli quotes R. Ḥiyya bar Abba in the name of R. Joḥanan that quadruple or quintuple restitution applies in case of a fraudulent claim of theft, 62b.</i>?
It was stated: “It was stolen from the man’s house”<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:6" href="/Exodus.22.6"><i>Ex</i>. 22:6</a>, speaking of the responsibilities of the unpaid trustee. (Different <i>baraitot</i> explaining the verse are in <i>Šebuot</i> 8:1, fol. 38b). The verse ends: If the thief is found he has to make double restitution.</i>, but not from the woman’s house<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since this interpretation does not make sense, it is natural to read the verse meaning that the thief stealing from the trustee has to pay double but the thief stealing from the thief does not. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bekhorot 11a" href="/Bekhorot.11a">Babli, <i>Bekhorot</i> 11a</a>, the expression is read to exclude stealing from public property from double restitution.</i>? Is not “his neighbor” only written in respect of the borrower<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:13" href="/Exodus.22.13"><i>Ex</i>. 22:13</a>. In fact, “his neighbor” is also written for the paid trustee, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:9" href="/Exodus.22.9"><i>Ex</i>. 22:9</a>. In all these cases, it is emphasized that one only deals with the principal, not that anything was stolen from a thief. E reads: “Only the borrower is called neighbor.”</i>? But one has to say that “he who steals from a thief does not pay [double restitution, nor does the one who slaughters or sells after a thief pay]<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This is a quote from the Mishnah, complete only in E. In the Genizah fragment, after the first few words the rest is indicated by “etc.” In the Leiden ms. it is missing; the scribe skipped from תשלומי to תשלומי.</i> quadruple or quintuple restitution. If something was stolen which then was stolen from him; <sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Addtion from E supported by the Genizah fragment. It is again missing in L because the scribe omitted the text between two identical expressions.</i>[when in the end the stolen object was found, to whom does he pay? To the first owner, to the second<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The thief.</i>, to both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The answer is given at the end of the paragraph.</i>? If something was stolen which then was stolen from him<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The thief.</i>]; the owners apprehended the second thief who swore to them<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">He swore falsely that he did not steal the object from them. If the owners can recover the object from the second thief, he swore falsely and has to bring a reparation sacrifice as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:1-13" href="/Leviticus.5.1-13"><i>Lev</i>. 5:1–13</a>. But if the owners can only demand payment of the object’s worth, he did not swear falsely since he did not steal from them.</i>. If you say that one takes it away from the second, he has to bring a sacrifice for the oath. If you say that one does not take it away from the second, he does not have to bring a sacrifice for his oath. If something was stolen which then was stolen from him<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The thief.</i> and the second thief decides to return it<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Who also will confess from whom he took the object.</i>, if you say that he has to return it to the owners, they might not inform the first thief<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">That he has to pay simple restitution since the object already was returned.</i>. If you say that he has to return it to the first thief, that one might not inform the owners<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">That he owes them double restitution.</i>. What does he have to do? He returns it to the owners in the presence of the thief.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who stole according to the testimony of two people<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">To recover stolen property is a civil matter and is judged according to civil law. But for the owners to collect double, quadruple, or quintuple restitution requires a criminal conviction of the thief which can be based only on the testimony of at least two eye witnesses to the act.</i> and slaughtered or sold according to their testimony or the testimony of two others, has to pay quadruple or quintuple restitution. He who stole and sold on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">This is forbidden but in itself is not a capital crime which would preempt the imposition of a fine. But if he slaughtered on the Sabbath he committed a capital crime and could not be sentenced to a fine; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 6:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.6.7.1">Chapter 6, Note 102</a>.</i>, stole and sold to pagan worship<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">As long as he does not himself participate in pagan worship, no capital crime has been committed.</i>, stole and slaughtered on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The punishment for this deadly sin is left to Heaven, not the human court.</i>, stole from his father, slaughtered, or sold, and then his father died,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Even though he is an heir, he has to pay to his co-heirs their share in the fine payable to the estate.</i> stole, slaughtered, and then dedicated it to the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since he already was subject to the fine when he dedicated, the dedication cannot erase the liability.</i>, has to pay quadruple or quintuple restitution. He who stole and slaughtered for medical purposes or for the dogs, he who slaughtered and it turned out torn<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">On inspection the animal was found to be sick and the meat unfit for human consumption.</i>, he who slaughtered a profane animal in the Temple courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The slaughtered animal is forbidden for any use.</i>, has to pay quadruple or quintuple restitution. Rebbi Simeon frees from liability in these two cases<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that slaughter which does not prepare meat for human consumption technically is called “killing” rather than “slaughter” and is not covered by the rule of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:37" href="/Exodus.21.37"><i>Ex</i>. 21:37</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">29a</sup><i class="footnote">This is the beginning of the discussion about when a fine can or cannot be imposed. The correct text is in Terumot 6:1, Notes 5,6. There is no sacrifice which might atone for an intentional sin.</i> Did not Rabbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he is intentional about fat but in error about a sacrifice, he can be warned, is whipped, and brings a sacrifice. <sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Notes 2,14.</i> Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:2" href="/Deuteronomy.25.2"><i>Deut</i>. 25:2</a>) “Because of his guilt.” If two alternatives are presented to the court, one chooses one of them. This excludes matters in the power of Heaven.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One was convicted of theft on the testimony of two witnesses and of slaughtering or selling on their testimony; if they are found perjured they pay everything<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:19" href="/Deuteronomy.19.19"><i>Deut</i>. 19:19</a> requires that the punishment of a perjured witness be the statutory punishment which would have been imposed on the accused. Testimony can be shown to be perjured only by other testimony which establishes that the witness was not in a position to see the act which he testifies about; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:10:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.10.6"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:11, Note 199</a>.</i> to him. If he was convicted of theft on the testimony of two witnesses and of slaughtering or selling on the testimony of two others who all were found perjured, the first group pays double restitution and the second group triple<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">As pointed out at the end of the Mishnah, everybody is permitted to sell or slaughter his livestock. The act becomes criminal only if the animal was stolen. Therefore the testimony of the second group is predicated on that of the first group; they only have to pay the difference their testimony would make in the sentence imposed on the defendant had their testimony been true. For the imputed sale of a stolen sheep they pay double, for that of a stolen ox triple damages.</i>. If the second group were found perjured, he pays double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For the valid conviction of theft.</i> while they pay triple<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Or double, as the case may be.</i>. If one of the second group was found perjured, the second testimony is invalidated<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A single witness in a criminal proceeding is disregarded, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:15" href="/Deuteronomy.19.15"><i>Deut</i>. 19:15</a>.</i>. If one of the first group is found perjured, the entire testimony is invalidated since if there is no theft there is no slaughter or sale<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">No possibility to prosecute for sale or slaughter.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “A person convicted of theft on the testimony of two witnesses,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">A differently edited text is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Makkot 1:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Makkot.1.5.1"><i>Makkot</i> 1:8</a>; the subject of this paragraph is hinted at in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:5:6-6:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.5.6-6.3"><i>Bava batra</i> 3:6</a>. The paragraph refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>, not <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.4.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i> Rebbi Ze‘ira said, this implies that a perjured witness is not disqualified by the court but is disqualified by himself<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 72b" href="/Bava_Kamma.72b">Babli 72b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 27a" href="/Sanhedrin.27a"><i>Sanhedrin</i> 27a</a>, as opinion of Abbai. The testimony of a proven criminal is invalid since by accepting such testimony the court would become the accomplice of an evildoer, which is forbidden in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:1" href="/Exodus.23.1"><i>Ex</i>. 23:1</a>. The question is whether testimony becomes invalid only after the witness has been convicted of a felony or by his felonious act. While in general testimony of a criminal becomes unacceptable only after conviction, perjury carries its own conviction and all testimony rendered after one which was determined to be perjured automatically becomes void.<br>The argument presented by R. Ze‘ira is based on the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.3.1">Mishnah 3</a>: If the first testimony was perjured, the second testimony becomes void. But at the time that the second group of witnesses testified it was not known that the first testimony was perjured. This proves that a later discovery of perjury invalidates testimony retroactively.</i>. Explain it by warning<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Criminal intent can be proven only if the person was duly warned that his intended action was criminal (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.8.1.4"><i>Kilaim</i> 8:1 Note 9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sotah 7:1:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sotah.7.1.7"><i>Soṭah</i> 7:1 Note 26</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 8:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.8.1.6"><i>Nazir</i> 8:1 Note 46</a>). The court before accepting testimony has to warn the witnesses of the consequences of perjury. If both groups of witnesses were warned simultaneously, there is only one testimony and R. Ze‘ira’s argument does not prove anything.</i>, as it was stated: “Rebbi Yose said, when has this been said? For two testimonies with two warnings. But for one testimony and one warning any testimony which is partially disqualified is totally disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 73a" href="/Bava_Kamma.73a">Babli 73a</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 6:23" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).6.23">Tosephta 6:23</a>.</i>.” What does it mean that if it is partially disqualified it is totally disqualified? If they were standing and testifying against him on then tenth of Nisan that he had stolen an ox on the first of Nisan. He slaughtered or sold on the tenth of Nisan. They were shown to be perjured on the fifteenth of Nisan. Any testimony which they delivered between the tenth and the fifteenth of Nisan is retroactively disqualified. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, explain it if they deliver their testimony in one group and you cannot infer anything, as we have stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Batra (Lieberman) 2:9" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Batra_(Lieberman).2.9">Tosephta <i>Bava</i> <i>batra</i> 2:9</a>. A person claims ownership of real estate currently in the possession of another person. Neither party has documentary proof. The claimant can show that he was a prior owner or is the heir of a prior owner. The person in possession claims to have bought or otherwise legally acquired the property. If the person can show by testimony that the claimant had failed to protest during three years in which he had undisturbed and continuous possession, the court will declare him the legal owner. (This is one of the many meanings of <i>ḥazaqah</i>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5 Note 100</a>). If the same group testifies three times on the possessor’s behalf, covering three years, and one of their testimonies later was found to be perjured, that and all subsequent testimonies arc thrown out by the court.</i>“They were the first and they were the later [witnesses]. If they were shown to be perjured at first, there is nothing. At the second time, there is one testimony. At the third time, there are two testimonies.” And how is<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">היכי דמי is an expression otherwise known only from the Babli. E and the parallel in <i>Makkot</i> have היכי אמר, idiomatic Galilean Aramaic.</i> that if they give testimony in one group, you cannot infer anything? It comes only based on multiple testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">R. Abba bar Mamal’s argument is rejected. Since possession is established by separate testimonies regarding three successive years, it is impossible to explain the Tosephta as referring to a single testimony. R. Ze‘ira’s inference is confirmed.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In L, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.3.3">Halakhah 3</a> comes after <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.3.2">Halakhah 4</a>, but in the Genizah and E texts, and <i>editio</i> <i>princeps</i>, the order is as given here.</i> “One was convicted of theft on the testimony of two witnesses,” etc. So far if the witnesses for theft and those for slaughter came together<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since slaughter or sale arc not criminal unless the animal was stolen, it is clear that the Mishnah refers to a case in which both theft and slaughter are dealt with in the same court procedure.</i>. If witnesses for theft appeared but were not accepted<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Their testimony did not stand up under preliminary cross-examination. Failing there is not proof of perjury.</i> and after that came witnesses for slaughter. One tells them “witnesses for theft already had appeared but were not accepted, but on the strength of your testimony<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Which supports the testimony of the witnesses to the theft.</i> we shall accept them.” If the witnesses for slaughter turn out to be perjured, they pay because of the witnesses for theft<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Since without proof of theft, testimony of slaughter cannot be accepted in court; the witnesses to the slaughter have to pay double or triple restitution only because they caused the testimony to the theft to be accepted.</i>. Rebbi Ḥizqiah does not say so but: So far if the witnesses for theft and those for slaughter came together. If witnesses for slaughter appeared but were not accepted<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">They cannot be heard as long as there were no witnesses to the theft.</i> and after that came witnesses for theft. One tells them “witnesses for slaughter already had appeared but were not accepted, but on the strength of your testimony we shall accept them.” If the witnesses for theft turn out to be perjured<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In that case the testimony about slaughter is “testimony of the wicked” which has to be thrown out of court (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 23:1" href="/Exodus.23.1"><i>Ex</i>. 23:1</a>). But since the witnesses to the theft caused the testimony about slaughter to be accepted, they have to pay the entire quadruple or quintuple restitution.</i>, they pay because of the witnesses for slaughter.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person convicted of theft on the testimony of two witnesses, who slaughtered or sold by the testimony of a single witness or his own confession, pays double restitution but does not have to pay quadruple or quintuple<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Since the testimony of a single witness is not admissible in a criminal procedure, he cannot be convicted of slaughtering or selling and does not have to pay the fine. Similarly, since a person is always related to himself and relatives are barred from being witnesses in criminal proceedings, his confession is inadmissible and he never pays a fine on his own admission. In civil procedure one has to pay on the basis of his admission.</i>.<br>One who stole and slaughtered on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Stealing animals on the Sabbath is forbidden as on weekdays and incurs the same penalty. But slaughtering on the Sabbath is a capital crime which cannot be punished by an additional fine (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.2.1">Note 22</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.2-19"><i>Ketubot</i> 3:1</a>, <i>Terumot</i> 7:1).</i>, or stole and slaughtered for idolatry<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Pagan worship is a capital crime; a fine can be imposed for the theft but not for the slaughter.</i>, or stole from his father and after his father’s death slaughtered or sold<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Since he is a co-heir, he slaughtered or sold what is partially his own and cannot be sanctioned for the act.</i>, or stole, dedicated it<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">As a sacrifice in the Temple. Temple property is not under the rules of civil law; illicit use is subject to the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:14-16" href="/Leviticus.5.14-16"><i>Lev</i>. 5:14–16</a>, imposing a payment of 125% of the value taken.</i>, and afterwards slaughtered or sold, pays double restistution but not quadruple or quintuple. Rebbi Simeon says, for <i>sancta</i> for which he is liable by a warranty<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">If the thief made a vow in which he obligated himself for a certain sacrifice and then dedicated the stolen animal for that sacrifice, the animal still was in his possession since if it died or was lost he would be required to provide a replacement. But if he simplydedicated the stolen animal as a sacrifice, implying that he would not be obligated to provide a replacement if it were lost or died, the animal would become Heaven’s possession and its slaughter or sale would not trigger the obligation of quadruple or quintuple restitution.</i> he has to pay quadruple or quintuple, but if there is no warranty, he is not liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who stole and slaughtered on the Sabbath,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that slaughter which does not prepare meat for human consumption technically is called “killing” rather than “slaughter” and is not covered by the rule of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:37" href="/Exodus.21.37"><i>Ex</i>. 21:37</a>.</i> Did not Rabbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he is intentional error about fat but in error about a sacrifice, he can be warned, is whipped, and brings a sacrifice. Who stated it<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The statement in the Mishnah that the thief does not pay multiple restitution for slaughter or sale after dedication.</i>? [Rebbi Simeon, as we have stated]<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Added from E supported by a Genizah fragment.</i>: “Rebbi Simeon says, for <i>sancta</i> for which he is liable by a warranty he has to pay quadruple or quintuple<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Since these are not Heaven’s property.</i>, but if there is no warranty, he is not liable.”<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The argument is rejected in the Babli which holds that R. Simeon does not at all address the problem of the Mishnah (76a–78a).</i>
Rebbi Justus ben Rebbi Matton asked: If somebody redeems a string of dry figs from the Temple treasurer, does it become <i>ṭevel</i> for tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Produce becomes obligated for tithes only at the very end of harvest. A profane sale triggers the obligation of tithes for previously exempt produce (<i>Ma‘serot</i> 4:1, Note 7.)</i>? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked: Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah.</i> about him who “stole, dedicated it, and afterwards slaughtered or sold”? As you say there, the one who dedicates is not like a seller<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">If dedication were the equivalent of a sale, the thief who dedicated the stolen animal should immediately be obligated for quadruple or quintuple restitution by the act of dedication alone. Since dedication is not a sale, redemption cannot be a buy.</i>, so one who redeems is not like a buyer. The word of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish implies that selling is like redeeming. They said, he has the choice of redeeming and eating it<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon ben Laqish’s argument is rejected: Dedication is not a sale because the person dedicating retains the right of first refusal (<i>Ma’serot</i> 1:1 Note 23). R. Justus’s problem is not resolved.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar said: If they saw him slaughtering a black ox in a forest, it is <i>prima</i> <i>facie</i> evidence that it was stolen<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The animal was slaughtered at a place where it was not easily seen and where usually cattle are not found and slaughter does not happen. Then the court can accept testimony about the slaughter to be used later if the police can find witnesses to the theft.</i>. Could you not assume that it was lying down<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since cattle periodically lie down to ruminate, could the ox not have chosen a shady place for his convenience?</i>? Explain it if they saw him dragging it<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Viz., the animal into the forest. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 69b" href="/Bava_Kamma.69b">Babli, 69b</a>, points out that it is necessary for the thief to be seen dragging the animal to establish the theft. A person who slaughters a ruminating ox without moving the animal first did not steal before slaughtering and can be sued only for simple restitution.</i>.
There is a rule about the slaughterer which does not apply to the seller and to the seller which does not apply to the slaughterer, for he who slaughters on the Sabbath is criminally liable but he who sells on the Sabbath cannot be prosecuted<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The seller violates the positive commandment to rest on the Sabbath, but he did not violate any prohibition. Criminal prosecution is possible only for violation of a prohibition. The Mishnah is correct in only mentioning slaughter but not sale in connection with the Sabbath.</i>. Rebbi Yannai ben Rebbi Ismael said, we find a case of sale for which one is criminally liable on the Sabbath. Think of it, if somebody said to another: tear something off from this ground<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Which is an act of harvesting, forbidden in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 34:21" href="/Exodus.34.21"><i>Ex</i>. 34:21</a>.</i> to acquire, did he not acquire? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, the tearing makes him liable, not the sale<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 70b" href="/Bava_Kamma.70b">Babli disagrees, 70b</a>, and holds that no quadruple or quintuple restitution is due if the sale is simultaneous with and conditioned on a prohibited action.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who sold it except for a one percent interest in it<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">If he did not sell the animal outright, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:37" href="/Exodus.21.37"><i>Ex</i>. 21:37</a> is not applicable. As <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Simeon</i> <i>ben</i> <i>Ioḥai</i> (p. 191) explains, slaughter and sale are treated on an equal footing in the verse. Since slaughter is all or nothing, a sale must be all or nothing.</i>, or had a partnership interest in it<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">It was not completely stolen; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.4.1">Note 54</a>.</i>, one who slaughtered and it became a carcass in his hand<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">If the carcass is unfit for Jewish consumption, he killed the animal but did not slaughter it; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:37" href="/Exodus.21.37"><i>Ex</i>. 21:37</a> is not applicable.</i>, or one who stabs or tears out<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">He kills the animal by tearing out some vital organ; the carcass is not fit for Jewish consumption.</i>, pays double restitution but not quadruple or quintuple.<br>He who stole on the owner’s property but slaughtered or sold off their property<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">By moving the animal off the property he took possession. This makes him a thief and subject to the law of quadruple or quintuple restitution.</i>, or stole off their property and slaughtered or sold on their property, or stole and slaughtered or sold off their property has to pay quadruple or quintuple restitution. But if he stole and slaughtered or sold on their property<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If he never took possession, he cannot be called a thief and cannot be prosecuted under the terms of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:37" href="/Exodus.21.37"><i>Ex</i>. 21:37</a>.</i>, he is not liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who sold it except for a one percent interest in it,” etc. “He who stole on the owner’s property,” etc. Rebbi Ze‘ira asked: It he sold it in halves, what is the rule<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">He sold the entire animal to one person but in two transactions.</i>? If he sold half of it to one person and the other half to another, what is the rule? Let us hear from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 7:18" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).7.18">Tosephta 7:18</a>; essentially the same text is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 78b" href="/Bava_Kamma.78b">Babli, 78b</a>, only except that the Babli in the statements of the anonymous Tanna and of Rebbi for “he pays” has “he does not pay” and vice-versa (similarly in <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Simeon</i> <i>ben</i> <i>Ioḥai</i> p. 191). The Babli text conforms to the Mishnah; the Yerushalmi-Tosephta text contradicts the Mishnah and defies rational explanation. Since the text is confirmed by all three Yerushalmi and both Tosephta mss., it cannot be emended, as noted by Rashba, <i>Novellae</i> <i>ad</i> 78b.</i>: “If he sold it except for its forefoot, except for its hindfoot, except for its horn, except for its shearings<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sheep could be sold except for its wool and then the seller could shear it before delivery, the sheep was essentially sold completely in two transactions. Therefore, the answer to R. Ze‘ira’s question depends entirely onwhether in this case one reads “he pays” or “he does not pay”.</i>, he pays quadruple or quintuple restitution. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">In all other sources: R. Simeon ben Eleazar.</i> says, except for its horn he pays double restitution, except for its shearings he pays quadruple or quintuple restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees with the Mishnah but holds that the wool is external to the sheep, not part of its intrinsic body.</i>. Rebbi says, except for a vital organ he pays quadruple or quintuple restitution; except for a non-vital organ he is not liable.”

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he was drawing it to lead it out but it died on the owner’s property, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Drawing an animal by its halter or leading it on the owner’s property is not an act of acquisition. If the animal died before it left the owner’s property it was not stolen and no restitution is due.</i>. If he lifted it<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">If the thief lifts the animal (e. g., a sheep or a goat) on the owner’s property with the intent of stealing it, he acquired it and as a thief becomes liable for double restitution.</i> or had led it outside the owner’s property when it died, he is liable.<br>If he gave it for his first-born son<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In lieu of paying 5 <i>šeqalim</i> to a Cohen as redemption money, he tells the Cohen to take a sheep from such and such a flock, implying that the flock was his. If the Cohen takes the animal in good faith, the person who tricked him into taking it becomes the thief. The same holds for the other cases mentioned in the Mishnah.</i>, to his creditor, to an unpaid guardian or a borrower, to a paid guardian or a renter, and that person was drawing it to lead it out but it died on the owner’s property, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since there was no theft even if one was intended.</i>. If he<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">The intended recipient.</i> lifted it or had led it outside the owner’s property when it died, he<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">The instigator.</i> is liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he was drawing it to lead it out but it died,” etc.; “If he gave it for his first-born son,” etc. Rebbi Mana said, if somebody would explain to me the following, I would carry his בנרייה<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The word and its meaning are unclear. In E, the reading is בנרייתיה, the Genizah text reads either בנליתא or בולנייתה or ברלייתה. None of these words has a parallel in Talmudic literature.</i>: What means “is not liable”? Is he not liable for his obligation or not liable for theft<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.6.1">Mishnah 9</a>, what means “is not liable”? If the Cohen or the creditor accepted the assignment but then the animal died in the assignee’s hand before a theft happened, are they barred from asking the person who tricked them for payment or is the explanation as given in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.6.1">Note 85</a>, that no theft did occur? The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 80a" href="/Bava_Kamma.80a">Babli, 80a</a>, only considers the second alternative.</i>?

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not raise sheep and goats in the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Since they eat anything they see, if raised near agricultural land they would eat any crop and any sapling and their owner would by necessity become a robber, robbing farmers of their crops.</i> but one raises them in Syria<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Which has plenty of empty spaces.</i> and in the non-arable tracts<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">מִדְבָּר, from Aramaic דבר “to lead”, is a place where sheep are lead to graze because it is not agricultural land. For such a “desert” in a non-desert region cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 37:22" href="/Genesis.37.22"><i>Gen</i>. 37:22</a>; the word is also used for the grasses which sheep find in the prairie, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 3:1" href="/Exodus.3.1">Targum Onqelos to <i>Ex</i>. 3:1</a>.</i> in the Land of Israel. One does not raise chickens in Jerusalem because of the sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Chickens were always kept in the open. They dig up all kinds of things; in Jerusalem they might dig up the remains of a human bone. This is incompatible with the duty to eat “simple <i>sancta</i>” all over the walled city of Jerusalem.</i>, nor may Cohanim in the Land of Israel do so because of purities<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">They have to be pure in order to eat heave; chickens would present too great a risk.</i>. A Jew may not raise pigs, and a person should not raise a dog unless it is tied by a chain. One does not set traps for pigeons unless thirty stadia<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>parasang</i> (5523m). Since a <i>parasang</i> in the Talmudim is usually identified with 4 <i>mil</i>, this would make the <i>mil</i> 1281m and the underlying cubit 64cm. It is assumed that at this distance no pigeons which are private property can be found.</i> distant from any settlement.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not raise sheep and goats in the Land of Israel,” etc. Rebbi Abba said, for example Mahir which is sixteen by sixteen <i>mil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 5:3:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.5.3.2-9"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 5:3</a> ‘<i>Avodah</i> <i>zarah</i> 1:6; <i>Eccl</i>. <i>rabba</i> 1(34), v. 1:13. The place has not been identified; in <i>Eccl</i>. <i>r</i>. it seems to indicate a place in Transjordan of 16 square parasangs without agriculture.</i>.
It was stated: “One does not raise chickens in Jerusalem because of the sacrifices, nor may Cohanim in the Land of Israel because of purities.” I could think also in Jerusalem in this way<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">That people eating sacrifices may not raise chickens but all others may.</i>, but it might follow what Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, “Jerusalem the built-up<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 122:3" href="/Psalms.122.3"><i>Ps</i>. 122:3</a>. The argument is about the second half of the verse which is not quoted: “A city joined together.” All its inhabitants are joined in its holiness.<br>In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 3:6:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.3.6.2-3"><i>Ḥagigah</i> 3:6</a>, The same remark by R. Joshua ben Levi’s is given quite a different interpretation.</i>” etc., the city which joins Israel one to the other.
<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">This is from <i>Ševi’it</i> 7:4, Notes 75–76 (‘<i>Orlah</i> 3:1, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:1:2-16" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.1.2-16"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 2:1</a>).</i> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:28" href="/Leviticus.11.28">Lev. 11:28</a>.</i>: “They are impure for you”. Why does it say<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:35" href="/Leviticus.11.35">Lev. 11:35</a>.</i> “they shall be impure for you”? One is for the prohibition of eating, the other for the prohibition of usufruct. Anything forbidden [by the Torah is forbidden for trade, but everything whose prohibition]<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Text missing in the Leiden ms.; (the scribe skipped from איסורו to איסורו), taken from E, supported by the Genizah text and the parallels.</i> is rabbinical is permitted for trade. But is there not the donkey? It is raised for work. Is there not the camel? It is raised for work.
“A person should not raise a dog unless it is tied by a chain.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina said: About anybody who raises a biting dog the verse says: “He who refuses kindness to his neighbor,<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 6:14" href="/Job.6.14"><i>Job</i> 6:14</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 63" href="/Shabbat.63">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 63a/b</a>. As Eliahu Fulda explains, a biting dog will frighten away the poor who cannot come and ask for alms.</i>” etc.
“One does not set traps for pigeons unless distant thirty stadia from any settlement.” [Here, you say thirty stadia.]<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Text missing in the Leiden ms.; added from E supported by the Genizah text.</i> But there<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 2:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.2.5.1">Mishnah <i>Bava</i> <i>batra</i> 2:5</a>: One may not have a dove-cot within 50 cubits of agricultural land, to prevent the pigeons from eating the farmer’s seeds.</i>, you say 50 cubits. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, they feed within fifty cubits<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 83a" href="/Bava_Kamma.83a">Babli 83a</a> (in the name of Abbai).</i>; they fly even four <i>mil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>parasang</i> (5523m). Since a <i>parasang</i> in the Talmudim is usually identified with 4 <i>mil</i>, this would make the <i>mil</i> 1281m and the underlying cubit 64cm. It is assumed that at this distance no pigeons which are private property can be found.</i>.

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who injures another person owes him under five categories: Damages, suffering, medical costs, loss of earnings<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Payment of medical costs and replacement of lost earnings are biblical obligations, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:19" href="/Exodus.21.19"><i>Ex</i>. 21:19</a>.</i>, and embarrassment. How are damages determined? If he blinded his eye, or cut off his hand, or broke his leg, one considers it as if he were sold as a slave; how much was he worth and how much is he worth now<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Since slaves are bought for their working power (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:1:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.1.2-4">Halakhah 7:1</a>), what has to be estimated is the loss of earning power.</i>. Suffering: If he burned him with a spit, or with a nail, even on his fingernails where it does not cause a wound<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">If no open wound is caused, no damages are due. The payment for pain is independent of the payment for damages.</i>, one estimates how much a person in his state would ask to be paid in order to be willing to suffer such pain.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who injures another person,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 9:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).9.1">Tosephta 9:1</a>.</i>: “One who injures another person in five [respects] pays him for five categories, in four he pays him four, in three he pays him three, in two he pays him two, in one he pays him one.” How? If he hit him on his hand and severed it, he pays him five times: Damages, suffering, medical costs, loss of earnings, and embarassment. If he hit him on his hand and it became swollen<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Since the damage is not permanent, no damages are due.</i>, he pays four times: Suffering, medical costs, loss of earnings, and embarassment. If he hit him on his head which became swollen, he pays three times, suffering, medical costs, loss of earnings<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Maimonides (<i>Hilkhot</i> <i>Hovel</i> <i>umazziq</i> 2:2) and Rosh (Chapter 8, §1) read: embarrassment. This is reasonable since a person with a swollen head is embarrassed but not disabled. But the Genizah text supports the Leiden reading; the error, if it is one, must be a very old one. E hs a lacuna at this place.</i>. At a place where it is not visible he pays twice, suffering and medical costs<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This supports the reading of Maimonides and Rosh.</i>. With a tome<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>tomus</i> “cut”, Greek τόμος “piece, slice”. There is no injury or hurt; therefore payment is only due for embarassment. E reads בניומוס, a scribal error. A differently formulated parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 20 4" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_20.4"><i>Sifra</i> <i>Emor</i> <i>Pereq</i> 20(4</a>) reads בְּלוּחַ וּבְפִינַקְס וּבְטוֹמוֹס נְייָרוֹת “with a plank, or a wooden writing tablet (πίναξ), or a bundle of papers.”</i> in his hand he pays only once, for embarrassment.
Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If he severed his hand and returned and severed the arm, does he have to pay additionally for the pain inflicted the first time<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">If the case came to court only after the second attack. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 85b" href="/Bava_Kamma.85b">Babli, 85b</a>, it is considered obvious that suffering and embarrassment must be treated separately for each attack whereas the other payments can be combined.</i>?
It is written: “An eye for an eye, a tooth for a tooth<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:20" href="/Leviticus.24.20"><i>Lev</i>. 24:20</a>, speaking of payment for either animal or man.</i>.” At another place, He says: “Do not be softhearted.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:21" href="/Deuteronomy.19.21"><i>Deut</i>. 19:21</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:12" href="/Deuteronomy.25.12">25:12</a>.</i>” One refers to inadvertent acts, the other to intentional ones<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>. 293. The quote <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:21" href="/Deuteronomy.19.21"><i>Deut</i>. 19:21</a> refers to the punishment of the perjured witnesses. Since in injury cases the false accuser wanted to extort money and therefore would have to pay money, it is concluded that <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:12" href="/Deuteronomy.25.12"><i>Deut</i>. 25:12</a> which prescribes punishment for intentional injury also must mean payment of money. Therefore <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:20" href="/Leviticus.24.20"><i>Lev</i>. 24:20</a> refers to payment for unintentional injury.</i>. He could mention the inadvertent but not mention the intentional. But if the inadvertent had been mentioned but not the intentional, I would have said that for the inadvertent act he shall pay money, for the intentional [have his hand cut off. Therefore it was necessary to mention the intentional. Or if the intentional had been mentioned but not the inadvertent, I would have said that for the intentional act he shall pay money, for the inadvertent]<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Text missing in the Leiden ms., supplied from E and supported by the Genizah text.</i> he should not pay at all. Therefore it was necessary to mention both inadvertent and intentional. And if he blinded a blind man or cut off the arm of an amputee, how could one fulfill: “Do to him as he intended to do to his brother”<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 19:19" href="/Deuteronomy.19.19"><i>Deut</i>. 19:19</a>, referring to the punishment of perjured witnesses. The quote is confirmed by all three ms. sources but probably the reference should be to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:19" href="/Leviticus.23.19"><i>Lev</i>. 23:19</a>: “As a person injures another, so should be done to him”. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 84a" href="/Bava_Kamma.84a">Babli, 84a</a>, in quoting a similar <i>baraita</i> refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 24:22" href="/Leviticus.24.22"><i>Lev</i>. 24:22</a>: “A uniform law shall it be for you”; cf. also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Emor, Chapter 20 7" href="/Sifra,_Emor,_Chapter_20.7"><i>Sifra</i> <i>Emor</i> <i>Pereq</i> 20(7</a>).</i>? This indicates that he only pays money.
“Suffering: If he burned him with a spit, or with a nail, even on his fingernails where it does not cause a wound.” How is this<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Genizah and E texts do not have דמי; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 7:3:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.7.3.2">Chapter 7, Note 50</a>.</i>? If he wounded him so that he lost blood, it already is written: “A wound for a wound<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:25" href="/Exodus.21.25"><i>Ex</i>. 21:25</a>. Of the three expressions mentioned there, the third one, חַבּוּרָה, “contusion”, already seems to be subsumed under the first, כְּוִיָּה, “burn”, or the second, פצע, “open wound”. A similar discussion is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 84b" href="/Bava_Kamma.84b">Babli, 84b</a>.</i>.” Why does the verse say “a contusion”? To tell you that if he burned him with a spit on the palm of his hand and it swelled, on the sole of his foot and it swelled, or he put snow or ice on him at a place which is not visible, he has to pay his medical expenses.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">A somewhat different text is in <i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Ismael</i> <i>Neziqin</i> 8: “If he wounded him so that he lost blood, it already is written: ‘A wound for a wound’; if he caused a contusion, is there not written ‘a contusion for a contusion’? Why is written ‘a burn for a burn’? To tell you that if he burned him with a spit on the palm of his hand or on the sole of his foot and <i>it</i> <i>was</i> <i>not</i> <i>noticeable</i>, or he put snow on his head and gave him a cold, he has to pay for his <i>suffering</i>.”</i>
“One estimates how much a person in his state would ask to be paid in order to be willing to suffer such pain.” Rebbi Ze‘ira said, one sees a person and asks him, how much would you give that such pain would not be inflicted on you? And what he says, he has to give<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The court determines the payment for suffering by asking a randomly chosen member of the public. The amount indicated by that person is imposed on the attacker.</i>. Mar Uqba said, the Mishnah is “how much a person would ask to be paid,” and you say so? But one sees a person and asks him, how much would you ask that such pain not be inflicted on you? And what he says, he has to give<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mekhilta</i> <i>dR</i>. <i>Ismael</i> <i>Neziqin</i> 8 explains this as the meaning of payment “burn for burn”.</i>. Ben Azai said, one gives food invalidity<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 9:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).9.3">Tosephta 9:3</a>. The Erfurt text reads “the cost of food”, for the additional cost of a sick person’s diet.</i>. What is food invalidity? Before that, I was eating lentils and vegetables but now I can only eat eggs and chicken. What he asks one<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The court imposes the additional costs on the attacker.</i> gives him. Rebbi Yose ben Jacob said, a Roman who was sick would not go into battle until he could eat all he had been missing those days.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Medical costs. If he hit a person, he has to pay that person’s medical costs. In case a growth on that person’s body developed, if it was a consequence of the injury, he has to pay medical costs. If it started to heal but regressed several times, he has to pay medical costs. After it was healed completely he no longer has to pay medical costs.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Medical costs. If he hit a person, he has to pay that person’s medical costs,” etc. Rebbi Jeremiah asked: If there was a growth around the wound and complications ensued because of the wound, what is the rule<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Is the attacker liable for delayed consequences of his attack?</i>? Since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Payment of medical costs and replacement of lost earnings are biblical obligations, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:19" href="/Exodus.21.19"><i>Ex</i>. 21:19</a>.</i>: “And healing he shall heal,” if [the patient] did not follow the doctor’s instructions, he is [not liable]<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For continued medical care. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 85a" href="/Bava_Kamma.85a">Babli 85a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 9:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).9.4">Tosephta 9:4</a>.</i>. It was stated in another <i>baraita</i>: If [the wound] was covered with scab, he must pay for medical treatment. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For continued medical care. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 85a" href="/Bava_Kamma.85a">Babli 85a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 9:4" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).9.4">Tosephta 9:4</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel texts, this is R. Jehudah’s own opinion.</i> says, if there was a growth around the wound, even if complications ensued because of the wound, he must pay for medical treatment but he is not liable to pay for his loss of earnings. Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, this is quoted as “the Sages’ ” opinion. Whenever one is obligated to pay for medical treatment, he automatically is obligated to pay for lost earnings. This answers R. Jeremiah’s question in full.</i> says, in this paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Payment of medical costs and replacement of lost earnings are biblical obligations, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:19" href="/Exodus.21.19"><i>Ex</i>. 21:19</a>.</i> Scripture introduced a novelty stating that he has to pay for loss of earnings together with medical treatment: ‘Only his loss of earnings he shall compensate and healing he shall heal.’ ”

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Loss of earnings. One considers him as if he were a watchman of pumpkins since he already compensated him for the loss of his hand or loss of his foot<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Once long-term disability has been compensated for, short term inability to work has to be compensated on the level of his remaining long term earning power.</i>. Embarrassment. Everything depends on the person embarrassing and the one embarrassed<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The lower the social standing of the agressor, the more weighty is his insult. The higher the social standing of the victim, the more damaging is the insult.</i>. One who embarrassed a naked person<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Who is in an embarrassing situation.</i>, or embarrassed a blind person<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Who cannot see the reaction of others to his embarrassment.</i>, or embarrassed a sleeping person<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Who does not notice his embarrassment.</i> is liable, but the sleeper who embarrassed is not liable. If a person fell from a roof and caused damage and embarrassed, he is liable for the damage but not liable for the embarrassment since is was said<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:11" href="/Deuteronomy.25.11"><i>Deut</i>. 25:11</a>.</i>: “She reached out with her hand and grabbed him at his private parts;” one is not liable for embarrassment unless if was intended.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Loss of earnings. One considers him,” etc. Here you say, “one considers him as if he were a watchman of pumpkins.” But there, you say<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:11" href="/Deuteronomy.25.11"><i>Deut</i>. 25:11</a>.</i>, “if he was earning a mina per day, he pays him a mina; a tetradrachma, he gives him a tetradrachma.” Rebbi Isaac said, there are two kinds of inability to work. If he hit him on his hand and cut it off, one does not consider him as if he earned a mina a day but one considers him as if he were a lame person watching pumpkins, because he already compensated him for the loss of his hand or loss of his foot. But if he burned him on the palm of his hand and it swelled, on the flat of his head and it swelled, or he put on him snow or cold water at a place which is not visible, then he has to pay his medical expenses<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The last sentence is a quote from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.1.2.2">Halakhah 1 (Note 17</a>). According to the preceding Halakhah, the obligation of paying for medical care implies the obligation to pay for loss of temporary earnings.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is alluded to in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 28a" href="/Bava_Kamma.28a">Babli, 28a</a>, and <i>Sifry</i> <i>Deut</i>. #292. The verse <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 25:11" href="/Deuteronomy.25.11"><i>Deut</i>. 25:11</a> reads: “If men quarreled, a person with his brother, and the wife of one of them intervened to save her husband from the hand of one who hit him when she reached out with her hand and grabbed him at his private parts.” Grabbing a person’s private parts is given as an example of an action intended to embarrass. It is clear in the paragraph that “wife of x” stands for “x” himself. The court marshall meting out punishment is protected from a damage suit by the person suffering the punishment as long as he strictly follows the court’s instructions. He is not protected for actions taken on his own initiative.</i> It was stated: “She reached out with her hand,” but not the court marshal’s wife. Another Tanna stated, “she reached out with her hand,” including the court marshal’s wife. He who said, “she reached out with her hand,” but not the court marshal’s wife, if he whipped with permission. He who said, “she reached out with her hand,” including the court marshal’s wife, if he whipped without permission. It was stated: Not the wife of two men<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since a woman cannot have two husbands, one derives from the expression “the wife of one of them” that two people trading mutual insults have no case in court.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> This is more severe regarding a human than an ox since a human pays damages<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">And the other four categories of payments mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 8:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.8.1.1">Mishnah 1</a>. These are enumerated by the Mishnah in the Babli and most independent Mishnah mss.</i> and pays for unborn children<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:22" href="/Exodus.21.22"><i>Ex</i>. 21:22</a> prescribes that an attack on a woman which leads to a miscarriage but does not endanger the woman’s life entitles the woman’s husband to go to court and exact payment for the loss of prospective children.</i>, but the ox pays only damages and does not pay for unborn children.<br>A person who hits his father or mother without causing a concussion<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:15" href="/Exodus.21.15"><i>Ex</i>. 21:15</a> declares hitting father or mother to be a capital crime. Hitting one of the parents without causing visible damage is a sin but not a crime. Therefore there is no obstacle to pressing monetary claims. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 8:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.8.5.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i> or who causes injury on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Desecrating the Day of Atonement is a deadly sin but not a prosecutable crime; it is outside the purview of the human court. Injuring somebody on the Sabbath is a capital crime. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 8:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.8.5.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i> is liable for everything. He who injures a Hebrew slave<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Hebrew slavery was an institution permanently abolished, never resurrected in the Second Commonwealt; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.2.1"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:2, Note 150</a>. The argument is purely theoretical.</i> is liable for everything except for lost earnings if he is his own. He who injures another person’s Canaanite slave<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Any Gentile slave becoming potentially Jewish by circumcision and immersion in a <i>miqweh</i>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.1"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:3, Note 328</a>. A person severely injuring his own slave has to set him free (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:26-27" href="/Exodus.21.26-27"><i>Ex</i>. 21:26–27</a>).</i> is liable for everything. Rebbi Jehudah says, slaves have no claim for embarrassment.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “This is more severe regarding a human than an ox,” etc. “A person who hits his father or mother,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement is in <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Neziqin</i> 9.</i>: If the witnesses said, we testify that X blinded both of his eyes simultanously, or that he knocked out two of his teeth simultaneously, he does not have to pay anything.
