One after the other, he gains his freedom by the first and he pays him damages for the second<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:26-27" href="/Exodus.21.26-27"><i>Ex.</i> 21:26–27</a> states that a slave gains his freedom if his master blinds him or knocks out one of his teeth. If the master injures the slave repeatedly, the slave gains his freedom by the first injury and, therefore, can claim full payment for the second as a free Jew (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 42b" href="/Gittin.42b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 42b</a>). But if a double injury was inflicted in one blow, the slave was not free and has no claim beyond his automatic freedom.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This implies that one estimates embarrassment for slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since there is no exception made for shame in the previous statement.<br>The statement is difficult to understand since at the moment of the second injury the slave already is a free Jew rather than a slave. As R. Eliahu Fulda points out, it also is superfluous since R. Joḥanan always follows the anonymous Mishnah as practice. Since the Yerushalmi is so elliptic, it may not be excluded that it follows the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 42b" href="/Gittin.42b">Babli (<i>Giṭṭin</i> 42b</a>) in holding that the slave is automatically free only for eye and tooth which are mentioned in the verse, but for other injuries for which the court will force the slave’s freedom a bill of manumission would be required. Then R. Abbahu’s statement becomes relevant for the case of a slave in his period between servitude and freedom.</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">A text similar to the remainder of this paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:5:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.5.4"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5, Notes 120–130</a>.</i> Rebbi La in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If a person dedicates his earnings, he dedicates everything<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Everything he earns automatically is Temple property; he cannot take anything to feed himself but has to beg his sustenance from others. Tosephta <i>‘Arakhin</i> 3:8 does not support this statement.</i>. If he dedicates the earnings of his slave, he<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The slave can support himself from his own earnings; only the excess becomes Temple property; Tosephta <i>‘Arakhin</i> 3:8, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 12a" href="/Gittin.12a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 12a</a>.</i> can take from there his upkeep and the remainder is dedicated. Here you say, the remainder is dedicated, but there you say, everything is holy. Rebbi Aḥa said, Jews are more obligated to support free people than slaves<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Slaves would not be able to feed themselves from alms.</i>. But did not Rebbi Joḥanan say: If somebody cuts off the hands of somebody else’s slave, his master collects damages, suffering, medical costs, loss of earnings, and embarrassment, and that one should be supported by welfare<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 12b" href="/Gittin.12b">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i> 12b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa said, Jews are more obligated to support amputated slaves than unimpaired ones. But did not Rebbi Joḥanan also give to his slave when he ate meat? Give also to his slave when he drank wine? And recited for himself the verse<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 31:15" href="/Job.31.15"><i>Job</i> 31:15</a>.</i>: “Did not His maker make me in the womb”? They said, there a rule of law, here a rule of mercy.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> It is bad to meet a deaf-mute, an insane, or an underage person<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">These are not prosecutable and cannot be sued. Even if they were healed or grew up they cannot be sued for what they did when they were not responsible.</i>: Anybody who injures them is liable but they who cause injury are not liable. It is bad to meet a woman<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A married woman, any of whose private property is administered by her husband who has the usufruct and therefore can block any payment which would diminish his income. A married woman whose husband had waved his right of administration and usufruct before marriage is counted as a male for the rules of this Mishnah.</i> or a slave<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Any whose property is his master’s. This rule was contested by the Sadducees, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Yadayim 4:7" href="/Mishnah_Yadayim.4.7">Mishnah <i>Yadayim</i> 4:7</a>.</i>: Anybody who injures them is liable but they who cause injury are not liable, but they might have to pay later. If the woman was divorced or the slave manumitted, they have to pay.<br>A person who hits his father or mother causing a concussion<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:15" href="/Exodus.21.15"><i>Ex</i>. 21:15</a> declares hitting father or mother to be a capital crime. Hitting one of the parents without causing visible damage is a sin but not a crime. Therefore there is no obstacle to pressing monetary claims. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 8:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.8.5.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i> or who causes injury to a fellow man on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Desecrating the Day of Atonement is a deadly sin but not a prosecutable crime; it is outside the purview of the human court. Injuring somebody on the Sabbath is a capital crime. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 8:5:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.8.5.1">Mishnah 7</a>.</i> is not liable for anything since he is tried for his life<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he cannot be tried because of lack of eyewitnesses or other reasons; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.1.4"><i>Terumot</i> 7:1, Note 16</a>.</i>. One who injures his own Canaanite slave is not liable for anything.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “It is bad to meet a deaf-mute, an insane, or an underage person,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Makkot 1:3" href="/Tosefta_Makkot.1.3">Tosephta <i>Makkot</i> 1:4</a>.</i>: If the witnesses said, we testify that X blinded the eye of his slave and broke his tooth, for that is what the master said, and they are found to be perjured, they pay to the slave<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The master in fact blinded the slave and broke his tooth. Since the payment for the loss of an eye is much larger than that for the loss of a tooth, it is to the advantage of the master to free his slave for the eye and to pay only for the tooth. If the witnesses are found to be perjured, they have to pay the difference of the payments for eye and tooth to the slave, irrespective of what happened in reality.</i>. How can they say this about the case<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">How can such a case really occur? The same question can be asked about the case treated in the preceding Halakhah since even if it looked as if both eyes were blinded simultaneously, it might have been that one was injured a fraction of a second before the other.</i>? Rebbi Nasa said, those might say the following: We testify about X that [the slave] came to his hand unimpaired and left with two injuries<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This is unproblematic testimony.</i>. Then he gains his freedom with the first and he has to pay him for the second<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Since the burden of proof is on the claimant, the slave becomes free on the injury to the eye and receives payment for the injury to the tooth unless he can prove by witnesses that his tooth was knocked out first.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who hits another<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The meaning of this sentence is not clear. <i>‘Arukh</i>: He blows his horn into the victim’s ear. Rashi quotes the <i>Arukh</i> and adds his teacher’s explanation: He hit him on his ear. Maimonides in his Mishnah Commentary: He hit him with his fist. In his Code (חובל ומזיק 9:3): He hit him with his flat hand.</i> pays him a tetradrachma<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The word סלע used in both Talmudim for tetradrachma is not the Hebrew “rock” but the Sumerian <i>silà</i> used in Accadic as the unit of volume (1/300 of a <i>gur</i>, כּוֹר).</i>; Rebbi Jehudah says in the name of Rebbi Yose the Galilean, a <i>mina</i><sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">100 <i>denarii.</i></i>. If he slapped him, he pays 200 <i>zuz</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Accadic <i>zūzum,</i> from זוז “to split”, the half-šeqel piece, identified with the Roman <i>denarius.</i> The possession of 200 <i>zuz</i> disqualifies its holder from receiving public assistance (Mishnah <i>Peah</i> 8:8); it is a considerable sum.</i>, with the back of his hand he gives him 400 <i>zuz.</i> If he hurt him on his earlobe, tore out some of his hair, spat on him and the spit touched him, removed his stole, uncovered a woman’s hair<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">A married woman will indicate her status by having her hair covered in public. In the Babli and some Mishnah mss. it is stated explicitly “uncovered a woman’s hair in public”.</i>, he pays 400 zuz; everything corresponding to his honor<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The sums stated in the Mishnah are maxima, applicable to the highest levels of society; the court may lower them in appropriate cases.</i>.<br>Rebbi Aqiba said, even the poorest in Israel are treated as aristocrats who lost their property<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees with the last statement of the preceding Mishnah; the sums mentioned are mandatory in all cases.</i>, for they are descendents of Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob. In a case where somebody uncovered a woman’s hair, she came before Rebbi Aqiba who obligated him to pay her 400 <i>zuz</i>. He said to him, Rabbi, give me some time, which he did. He watched her at the entrance of her courtyard and broke a flask before her which contained an <i>as</i> worth of oil<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">A very small amount.</i>. She uncovered her hair, splashed her hand with the oil and worked it onto her head. He had her observed by witnesses and came before Rebbi Aqiba, telling him, to such a one<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Who uncovers herself for a very small gain.</i> I have to give 400 <i>zuz</i>? He told him, you did not say anything since a person who injures himself, while he is not allowed to do that<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:5" href="/Genesis.9.5"><i>Gen.</i> 9:5</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:15" href="/Deuteronomy.4.15"><i>Deut.</i> 4:15</a>.</i>, is not liable, but others who injure him are liable; a person who cuts down his fruit trees, while he is not allowed to do that<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:19" href="/Deuteronomy.20.19"><i>Deut.</i> 20:19</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sheviit 4:8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sheviit.4.8.1"><i>Ševi‘it</i> 4:10, Notes 109–110</a>.</i>, is not liable, but others who cut down his fruit trees are.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who hits another pays him a tetradrachma.” “Rebbi Aqiba said, even the poorest in Israel,” etc. Rav Qarni stated: For a kick, one. For an elbow, three. For <i>sqlvnqyt</i> fifteen<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 27b" href="/Bava_Kamma.27b">Babli, 27b</a>, the quote appears in Rav Ḥisda’s name. There, pushing with the elbow cost 3 tetradrachmas, a kick with the foot 5, and סנוקרת 13. The unexplained סנוקרת (which also is the reading of E here) according to R. Ḥananel (i. e., Gaonic tradition) means a hook with the fist under the chin; according to Rashi hitting somebody with a donkey saddle. The Genizah text reads יסקינוקינית, the <i>Metivot</i> fragment סקלוקינת. J. N. Epstein (ללקסיקון התלמודי 2/1 תרביץ p. 123–127) proposes Accadic <i>sunkirtu</i> (<i>su-un-kir-tum</i>) “camel’s hump” as origin. {Cf. Latin <i>sculpturigo</i> “scratching”, <i>sculptorium</i> “back scratcher” (E. G.).}</i>. Somebody said in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: He who puts an Elder to shame has fully to pay for his shame. A person insulted Rebbi Jehudah ben Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The case is mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 4:8:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.4.8.6"><i>Ketubot</i> 4:8, Note 202</a>.</i>. The case came before Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish who fined him a pound of gold.
“If one spat on him and the spit touched him.” How far does the spit have to reach? On his body, on his garments?<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">The question is not answered. One may conclude that there is no difference. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 91a" href="/Bava_Kamma.91a">Babli, 91a</a>, comes to the opposite conclusion, <i>viz.,</i> that spitting on somebody’s garments is not punishable.</i> But what is the rule if he spat and the spittle did not reach him? Rebbi Yose said, this implies that somebody who insults another person verbally is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Since certainly spitting is not punishable if the spit falls to the ground before it reaches the intended victim, verbal spit which never touches cannot be punishable (in contrast to contemporary Roman or Egyptian laws.)</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu stated before Rebbi Joḥanan: “You killed my ox, you cut down my fruit trees,” and the other says, I do not know, he is liable. He said to him, certainly you state that to the rabble<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">As J. Levy notes, the unexplained words מקרילות (Genizah מקבילות ,(מקליקות are onomatopoetic and intended as an insult. The Babli in such a situation uses חִילֵּק וּבִילֵּק “split and desolation” to designate the totally unworthy. R. Abbahu’s statement is not worth to be heard since nobody can be responsible for an undocumented claim about which he notes his own ignorance.</i>. He answered, it is my fault, they said the following: “We testify against X that he killed ox Y” and the other says, I do not know, he is liable. [He said, that was stated.]<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Not in the Leiden ms., added from the E and Genizah texts. R. Joḥanan notes that the statement in its present form is obviously true, hence unnecessary.<br>The Genizah text has a better version in R. Abbahu’s statement: “We testify against X that he killed Y’s ox or cut down his fruit trees.”</i>

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Even though he did pay, he will not be forgiven<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The person who injures another cannot obtain mercy in Heaven unless he first has asked forgiveness on earth.</i> unless he asks for forgiveness, as it is said: “Now return the man’s wife since he is a prophet<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 20:7" href="/Genesis.20.7"><i>Gen.</i> 20:7</a>. The reference as usual is to the part of the verse which is not quoted: “Let him pray for you that you will live.” This implies that Abraham first had to forgive Avimelekh before the latter obtained Heavenly mercy.</i>.” From where that the person asked to forgive shall not be cruel? It is said<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 20:17" href="/Genesis.20.17"><i>Gen.</i> 20:17</a>.</i>: “Abraham prayed to the Omnipotent, etc.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Even though he did pay,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">Paragraph missing in the Leiden ms., added from the Genizah text. E’s text is somewhat longer.</i>[And so you find with Job’s friends. <sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 42:8-10" href="/Job.42.8-10"><i>Job</i> 42:8–10</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 9:29" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).9.29">Tosephta 9:29</a>.</i>“Now yourselves take seven bulls and seven rams, etc.” “Eliphas the Temanite, Bildad the Shuhite, and Ṣophar the Naamite went and did, etc.” What does it say at the end? “The Eternal changed Job’s fortunes.” When? “When he was praying for his neighbor, etc.” “The Eternal doubled everything that Job had had.”]
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 9:30" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).9.30">Tosephta 9:30</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 151b" href="/Shabbat.151b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>151b</a>.</i>: Rebbi Jehudah says in the name of Rabban Gamliel. Since it says that<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 13:18" href="/Deuteronomy.13.18"><i>Deut.</i> 13:18</a>.</i> “He will give you mercy etc.”, let the following be a sign in your hand: Whenever that you show mercy, the Omnipresent will have mercy on you. If you do not show mercy, the Omnipresent will not have mercy on you. <sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 8:7:6-10" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.8.7.6-10"><i>Yoma</i> 8:9</a> (45c), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 87b" href="/Bava_Kamma.87b">Babli 87b</a>.</i> Rav said: If a person misbehaved towards another and asked for pardon but the other did not respond, let him assemble a row of people and ask for pardon, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 33:27" href="/Job.33.27"><i>Job</i> 33:27</a>.</i>: “Form a row of men, etc.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">“Let him say, I sinned, I did evil without caring.”</i>” If he does so, what is written there? <sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 33:28" href="/Job.33.28"><i>Job</i> 33:28</a>.</i>“He redeemed his soul from the pit, not to go to destruction etc.” Rebbi Yose said, that is if he did not defame, but the defamer is never pardoned.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one says: Blind my eye, cut off my hand, break my leg, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to injure another person (except for medical treatment). Since “there is no agency for committing crimes”, a person committing a crime on the instigation of another is not absolved from liability.</i>. On condition not to be liable<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the instigator/victim agrees beforehand that no liability would be involved.</i>, he is liable. Tear my garment, break my pitcher, he is liable. On condition not to be liable, he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is purely a money matter.</i>. Do that to another person, on condition not to be liable, he is liable whether about his person or his money<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to injure another person (except for medical treatment). Since “there is no agency for committing crimes”, a person committing a crime on the instigation of another is not absolved from liability.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one say: Blind my eye, cut off my hand,” etc. “If he says, blind my eye because it damages me, cut off my hand because it damages me, he is liable. On condition not to be liable, he is liable.”<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 9:32" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).9.32">Tosephta 9:32</a>: “If one says: blind my eye because it damages me, cut off my hand because it damages me, he is not liable.” This may be a Babylonian version.</i> Rebbi Eleazar said, the Mishnah deals with a “yes” which really means “no”. But for a “yes” which does mean “yes” he is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">If a person asks to be mutilated, it is obvious that this person cannot sue for damages. Then the question arises, what does the Mishnah mean by holding the person who does the mutilation liable? The relatives can sue because they have to bear the shame of having a mutilated member in their family. If the person doing the mutilation can show that the immunity granted him by the victim was honest, they cannot sue.</i>. The statement of Rebbi Eleazar is about the family’s loss of standing. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the Mishnah deals with a “yes” which means “yes”. But for a “yes” which means “no”, he is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Even for the willful destruction of property.</i>. The statement of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish imples that one estimates the relatives’ loss of standing<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">He gives the relatives the right to sue in any case. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 93a" href="/Bava_Kamma.93a">Babli, 93a</a>, R. Simeon ben Laqish’s position is attributed to his teacher R. Hoshaia, R. Eleazar’s position to his teacher R. Joḥanan. Since R. Simeon ben Laqish is a higher authority than R. Eleazar but R. Joḥanan is higher than R. Hoshaia, implicitly the two Talmudim decide in opposite ways.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 9:33" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).9.33">Tosephta 9:33</a>; quoted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 117a" href="/Bava_Kamma.117a">Babli 117a</a>.</i>: “If Gentiles forced a Jew and took from him another person’s money in his presence, he is not liable. If he handed it over, he is liable.” Rebbi Yose said, that means if they simply demanded “money”<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">One may use another person’s money to save his own life but he has to pay for it.</i>. But “X’s money”, he is not liable even if he handed it over<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:4:11" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.4.11"><i>Terumot</i> 8:10, Note 248</a>.</i>.

Chapter 9



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who robbed<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The robber, who takes things openly and by force, has to make restitution with possibly a 25% fine, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21-24" href="/Leviticus.5.21-24"><i>Lev.</i> 5:21–24</a>. The thief who takes things by stealth has to pay double.</i> wood and turned it into utensils, wool and turned it into garments<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Once the nature of the robbed item was changed, the robber became its owner and only owes the monetary value to the original owner.</i>, pays their value at the time of the robbery. If he robbed a pregnant cow and it calved, a sheep in its wool and he shore it<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">And now it is not in the state in which it was robbed.</i>, he pays the price of a pregnant cow ready to calve, or a sheep in its wool ready to be shorn. If he robbed a cow which became pregnant in his possession and it calved, or a sheep which grew wool with him and he shore it, he pays their value at the time of the robbery. This is the principle: All robbers pay for value at the time of the robbery.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who robbed wood and turned it into utensils,” etc. Here<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.2">Tosephta 10:2</a> states that raw wool that was bleached does not have to be returned; only its value as raw wool has to be paid. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 93b" href="/Bava_Kamma.93b">Babli 93b</a>.</i>, you say that bleaching is a change, and there<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah, which says that only turning wool into a garment is a change by which the robber becomes the owner; this implies that cleaning, spinning, and dying the wool prior to weaving is not a change which absolves the robber from returning the wool as is.</i> you say that bleaching is not a change. If you say that bleaching is a change, if he made it into wool flakes<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Arabic صوفة, Samaritan Aramaic צוף. The wool flakes can be used to stuff pillows; they cannot be spun into thread. The robber changed raw material into a finished product.</i>. If you say that bleaching is not a change, if he left it unchanged. We find bleaching without flakes. Are there flakes without bleaching<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">At least the raw wool has to be washed before being turned into flakes.</i>? Rebbi Yudan said, it is a leniency instituted for the robber that he pay their value at the time of the robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Since robbers, in contrast to thieves, are known, it should be made easy for them to return the robbed goods and avoid prosecution. This is the position of the House of Hillel as explained in the next paragraph. The Mishnah represents the teaching of the House of Hillel.</i>. If somebody robbed raw hide and cleaned it, can you say that he turned it into flakes<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Nonetheless, he only owes the value of the hide and cannot be forced to return it as is.</i>? Again it is a leniency instituted for the robber that he pay the value at the time of the robbery.
<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:5-7" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.5-7">Tosephta 10:5–7</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Gittin 55a" href="/Gittin.55a">Babli <i>Giṭṭin</i>55a</a>. A different version of the entire paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 5:6:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.5.6.5"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 5:5 (Notes 158–168</a>).</i>“One who robbed a beam and built it into a large building. The House of Shammai say, he has to tear down the entire building and return it. But the House of Hillel say, he has to pay its worth to support the penitents. If he went into another person’s ruined building and rebuilt it without permission, one estimates for him and his hand is disadvantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">He only can expect the lesser of his expenses or the value created.</i>. If he rebuilt it with permission, one estimates for him and his hand is advantaged<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The Tosephta explains that he is entitled to the larger of his expenses or the value created.</i>. If he wanted to take his wood and stones, one does not listen to him.” Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Because of the settlement in the Land of Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, the rule only applies to the Land of Israel. In the version in <i>Giṭṭin</i> the reason is “because of civilization”, that one does not wilfully destroy houses, applicable worldwide.</i>. Rav Naḥman bar Isaac<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">In E, the Genizah fragment, and in <i>Giṭṭin:</i> Naḥman bar Jacob. This reading is also confirmed by the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 101a" href="/Bava_Metzia.101a">Babli, <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 101a</a>. Since otherwise only Babylonian authorities of the first two generations are mentioned in <i>Neziqin,</i> L’s reading is a scribal error.</i> asked: If somebody temporarily entered another person’s ruin to put down his bundle, when he wanted to take it up again does one not listen to him<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Then why can the illegal builder not take his logs with him? In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 101a" href="/Bava_Metzia.101a">Babli, <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 101a</a>, Rav Naḥman is quoted as letting the illegal builder take his materials instead of accepting the estimated value.</i>? When he heard what Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa had said, because of the settlement in the Land of Israel, he said: That is a statement<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A valid statement.</i>. But if he built<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Without permission.</i> at a place unfit for building, or planted trees at a place unfit for planting, Rav said one estimates<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In E: “One listens”.</i> for him<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">He only can expect the lesser of his expenses or the value created.</i> but one tricks him to permit entry both in summer and in winter<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">“Him” refers to the legal owner of the land. It is explained in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 101a" href="/Bava_Metzia.101a">Babli, <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 101a</a>, that the person who planted is advantaged (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.1.3">Note 12</a>) if the owner restricts entrance to the newly planted area.</i>. One said to him: Was that not already stated: “One estimates for him and his hand is disadvantaged?”
Rebbi Ze‘ira said, this implies that if he robbed a fattened animal and it became thin<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">This is a natural change; it is not enough to make the robber an owner. But since the Mishnah states that he has to give back the value at the time of robbery, he has to make up for value lost. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 65a" href="/Bava_Kamma.65a">Babli 65a</a> for the case of a thief.</i>, he has to replace the fat. Rav Ḥisda said, this implies that if he robbed a thin animal and fattened it, he has to return it as is<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since the robber never became the owner (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.1.1">Note 2</a>).</i>.
Because it became pregnant at his place and calved he pays its value at the time of the robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">E has an addition: “Therefore, if it did not become pregnant at his place and calved he pays …” This addition is out of place.</i>. Who stated this? Rebbi Jacob, as it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">An anonymous statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:3" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.3">Tosephta 10:3</a>.</i>: “This is the principle about which Rebbi Jacob said: About any robbed object which exists as before and was not changed from the state in which it was created<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Even if now it is worthless as, e. g., leavened matter after Passover which was robbed before Passover. Since the deficiency is not visible, the robber can return the leavened matter to discharge his obligation while a thief has to pay double the value it had at the time of the theft.</i>, he tells him: Here is your property before you. And the thief always pays the value at the time of theft.”

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he robbed an animal and it aged, slaves and they aged<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">They do not look the same as at the time of the robbery and are not worth the same; the robber cannot return them but has to pay up.</i>, he has to pay their value at the time of the robbery. Rebbi Meīr says, he may say about slaves: Here is your property before you<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since slaves are treated according to the law of real estate (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Batra 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Batra.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Bava batra</i> 3:1</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.6"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:2 Notes 347</a> ff.) and real estate can never be acquired by a robber, for him a slave can never be acquired by a robber and ages as the original owner’s property.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he robbed an animal and it aged,” etc. Rav Huna said, if he robbed a calf and it became a bull<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">This is a change in the nature of the animal; he cannot return it but must pay.</i>; but if he robbed a bull and it aged, he tells him: Here is your property before you<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 96b" href="/Bava_Kamma.96b">Babli, 96b</a>, Rav Pappa disagrees and holds that the weakened animal cannot be returned.</i>. Samuel said, even if he robbed a calf and it became a bull, he tells him<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that it still is called cattle, not changed from the date of its birth.</i>: Here is your property before you. Rav Qarna stated: Even if he robbed<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">One has to read “stole” since robbers are not under the law of quadruple or quintuple restitution.</i> a calf and it became a bull before he slaughtered or sold it, he pays quadruple or quintuple the value of a calf<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">He follows Samuel that the bull is still the same animal as the calf. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 65b" href="/Bava_Kamma.65b">Babli, 65b</a>, R. Ilai (La) is quoted as following the Mishnah and Rav Huna that the change of the calf into a bull is an essential change which lets the thief become the owner, not only the possessor, of the animal. If he slaughtered or sold the animal after it reached sexual maturity, he slaughtered or sold his own property and only pays double restitution for the theft.</i>. The father of Samuel bar Immi said in the name of Rav Jehudah<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The student of Samuel. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 96b" href="/Bava_Kamma.96b">Babli, 96b</a>, the statement is in the name of Rav.</i>: Practice follows Rebbi Meïr.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he robbed a coin and it was split, fruits and they rotted, wine and it spoiled, he pays their value at the time of the robbery<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In all these cases, the thing robbed was changed materially; it cannot be returned.</i>. A coin and it was declared invalid, heave and it became impure<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">It lost its value as food and only can be used as fuel.</i>, leavened matter and Passover passed<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">It is rabbinically forbidden for all usufruct.</i>, an animal which was used for sin<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Either it was used for idolatry which makes it forbidden as sacrifice, or was used for bestiality which makes it subject to a death sentence.</i>, or which became disqualified from the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The living animal no longer can be offered as a sacrifice.</i>, or which was taken out to be stoned<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">After it killed a human.</i>, he tells him: Here is your property before you<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Since these defects are not visible, following R. Jacob the robbed objects can be returned as they are (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 98b" href="/Bava_Kamma.98b">Babli 98b</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he robbed a coin and it was split,” etc. Rav Huna said, if the government invalidated it. Samuel said, if really it was split<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Huna considers a coin invalidated by the government as a piece of metal, not a coin. Therefore its nature was changed and it is as if it was split. Samuel considers an invalidated coin still as a coin. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 97a" href="/Bava_Kamma.97a">Babli, 97a</a>, Rav Huna is quoted with the opinion here ascribed to Samuel, and Rav Jehudah, Samuel’s student, with that ascribed to Rav Huna.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah ben Pazi in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Only if they rotted because of maggots but if they rotted because of worms they are like healthy ones<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the rotten fruits mentioned in the Mishnah. If the fruits were damaged by larvae but now are free of insects they are usable, not lost or changed. Before the days of pesticides almost no fruits were completely free of any insect damage.</i>. Rav Huna said, if the government invalidated it, but if the country invalidated it<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">As in the military anarchy of the Roman empire when the silver-washed copper coins given out as silver were no longer used by the public which switched to the gold standard (cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, <i>Notes on the Talmudic Vocabulary</i> 8, Lešonēnū 37(1973) 105–112.)</i>, he may say to him: Here is your property before you. Samuel said, even if the government invalidated it, he may say to him: Here is your property before you.
Rebbi Yudan did not go to the house of assembly. He met Rebbi Mana and asked him, what was new for you in the house of study today? He told him, such and such a subject. He replied, is that not a Mishnah, “heave and it became impure”? He replied, explain it if it became impure by itself<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">For example, if a dead reptile was found in the utensil containing the heave. Then it implies nothing about the status of the object if the robber caused the change.</i> and you cannot infer anything.
Ḥilfai said, if he<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The robber was challenged by its owner to return to him his leavened matter before Passover and swore that he did not have it. Because of the oath he now is obligated for valuable leavened matter; he is barred from returning after Passover the leavened matter which now is worthless.</i> swore to him before Passover, then after Passover he pays him for fully useable leavened matter. Rebbi Mana said, I went to Caesarea and asked Rebbi Ḥizqiah, who quoted this in the name of Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, is there anybody who disagrees with Ḥilfai so that he would not have to pay for fully useable leavened matter? Everybody agrees about leavened matter that he has to pay for fully useable leavened matter.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one gave something to artisans to repair and they broke it, they are liable. If one gave a chest, a box, or a cupboard to a woodworker to repair and he broke it, he is liable. And the builder who contracted to take down a wall is liable to pay if he ruined the stones or caused damage. If he was taking it down on one side when if collapsed on the other side he is not liable, but when it happened because of the pounding he is liable.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one gave something to artisans to repair and they broke it,” etc. Rav Huna said, only if he put in the last nail to acquire the rights to his utensils<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 98a" href="/Bava_Kamma.98a">Babli, 98a</a>, infers from the second sentence of the Mishnah, which clearly refers to existing containers given to be fixed, that the “something” given in the first sentence is raw material, given to be turned into a utensil. The question then arises whether the artisan who ruins the utensil has to pay only for the material or for the finished utensil (since the client has to find another artisan and pay the latter.) About this question, Rav Huna (in the Babli, Rav Assi) notes that the artisan is liable only for the raw material as long as he did not put in the last nail. Once the object is finished, the artisan has the right to retain it until his bill be paid. Therefore, he then holds the object under the rules of the paid trustee and is liable for damage to it (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:8" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.8">Tosephta 10:8</a>).</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one gave wool to the dyer and the vat burned it, he gives him the value of his wool<sup class="footnote-marker">47,</sup><i class="footnote">By his unprofessional work he changed the material from his prior state and acquired it as his property for which he has to pay.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">In this case, the material is lost; it is impossible to refer to improved value.</i>. If he dyed it poorly, if the increased value is more than the expenses<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The dyer’s cash expenses for dye and fuel.</i>, he gives him his expenses; if the expenses are more than the increased value he gives him the increased value.<br>To dye it red and he dyed it black, black and he dyed it red, Rebbi Meïr says, he gives him the value of his wool<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">By his unprofessional work he changed the material from his prior state and acquired it as his property for which he has to pay.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah says, if the increased value is more than the expenses, he gives him his expenses; if the expenses are more than the increased value he gives him the increased value.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one gave wool to the dyer,” etc. Rav Huna said, if chemicals burned it<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The dye ruined the wool.</i>, but if the vat burned it<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">By overheating with too little fluid in the vat before dye was added. Then the wool was not changed from its original state. Rav Huna has to emend the text of the Mishnah.</i>, he may say to him: Here is your property before you. Samuel said, even<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This means: Only if chemicals burned it; otherwise one follows the text of the Mishnah.</i> if chemicals burned it, he may say to him: Here is your property before you.
What means “if the increased value is more than the expenses, he gives him his expenses”? A person gave to another five lots<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the reading of E and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Bava Kamma 100b:2:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Bava_Kamma.100b.2.1">Tosaphot 100b, <i>s.v.</i> אם</a>, which read מנוי “portions” rather than מיני “kinds”. The same reading is found in שיטה מקובצת <i>ad</i> 100b. E starts the sentence by: אית בר נש “there is a man”.</i> of wool, five portions of dye, and ten minas for his wages. He told him, dye it red, but the other dyed it black. He told him, if you had dyed it red, it would have been worth 25 minas, now that you dyed it black it is worth only 20 minas. You lost yours, I did not lose mine<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">He deducts 5 minas from the dyer’s wages. This argument is about value, not cost.</i>. Rebbi Jonah<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">E and שיטה מקובצת read: R. Yose.</i> said, you can understand from here that if a person gave to another eight denars to buy for him wheat in Tiberias but he bought for him in Sepphoris, he may say to him, if you had bought for me in Tiberias there would have been 25 <i>modii</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Modius,</i> a Roman measure of volume, 64 <i>quartarii,</i> about 8.51, 2/3 of a <i>se’ah.</i></i>; now that you bought in Sepphoris there are only 20 <i>modii.</i> You lost yours, I did not lose mine<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">He deducts the price of 5 <i>modii</i> from the broker’s fee.</i>.
It was stated: One gives money to another to buy wheat for him, but the man bought barley. If it went down in price, it went down in price for him; if it rose in price, it rose for him<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since the agent did not fulfill the conditions of his agency, he acquired the barley for himself and has to carry the entire financial risk in selling the barley and buying wheat instead. Since the risk is entirely the agent’s, the eventual gain also is his.</i>. We stated otherwise<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 4:20" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).4.20">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 4:20</a>.</i>: “If it went down in price, it went down in price for him; if it rose in price, it rose for both of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">The unfaithful agent is disadvantaged. Nevertheless, one has to find a reason for the unequal treatment of loss and gain.</i>” <sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Added from E; there is no Genizah text for the remainder of the Tractate. The addition seems necessary and the oversight of the Leiden scribe is easily explained. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 102b" href="/Bava_Kamma.102b">Babli, 102b</a>, the identification of the Tannaїm is ascribed to R. Joḥanan who, however, is quoted there to the effect that R. Jehudah does not accept that unfaithful execution of an agency makes the agent the owner of the object acquired.</i>[He who said if it went down in price, it went down in price for him; if it rose in price, it rose for him, is Rebbi Meïr. What is Rebbi Meïr’s reason? Because the seller intended to transfer ownership only to the buyer<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 6:8:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.6.8.6"><i>Demay</i> 6:12 (Notes 194–196</a>), this opinion is attributed to R. Jehudah. Since ownership is transferred when the seller hands over the goods, it matters to whom the seller intended to transfer title. Usually the seller does not know whether the buyer is acting on his own account or as an agent; the law will have to tell the seller what to intend.</i>. But he who said, if it went down in price, it went down in price for him; if it rose in price, it rose for both of them, is Rebbi Jehudah.] What is Rebbi Jehudah’s reason? Because the seller intended to transfer ownership only to the owner of the money<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Demai 6:8:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Demai.6.8.6"><i>Demay</i> 6:12 (Notes 194–196</a>), this opinion is attributed to R. Yose. R. Meïr is not mentioned there.</i>. Why does he (not)<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this word should be deleted; it is missing in the quote given by <i>Bet Yosef, Ḥošen Mišpaṭ</i> §123.</i> split with him? Because it is forbidden to take advantage of another person<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">One should not trade with another person’s money. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 117b" href="/Bava_Metzia.117b">Babli, <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 117b</a>, this is a statement of R. Joḥanan.</i>. Then even if one gave him money to buy wheat and he bought wheat?<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">According to R. Jehudah, anything bought through an agent should need another transfer of ownership from agent to principal. This clearly is not practice.</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Added from E, not in L.</i>[Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he had given money to buy wheat and he bought wheat,] the seller intended to transfer ownership only to the owner of the money. If he had given money to buy wheat and he bought barley, the seller intended to transfer ownership only to the buyer, who is stuck with what he has on his hands. Rebbi Nasa said, if he was true to his agency, did the seller not intend to transfer ownership to the owner of the money? If he was not true to his agency, he intended to transfer ownership to the buyer<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">R. Nasa agrees with R. Joḥanan in practice but formulates it in a way applicable to any situation.</i>. Then why does he split with him? He said, because he realizes a profit by means of the other man’s money, he splits with him<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">One should not trade with another person’s money. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Metzia 117b" href="/Bava_Metzia.117b">Babli, <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 117b</a>, this is a statement of R. Joḥanan.</i>.
Rebbi Samuel bar Abba objected before Rebbi Joḥanan. Did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>‘Arakhin</i> 6:5.</i>: “Both the person who dedicates<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">He dedicates his entire property to the Temple. The administrators of the Temple have to foreclose all of his properties.</i> and the one who vows his own valuation<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">He vows the amount specified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:1-8" href="/Leviticus.27.1-8"><i>Lev</i>. 27:1–8</a> as valuation, which for an adult male is fixed in v. 3 as 50 <i>holy sheqel,</i> interpreted as 50 tetradrachmas (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.4"><i>Qiddushin</i> 1:1, Note 339</a>).</i> exclude their wives’ and children’s clothing.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah there also excludes “dye which was dyed for them (the wife and children).” If R. Joḥanan is correct and by law the seller intends to benefit the owner of the money by which he is paid, who told the dyer or the clothier to transfer his intent from the husband, the giver of the money, to the wife and children, the beneficiaries?</i>” He answered, nobody thought to include their wives’ and children’s clothing<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The problem in <i>‘Arakhin</i> has nothing to do with our problem here since a vow is always subject to the exclusions stipulated by the person making the vow.</i>. But did we not state: “My valuation on myself”; at the moment when he said, my valuation on myself, he only obligated himself for fifty tetradrachmas<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">He vows the amount specified in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:1-8" href="/Leviticus.27.1-8"><i>Lev</i>. 27:1–8</a> as valuation, which for an adult male is fixed in v. 3 as 50 <i>holy sheqel,</i> interpreted as 50 tetradrachmas (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:3:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.3.4"><i>Qiddushin</i> 1:1, Note 339</a>).</i>. If he says, my valuation on myself on condition not to pledge what is worn by my wife and children? Rebbi Nasa in the name of Samuel bar Abba: One who says, my valuation on myself on condition not to pledge a particular thing, one cannot use that particular thing as a pledge<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Again, this is a reformulation of the previous statement in a form universally applicable.</i>. Rav Hoshaia in the name of Samuel bar Abba: This implies that one does not foreclose valuations from movables by biblical decree<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">If the administrators of the Temple operated according to a biblical decree, all stipulations would be invalid (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 9:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.9.1.1"><i>Ketubot</i> 9:1, Note 5</a>). If stipulations are respected, the process of foreclosure of movables cannot be biblical.</i> but only from the rabbis.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who robbed at least a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth from a fellow man and swore to him<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">The victim had asked him to return the robbed item when he swore that he had not robbed him. When later he admits to the robbery, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:23-25" href="/Leviticus.5.23-25"><i>Lev.</i>5:23–25</a> makes it clear that after a false oath there can be no atonement nor any divine forgiveness until the falsely denied debt was paid in full. The “fifth” is computed from above, a fifth of the entire payment which is a fourth of the amount of the robbery.</i> should deliver it to him even in Media<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">Meaning any far-away place which is difficult to reach.</i>. He should deliver neither to [the victim’s] son nor to his agent<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">While one can liquidate a debt through the creditor’s agent, one cannot fulfill the religious duty of restitution by proxy.</i>, but he may deliver to an agent of the court<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Duly appointed in a public act at the request of the victim (cf. <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 3:6).</i>, and if [the victim] had died, he has to make restitution to his heirs.<br>If he had returned the capital<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">The value of the robbed item.</i> but not the fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">1,</sup><i class="footnote">The robber, who takes things openly and by force, has to make restitution with possibly a 25% fine, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21-24" href="/Leviticus.5.21-24"><i>Lev.</i> 5:21–24</a>. The thief who takes things by stealth has to pay double.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The verse requires personal restitution. The additional 25% constitute a fine; it is additional money, not restitution, and can be liquidated by agent or by mail.</i>, or [the victim] had forgiven him the capital but not the fifth or had forgiven him both except for less than a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth of the capital<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Less than a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth is not money; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:1:17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.1.17"><i>Qiddušin</i> 1:1, Note 7</a>.</i>, he need not follow him.<br>If he had returned the fifth but not the capital, or [the victim] had forgiven him the fifth but not the capital or had forgiven him both except for a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth of the capital, he has to follow him.<br>If he paid the capital but swore on the fifth, he pays a fifth for the fifth until the capital<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">As is explained in the Halakhah, the “capital” is the object of the false oath. For the embezzlement or robbery, the “fifth” is 25% of the original amount. For a false oath about these 25%, the additional fine is 6.25% of the original amount. In the next step, 1.5625% would have to be added and so on. But once the amount in dispute becomes less than a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth, the process stops since that amount no longer represents money.</i> becomes less than a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth. And so “for a deposit, or a partnership, or robbery, or if he oppressed his neighbor, or found lost property and denied it, and swore falsely,<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:21" href="/Leviticus.5.21"><i>Lev.</i> 5:21</a>.</i>” has to pay the capital, and a fifth, and a reparation sacrifice.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who robbed at least a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth from a fellow man,” etc. So is the Mishnah: Until the last fifth becomes less than a <i>peruṭa</i>’s worth<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.6.1">Mishnah 10</a>, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.6.1">Note 83</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jonathan said: By handing over the fifth, the fifth becomes capital<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">If the robber starts paying the fifth, it becomes capital in the terminology of the Mishnah.</i>. This only applies with witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">For if the robber confessed in presence of the victim and the latter agreed to be paid later, the sum becomes a loan and has to be paid under the law of loans rather than that of restitution; it can be repaid to the creditor’s agent. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 108a" href="/Bava_Kamma.108a">Babli, 108a</a>, disagrees and holds that the fifth be due only on the confession by the robber but not on the testimony of witnesses. The explanations of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:7" href="/Numbers.5.7">Ibn Ezra (<i>Num.</i> 5:7</a>) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:24" href="/Leviticus.5.24">Ḥizquni (<i>Lev.</i> 5:24</a>) who require a simple fifth on the confession and two fifths on testimony of witnesses are based on the Yerushalmi.</i> and at the time of sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">Since payment of the fifth is stated as a precondition to atonement by sacrifice, the payment of the fifth cannot be enforced in the absence of a Temple; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Bava Kamma 63b:2:4" href="/Rashi_on_Bava_Kamma.63b.2.4">Rashi 63b, <i>s.v.</i> הודה מעצמו</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Where is my deposit? He told him ‘it was lost<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">As an unpaid trustee he does not have to pay if it was lost and the trustee never had used it for himself.</i>’. ‘I will make you swear’ and he said ‘amen’<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">This is the oath.</i>. If witnesses testify against him that he ate it, he pays the capital<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">As a robber; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.6.2">Note 87</a>.</i>. If he confessed on his own, he has to pay the capital, and a fifth, and a reparation sacrifice.<br>Where is my deposit? He told him ‘it was stolen’. ‘I will make you swear’ and he said ‘amen’. If witnesses testify against him that he stole it, he pays double restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">For the unpaid trustee who claimed falsely that the deposit was stolen, double restitution is required by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 22:8" href="/Exodus.22.8"><i>Ex.</i> 22:8</a>.</i>. If he confessed on his own, he has to pay the capital, and a fifth, and a reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Nobody pays multiple restitution on his own confession since a confession is not acceptable in a criminal trial. He pays a fifth and brings the sacrifice for the false oath, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:23-25" href="/Leviticus.5.23-25"><i>Lev.</i> 5:23–25</a>.</i>.<br>He who robbed from his father and swore to him when he<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The father.</i> died has to pay capital and fifth to his sons or brothers<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Yerushalmi does not discuss this Mishnah, we do not know whether the intention is that the robber has to pay to the father’s brothers or other sons ot to his own sons or brothers. In any case he cannot obtain atonement as long as a <i>peruṭa</i> from the robbery remains in his hand.</i>. But if he refuses<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">He does not want to pay anything to his family.</i>, or does not have the money, he takes a loan and the creditors come and make themselves paid<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">He takes a loan, pays his obligations, and when the loan comes due directs the creditor to the recipients of his payments. Then he has fulfilled his obligation without losing any of his money.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Where is my deposit? He told him ‘it was lost’,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 11:1" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).11.1">Tosephta 11:1</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 51b" href="/Bava_Batra.51b">Babli <i>Bava batra</i>51b</a>.</i>: “One does not accept deposits from women, or slaves, or underage children<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Since usually they have no independent property, one has to suspect that the deposit might be stolen property.</i>. If one accepted from a woman, he should return it to her; if she died, he should return it to her husband<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Who is her heir.</i>. If one accepted from a slave, he should return it to him; if he died, he should return it to his master. From an underage child, he should return it to him, if he died he should return it to his father, and make it a safe investment<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this sentence should be rearranged as in the Tosephta: “From an underage child, he should make it a safe investment, return it to him (once he reaches the age in which he legally can do business); if he died he should return it to his father.”</i>. If any of these said at the moment of their death, it should be given to X because it is his property, one should follow his interpretation<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">The translation follows the extensive discussion of the expression פירוש לפירושו by S. Lieberman in <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Neziqin,</i> p. 134.</i>.” Rebbi Zebida in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Mamal: Only before witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">Dispositions of a last will which do not follow the general rule should be executed only in the presence of witnesses, to protect the trustee against claims of the legal heirs.</i>. As the following<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Another version of the same story is told in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 52a" href="/Bava_Batra.52a">Babli, <i>Bava batra</i>52a</a>. There, the husband is Abba bar bar Ḥana. This is the correct attribution since Abba bar Ḥana, the elder R. Ḥiyya’s brother, died before the birth of his son, therefore also before his wife. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:1:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.1.2-7">Halakhah 10:1</a>, “R. Abba bar Ḥana” speaks of “my oncle R. Ḥiyya”. This proves that the person called Abba bar Ḥana in the Yerushalmi is called Abba bar bar Ḥana in the Babli.</i>: When Rebbi Abba bar Ḥana’s wife was dying, she said, these rings belong to my daughter. But he said no, they are mine. The case came before Rav who said, people are not given to lying at the time of their death<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">A statement of Rava in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 175a" href="/Bava_Batra.175a">Babli, <i>Bava batra</i> 175a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody says to his son: A <i>qônām</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">An expression of making a vow; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 1:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.1.1.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 1:1</a>.</i> that you cannot enjoy anything from me, when he dies he can inherit from him<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Since a deceased person has no property, the estate is no longer the father’s money.</i>. During my lifetime and after my death, when he dies he cannot inherit from him but should give it<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The inheritance.</i> to his sons or his brothers, and if he has nothing<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">To eat.</i> he may take a loan; the creditors come and make themselves paid<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">He takes a loan, pays his obligations, and when the loan comes due directs the creditor to the recipients of his payments. Then he has fulfilled his obligation without losing any of his money.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody says to his son: A <i>qônām</i> that you cannot enjoy anything from me,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">This text is a reformulation of a text in <i>Nedarim</i> 5:2, explained there in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.3.1">Notes 35–43</a>.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah said, look what we did state here: “If somebody says to his son, ‘a <i>qônām</i> that you can not enjoy anything from me,’ if he dies, the son inherits; ‘during my lifetime and after my death,’ if he dies, he cannot inherit.” Did we not state, ‘during my lifetime’, he inherits; ‘after my death’, he inherits, ‘during my lifetime and after my death’, he cannot inherit? What is the difference between the one who says it one by one and the one who says it by twos? Rav Jeremiah and Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina both say, ‘a <i>qônām</i> that I shall not enter this house during my lifetime and after my death’, since he said “this house”, he forbade it on himself during lifetime and after death. Rebbi Yose said, we have stated in <i>Neziqin</i> what we did not state in <i>Nedarim</i>: ‘A <i>qônām</i> that I cannot enjoy these my properties during my lifetime and after my death,’ from the moment he said “these” he forbade them to himself during his lifetime and after his death.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One who robbed from a proselyte<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Who failed to start a Jewish family. By becoming a Jew, he became a new person, no longer related to his previous Gentile family.</i>, swore to him, and [the proselyte] died, has to pay the capital and the fifth to the priests and the reparation offering to the altar as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:8" href="/Numbers.5.8"><i>Num.</i> 5:8</a>: “If the man has no heirs to give the reparation to, the reparation shall be returned to the Eternal, to the priests, except for the atonement ram by which he can reach atonement.” While the sacrificial meat of the ram is eaten by the priests in the Temple courtyard, they eat from the Eternal’s table and it never becomes their property.</i>: “If the man has no heirs to give the reparation to, the reparation etc.” If he was bringing the money and the reparation offering when he<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">The robber.</i> died, the money should be given to his sons<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">His death was atonement. Since he does not need atonement after death, the money does not need to be given to the priests; it becomes part of the estate.</i> and the reparation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">If it was dedicated as such by its owner, the dedication cannot be undone.</i> shall graze until it becomes disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">As a sacrifice, either by developing a defect or outliving its third birthday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Parah 1:3" href="/Mishnah_Parah.1.3">Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 1:3</a>).</i>, then be sold, and the amount be given for voluntary offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The account from which elevation offerings were paid if otherwise the altar would have been empty.</i>. If he had given the money to the men of the watch<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">The priests were organized into 24 watches; each watch served from one Sabbath morning to the next. The watch on duty had the exclusive rights to all emoluments accruing to priests during their term of office.</i> when he died, the heirs cannot ask it back from them since it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:10" href="/Numbers.5.10"><i>Num.</i> 5:10</a>.</i>: “What a man give to a Cohen shall be the latter’s.”
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One who robbed from a proselyte,” etc. Ḥilfai and Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina said, this is Rebbi Yose the Galilean’s, as Rebbi Yose the Galiean explained: Of what does the verse speak? About one who robbed from a proselyte, swore to him, who then died, that he would pay the capital and the fifth to the priests and the reparation offering to the altar. “If the man have no relative,” he who needs atonement is obligated; this excludes the one who does not need atonement. Rebbi Aqiba explained when he came from Zephyrium<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Possibly the town in Cyprus (Cape Paphos).</i>: About one who robbed from a proselyte, swore to him, who then died, that he would pay the capital and the fifth to the priests and the reparation offering to the altar, as it is said: “If the man have no relative,” etc. The only person in Israel who has no relative is the proselyte. Rav and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, it is Rebbi Aqiba’s who said that the wrongly acquired property has to be returned in any case<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph is elliptic; its meaning can be ascertained from the parallels in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 109a" href="/Bava_Kamma.109a">Babli 109a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:16" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.16">Tosephta 10:16</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:17" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.17">17</a>, <i>Sifry Num.</i> #4 (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 8:7" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.8.7"><i>Num. rabba</i>8(7</a>), <i>Yalquṭ</i> #701). Both R. Yose the Galilean and R. Aqiba agree that “the man without relative” must be a proselyte who failed to start a Jewish family. (In <i>Sifry,</i> this statement is attributed to R. Ismael.) The rules of inheritance imply that the property of the deceased proselyte becomes ownerless; therefore anything held by another person at the proselyte’s death becomes property of the holder. If the robber confessed his guilt after the proselyte’s death, the money becomes the Eternal’s property Who gives it to the priests. If the robber confessed his guilt to the proselyte, even if the proselyte agrees to payment at a later date, turning the debt into a loan, when he dies the robber does not acquire the money since he cannot atone for his guilt as long as the money is in his hand. If the robber dies before he returned the money, for R. Yose the Galilean the money becomes part of the robber’s estate since the dead “do not need atonement.” R. Aqiba holds that the money does not become part of the estate since “wrongly acquired property has to be returned in any case,” and the heirs have to turn the money over to the priests. <i>Sifry</i> notes that R. Aqiba agreed with R. Yose the Galilean before he went to Zephyrium but there changed his opinion. (The Tosephta attributes R. Yose the Galilean’s argument to R. Aqiba).</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he gave the money to Yehoiarib and the reparation offering to Yedaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">In the list of watches, <a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 24:7-18" href="/I_Chronicles.24.7-18"><i>1Chr.</i>24:7–18</a>, Yehoiarib is the first and Yedaiah the second watch. The restitution may precede the offering but cannot follow it. As explained at the end of the Mishnah, the reparation offering (whether for swearing falsely to a native born Jew or to a proselyte) cannot be accepted by the Temple if it was not ascertained that restitution was made since both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:25" href="/Leviticus.5.25"><i>Lev.</i> 5:25</a> as in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:8" href="/Numbers.5.8"><i>Num.</i>5:8</a> the sacrifice is clearly demanded <i>after</i> restitution had been made. There can be no ritual cleansing if the human aspect was not attended to.</i>, he has fulfilled his obligation. The reparation offering to Yehoiarib and the money to Yedaiah, if the offering still is alive it should be brought by Yedaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">Since the priests of Yehoiarib have no right to officiate in Yedaiah’s week; this is the “right sold by the forefathers” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 18:8" href="/Deuteronomy.18.8"><i>Deut.</i> 18:8</a>).</i>; otherwise he was to come back and bring another reparation offering; for he who brings the robbed item before he brings the reparation offering has fulfilled his obligation, but he who brings the reparation offering before he brings the robbed item has not fulfilled his obligation. If he paid the capital but not the fifth, the fifth is no obstacle<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Non-payment of the fifth does not invalidate the sacrifice but the sacrifice does not absolve from paying the fifth at a later date.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If he gave the money to Yehoiarib,” etc. Rebbi Ḥizqiah said, they disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">There is no disagreement in the Mishnah. The entire Halakhah is based on the <i>baraita</i> quoted in the following; paralleled in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:18" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.18">Tosephta 10:18</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 111a" href="/Bava_Kamma.111a">Babli 111a</a>), except that in the text here the disagreement is missing.</i> about two weeks, Rebbi Yose said about one week. He who said about two weeks, “if he gave the money to Yehoiarib on Yedaiah’s watch and the reparation offering to Yedaiah on Yehoiarib’s watch, he has fulfilled his obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">What is missing here is the statement of the diagreement: “If the offering was given to Yehoiarib and the money to Yedaiah, R. Jehudah requires the money to be transferred to the offerers of the sacrifice, the Sages that the sacrifice be transferred to the recipients of the money.”<br>Both watches acted incorrectly by not checking whether the money had been delivered before the sacrifice. If each watch received their part during their week then clearly the situation is that of the Mishnah where a new offering is needed if the first one is not available to the second watch. But if the second watch received the money at the same time the first received the sacrifice, then R. Jehudah and the Sages disagree about who has to give up his part.</i> Rebbi said, following Rebbi Jehudah’s words if the men from Yehoiarib offered the reparation offering there was no atonement; but the money should be transferred from the men of Yedaiah to the men of Yehoiarib; the men from Yehoiarib then shall offer the reparation offering for atonement.” This implies that one removes the money given to men of a watch not during their week. He who said that they disagree about one week, if he gave the money to Yehoiarib on Yedaiah’s watch and the reparation offering to Yedaiah on Yehoiarib’s watch<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice one week before the delivery of the money.</i>, means that he did not fulfill his obligation, etc. This implies that one does not remove a sacrifice which men of a watch received during their week. But if the watch of the men of Yehoiarib dragged their feet, then the <i>baraita</i> implies that they dragged their feet after the men of Yehoiarib received the reparation offering after days<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The watch cannot keep the animal for 24 weeks until their turn comes again.</i>, and there is no atonement. But one has to transfer the money from the men of Yedaiah to the men of Yehoiarib, the men of Yedaiah<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">In E: “Yehoiarib”. This clearly is a corruption. But the text common to both mss. is also incorrect. Since the hypothesis was that the money had been given to Yehoiarib, it is clear that one has to read: “One has to transfer the money from the men of <i>Yehoiarib</i> to the men of <i>Yedaiah</i>.”</i> shall offer the reparation offering, and there will be atonement. This implies that if men of a watch received a sacrifice during their week but were lazy and did not offer it, one removes it from their hand.

Chapter 10



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody robbed to feed his children and they inherited from him, they are not liable to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Even if they were the ones benefiting from the robbery.</i>; if it was mortgageable property they have to pay<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">A different formulation is in the Halakhah: “they have to return it”; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 10:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.10.1.3">Note 14</a>.</i>. One changes money<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">To exchange small coins for large or vice-versa. But one may take from them coins as change from a large coin used to pay the tolls even though the coins probably were taken by extortion.</i> neither from the chest of the publican<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah presupposes the system of toll and tax farming where the contractor paid the government the expected amount of taxes in advance and then had to collect more than that amount to pay his employees and to make a profit.
