Since therefore he had to instruct his employees to collect more than was due by the government’s computation, there is no toll or tax collector’s box which does not contain money tainted by extortion.</i> nor the wallet of the tax collector and one does not accept charity from these, but one accepts from their house<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Charity given by the publican or tax collector from his private money is not tainted; only that money collected in the course of his duty necessarily contains extortion money.</i> or from the market place<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Charity given by the publican or tax collector from his private money is not tainted; only that money collected in the course of his duty necessarily contains extortion money.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If somebody robbed to feed his children,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 5:25-26" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).5.25-26">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 5:25–26</a>; in a different version <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 94b" href="/Bava_Kamma.94b">Babli 94b</a>.</i>: “A Jew who is engaged in lending with interest and repented, has to return it<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The ill-gotten gains.</i>. If he died and left it to his children, about him the verse says ‘he prepares but the just will wear it.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Job 27:17" href="/Job.27.17"><i>Job</i> 27:17</a>. As usual, the reference is to the part of the verse not quoted: “he (the sinner) distributes clean money.”</i>’ If he left them a cow or a stole, they have to return it.” He went and stated that for robberies<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the statement starts with illegal interest income, it is not usual to obtain a cow or a stole as interest. The last sentence, therefore, has to refer to robbed goods.</i>.
“If it was mortgageable property they have to return it.” What is mortgageable property? Rebbi Jonathan says, if they inherited real estate. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, if they inherited robbed items<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 113a" href="/Bava_Kamma.113a">Babli, 113a</a>, quotes this in the name of Rebbi; E reads “Joḥanan” instead of Jonathan.</i>. Rav said, an heir is like the holder of encumbered property. Just as a loan executed before witnesses cannot be collected from encumbered property, so it cannot be collected from heirs<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Gittin 3:7:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Gittin.3.7.5"><i>Giṭṭin</i> 3:8 (Note 146</a>), 8:7 (Note 104), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Metzia 1:6:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Metzia.1.6.2-4"><i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 1:6</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 111b" href="/Bava_Kamma.111b">Babli 111b</a> (in the name of Rami bar Ḥama). A loan executed without a public document is not subject to a title search; the loan cannot be collected either from mortgaged property or from the innocent buyer of property pledged for the oral loan.</i>. Samuel said, what was received cannot be foreclosed on mortgaged property, but on free property it can be foreclosed<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 111b" href="/Bava_Kamma.111b">Babli 111b</a>, in the name of Rava. Since the loan made before witnesses can be collected from unencumbered property, it can be collected from unencumbered property of the estate.<br>In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 175b" href="/Bava_Batra.175b">Babli, <i>Bava batra</i> 175b</a>, Samuel agrees with Rav that an undocumented loan cannot be collected from the heirs; the opinion expressed here, that it can be enforced against the heirs but not against buyers of the property, is attributed there (176a) to Rav Pappa and declared judicial practice.</i>. But does [the Mishnah] not disagree with Rav? Since Rav said, an heir is like a holder of encumbered property; just as a loan executed before witnesses cannot be collected from encumbered property, so it cannot be foreclosed from heirs<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">But the Mishnah requires robbed real estate to be paid for. The question makes sense only in the formulation of the Mishnah, not the quote at the beginning of the present paragraph since both R. Jonathan and R. Simeon ben Laqish agree that originally robbed real estate available after the robber’s death must be returned to its original owners.</i>. Explain it if he left them real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">There is no question of “paying for it”, only of “returning it.”</i>. But might it<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah.</i> not disagree with Samuel? Did not Samuel say, what was received cannot be foreclosed on mortgaged property? Explain it if they inherited robbed items<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Which has to be returned according to everybody, for reasons other than the rules of foreclosure.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">This is a reformulation of the discussion in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 7:2:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.7.2.6"><i>Terumot</i> 7:5, Notes 92–95</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 115a" href="/Bava_Kamma.115a">Babli, 115a</a>, deals with the case that the robber sold the garment.</i> If he robbed a stole and gave it to another person. Rebbi Eleazar said in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya, one takes away from the first one but not from the second<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:20" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.20">Tosephta 10:20</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Yannai said, one takes away even from the second. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, even Rebbi Ḥiyya will agree with this since Rebbi Ḥiyya said, one takes away even from the second<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">In this version, R. Abba bar Mamal contradicts R. Eleazar. In the version of <i>Terumot,</i> one only states that R. Ḥiyya agrees that the robber cannot be sued if the recipient voluntarily gives the item back to its original owner.</i>.
If his vessels were switched at the artisan’s he may use them while he goes out and searches for his own. At a mourner’s house or at a wedding, he may not use them while he goes out and searches for his own<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 46a" href="/Bava_Batra.46a">Babli <i>Bava batra</i> 46a</a>, where the difference between both rules is explained it is stated that the artisan may sometimes sell vessels on the order of their owner whereas a switch at a social function always is in error.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Ḥanan said, my uncle Rebbi Ḥiyya explained this<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The permission to use the artisan’s vessels.</i> only as in the case of one Bar Qora where everybody who takes from him takes by permission and everybody who puts something up there does so with permission.
<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:39" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.39">Tosephta 10:39</a>.</i>“If thieves who stole by stealth repented, they have to make restitution<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">To clear their consciences. There is no question of double restitution which is possible only by judgment.</i>. If one of a gang repented, he has to make restitution for himself. But if he traded for them<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If he traded the loot to fences and distributed the money among the gang, he is responsible for the fact that the victims of the theft cannot recover their property.</i>, he has to make restitution for all of them.”
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:21" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.21">Tosephta 10:21</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 112a" href="/Bava_Kamma.112a">Babli 112a</a>.</i>: “If somebody robbed to feed his children, whether adult or underage, they are not liable to pay. If they inherited from him, both adult and underage are liable to pay. Symmachos says, the adults are liable, the underaged are not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The underaged cannot be sued since they have no standing in court and no witnesses in civil suits are admitted without both parties being present. Underaged heirs can be sued in court only in foreclosure proceedings based on documents, in which case the court will appoint a guardian who can examine the validity of the document for them.</i>. If they said, we do not know whether our father accounted for it at the end, they are not liable.” Rebbi Abba bar Mamal objected: Think of it, if they inherited real estate, they are not able to say, we do not know whether our father accounted for it at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah states unconditionally that robbed real estate has to be returned.</i>. Rav Hamnuna objected: Think of it, if it was a notorious robbery; they are not able to say, we do not know whether our father accounted for it at the end. Rav Sheshet objected: For the underaged the court will argue<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The court-appointed attorney will argue for the underaged that they cannot know whether their father arranged himself with his victim.</i>. What can you say? Rebbi Mana said, explain it if they argue, we do not know, etc., we know that our father was in dispute with you on this account, but we do not know how it was resolved<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">This is a credible defense; the claimant either has to prove that there were no contacts or provide documentary proof that the claim was not settled.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the publicans took his donkey and gave him another donkey, or armed robbers<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to a גַזְלָן, a לִיסטיס (λῃστής) always is an <i>armed</i> robber.</i> took his garment and gave him another garment, they are his since the owners had given up hope of recovery<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Any lost article for which the owners had given up hope of recovery is ownerless property and becomes the property of the finder. (This is the principle underlying the first two Chapters of <i>Bava meṣi‘a.</i>) It does not matter that the owners lost hope because of unlawful acts by others.</i>. Things saved from a flood or from armed robbers are his if the owners had given up hope of recovery. Similarly, a swarm of bees is his if the owners had given up hope of recovery. Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa said, a woman or a child are trusted if they say: “This swarm of bees came from here.”<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">By this they determine the ownership of the swarm of bees, allow the bee-keeper to try to capture the swarm and prohibit anybody else from taking it. While women are barred from formal testimony, they are admitted to indicate facts. Children are never believed about what they remember, but here they indicate a fact immediately after it happened; their indication can be taken as unbiased truth.<br>Swarming bees are lost if not captured immediately. Therefore the rules about swarms of bees belong to the rules of things that cannot be recovered later.</i> [The beekeeper] may enter another’s field to take his swarm; if he caused damage he has to pay what he damaged, but he cannot cut off a branch<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">On which the bees have settled.</i> intending to pay for it. Rebbi Ismael, Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa’s son, said: he may even cut a branch and pay for it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If the publicans took,” etc. It was stated: “Things saved from an army, or a fire, from a whirlpool in the sea, a whirlpool of a river, are his.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">A similar statement in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Metzia (Lieberman) 2:2" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Metzia_(Lieberman).2.2">Tosephta <i>Bava meṣi‘a</i> 2:2</a> adds: “because the owners gave up hope of recovery.”</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Another formulation of the same topic is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 2:10:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.2.10.8"><i>Ketubot</i> 2:11, Notes 203–209</a>.</i>“Similarly, a swarm of bees is his if the owners had given up hope of recovery.” Rebbi Ḥinena bar Pappos in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The Mishnah is about a swarm taken in flight and from a schoolboy<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">But not a younger child (Rashba, <i>Novellae ad</i> 114b.)</i>, and only immediately<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">“Immediately” refers to the testimony of women and children, who can determine ownership, even in their own behalf, on the pursuit ot the swarm.</i>. But if one left and returned, I am saying that he<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The underage witness.</i> said it because of fear, or being seduced. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 82b" href="/Bava_Kamma.82b">Babli 82b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 114b" href="/Bava_Kamma.114b">114b</a>.</i>: Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa says, it is a stipulation by the court that this one may cut a branch and pay for it since on this condition did Joshua distribute the Land to Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The last remark is not in the Babli. For the list of stipulations about land use ascribed to Joshua, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 7:1:21" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.7.1.21"><i>Nazir</i>7:1, Note 40</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one recognizes his own vessels or books in the hand of another<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">And he can prove that they were his.</i>, if the theft was a known fact in town, [the other] shall swear to him how much he paid and take this amount<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The owner can force the present owner to sell him his heirlooms for the price which he had paid to thief or fence.</i>. Otherwise<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">If the police had not been notified of the theft.</i> he<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The person who claims that his property was in the hand of another.</i> cannot be believed, for I am saying that he had sold the items to a third party and the other had bought them from there.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one recognizes his own vessels or books,” etc. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, it would have been correct that they should not have to swear<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">There certainly is no biblical reason why the case should be treated differently from any other claim about money.</i>. Why did they say they have to swear? That homeowners should not be fences<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If thieves find no buyers they will stop stealing.</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, only if it was a known fact that his books had been stolen<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The claimant can only force an oath on the owner of his books if the theft of specific books was made public.</i>. Rav said, he has to bring proof that he stayed there<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If the prior owner was not at his place at the time of the theft, one might suspect that the theft occurred with the owner’s connivance. This does not mean that he has no chance of getting back his books through a regular suit, but the rules of the Mishnah cannot be applied.</i> the night in question. Assi said, if he claims: “I bought them from X,” he is to be believed<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">If the prior owner was not at his place at the time of the theft, one might suspect that the theft occurred with the owner’s connivance. This does not mean that he has no chance of getting back his books through a regular suit, but the rules of the Mishnah cannot be applied.</i>. A case came before Rebbi Nasa and he did not accept it. Does he disagree? They said, he was a known troublemaker<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Explaining the <i>hapax</i> דעקין from Arabic ذعق “to produce fear by shouting”, cf. Hebrew צעק.</i>; therefore he did not accept the case<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah puts it into the hands of the judge to decide when a theft “is publicly known”, it can be read as allowing judicial discretion in deciding whether a case look legitimate or not.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One comes with his amphora of wine, the other comes with his jug of honey. If the amphora<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.3.1.1">Chapter 3, Note 2</a>.</i> of honey sprang a leak, if the other one poured out his wine and saved the honey into it, he only has his wages<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For the work, not for the lost wine.</i>. But if he said, I shall save yours if you pay me the value of mine, he<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner of the more valuable merchandise agreed, he has to keep the terms of this oral contract.</i> is liable to pay him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One comes with his amphora of wine,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:28" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.28">Tosephta 10:28</a>. The Tosephta adds: “When they come to an inhabited area, [the owner of the water] has to pay the value of the honey.” There is not much reason to believe that this or a similar text is understood in the Yerushalmi’s <i>baraita;</i> cf. <i>Tosefta kiFshutah Neziqin</i> p. 56.</i>: “Two people are in the desert; in the hand of one is an amphora of water, in the other’s hand an amphora of honey. If the water amphora springs a leak it is a stipulation of the court that the other pour out his honey in order to save the water since water sustains life in the desert but not honey.” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 10:29" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).10.29">Tosephta 10:29</a>. About the dangers and rules of the poor climbing trees to claim their <i>peah,</i> cf. <i>Peah</i> 4:1.</i>: “If a worker and a poor man climbed on a tree and broke a branch; if workers are supposed to climb the tree they are not liable, otherwise they are liable.”

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If a river washed away his donkey and another’s donkey; his was worth a mina, the other’s 200. If he abandoned his own and saved the other’s, he only has his wages<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For the work, not for the lost wine.</i>. But if he said, I shall save yours if you pay me the value of mine, he<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner of the more valuable merchandise agreed, he has to keep the terms of this oral contract.</i> is liable to pay him.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If a river washed away his donkey,” etc. It was stated: If he abandoned his to save the other’s, but the other’s climbed out by himself, he does not owe him anything<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 116a" href="/Bava_Kamma.116a">Babli, 116a</a>, it is stated that he is due payment for his work.</i>. But if he abandoned his to save the other’s, and his own climbed out by himself, may he say, “I did not abandon hope of recovery”<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Does the fact that he did not try to save his own donkey imply that he has given up hope of recovering it? If so, the donkey is ownerless and it becomes the property of anybody taking it. If he has not given up hope of recovering it, everybody taking hold of the donkey is required to return it to its owner.</i>? Let us hear from the following: If a river washed away his donkey and he was crying: “I did not abandon hope of recovery!” Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, as long as he is crying he did not abandon hope of recovery. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is a presumption<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">For the meaning of חֲזָקָה, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 5:4:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.5.4.3"><i>Ketubot</i> 5:5, Note 100</a>.</i> that he had abandoned hope.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Somebody had robbed a field which subsequently was taken by discharged veterans<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">“Retired Roman veterans who received a provincial’s property”, from Latin <i>missicius, missitius,</i> adj., “discharged from military service” (M. Gil).<br>In a Genizah ms. of the Mishnah: “If armed robbers took it.” Parallel sources leave no doubt that the Leiden ms. text is the correct one.</i>. If it is a provincial plague, he may tell him: Here is your property before you<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">If the properties of many provincials had been taken, the robber can tell the original owner that it is up to him to try to get indemnification.</i>. But if it was because of the robber<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">If the veterans were directed to take the land because it had come to the attention of the authorities that it was in the wrong hands, the robber has to indemnify the original owner with another field of equal value.</i>, he has to give him a field. If a river had washed it away, he may say to him: Here is your property before you<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since it also would have been damaged in the original owner’s hand.</i>.<br>He who robbed from another, or took a loan from him, or accepted a deposit in a built-up area cannot give it back to him in the desert. [If it was given] with the intent to depart for the desert, he can give it back to him in the desert.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Somebody had robbed a field from another person<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The longer text is that of the Babli and most independent Mishnah mss. (but not the Genizah text of this Mishnah).</i>,” etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">In another formulation, this paragraph appears in <i>Nedarim</i> 4:2 (Notes 27–33) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 13:2:2-7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.13.2.2-7"><i>Ketubot</i> 13:2</a> (35d l. 39).</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, but did they not say that “real estate cannot be robbed”<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Nothing the robber does can give him title to the real estate.</i>? Why did they say, “he has to give him a field”<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the field was taken from the robber, in law it was taken from the robbed.</i>? It is a fine by which they fined him. If the discharged veterans took it from the robber because of the robbed<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Because he was in arrears with payments to the government, the veterans were directed to his property.</i>, could he tell him, in any case give me what is mine and let any claimant come and deal with me? Rebbi Johua ben Levi said, nobody is held responsible for his neighbor and the latter has to repay him except <i>annona</i> and head-tax. Rav said, he can tell him, you redeemed your pledge with my money<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">If the government required A to pay for B’s obligation, B always has to indemnify A. It has to be noted that Rav lived under Parthian and Persian rule, R. Joshua ben Levi in the Roman Empire.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who says to another: I robbed you, or I took a loan from you, you deposited with me, but I do not known whether I returned it to you or did not return it, is liable to pay. But if he said to him: I do not know whether I robbed you, or I took a loan from you, or you deposited with me or did not deposit, he is not liable to pay.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who says to another: I robbed you,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he wants to clear himself before Heaven<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Without a definitive claim from the other party, there is no case for the courts. “Liable” and “not liable” in this Mishnah refer to moral, not legal, obligations. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 118a" href="/Bava_Kamma.118a">Babli 118a</a>.</i>. I robbed you, or I took a loan from you, you deposited with me; did I return it to you? The other party says, I do not know<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to be a reformulation of the Mishnah; in that case R. Jeremiah would disagree with R. Joḥanan. Sabbatai Cohen (<i>Šakh, Ḥošen Mišpaṭ</i> §95 Note 67) reads “<i>You</i> robbed me, etc. I did return it to you, but the other party says, I do not know.”</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah wanted to say, he is required to make good as a matter of law. Rebbi Yose wanted to say, still it is to become clear before Heaven. I took from the wallet and gave to you, and the other party says, I do not know. Rav Huna said, one tells him: You do not know but the other party knows<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Šebuot</i> 6, end (37b l. 64). There are two possible interpretations.<br><i>Šakh</i>: They both agree that something was paid but neither party checked whether the exact amount was given. There is no claim, neither legal nor moral.<br><i>Pene Moshe</i>: One party asserts that the debt was liquidated; the other party is not sure. Neither one has proof. Rav Huna asserts in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 118a" href="/Bava_Kamma.118a">Babli 118a</a> (also <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 12b" href="/Ketubot.12b"><i>Ketubot</i> 12b</a>) that “between ‘certain’ and ‘possible’ claims, the ‘certain’ claim is preferred [by the courts]”.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one stole a lamb from the flock<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">And the owners noticed the theft.</i> and returned it<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Silently, without notifying the owners. Then the lamb still remains his responsibility.</i>, if it died or was stolen he is liable. If the owners were unaware both of the theft and the return, he is no longer liable after they counted the flock and found it complete<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Since the owners did not notice the theft, they do not have to be informed of the return.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “If one stole a lamb from the flock,” etc. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if the owners knew of the theft, they have to be notified of the return. If they did not know of the theft, they do not have to be notified of the return. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, even if they did not know of the theft, they have to be notified of the return<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the first sentence of the Mishnah to apply to all cases, whether the owner knew or did not know. If they did not know, only a count of the flock frees the thief from responsibility.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, if it was similar to the case of a ram<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">In case it is obvious that the theft was returned, even without counting.</i>, they do not have to be notified of the return. What means “similar to the case of a ram”? Some say, the shepherd’s staff. Some say, a <i>pandura</i><sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Greek πανδοῦρα, a kind of guitar.</i>. Some say, the bell-wether.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> One does not buy wool, or milk, or lambs from shepherds<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Since shepherds usually are not the owners of their flock, one has to assume that they sell stolen goods.</i>, nor wood or fruits from orchard watchmen; but one may buy linen garments from women in Galilee, and calves in the Sharon<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In both cases, the trade was in the hand of women who ran the business for their husbands.</i>. In all cases it is forbidden if they<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">If the seller tells the buyer to hide the merchandise, the buyer has to know that he is buying stolen goods. He is forbidden to buy.</i> told to hide. One buys eggs and chickens everywhere.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “One does not buy from shepherds,” etc. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bava Kamma 9:7:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bava_Kamma.9.7.2">Halakhah 9:11, Note 94</a> ff.</i>: “One does not accept deposits from women, or slaves, or underage children. If one accepted from a woman, he should return it to her; if she died, he should return it to her husband.”

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Fibers which remain at the washer’s are his<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">They are a negligible amount.</i>, but what the carder produces<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">It is his job to produce fibers which can be used to manufacture felt.</i> are the consignor’s. The washer takes three threads and they are his, more than that belong to the consignor. If it was black on a white background he takes all of them and they are his<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Threads of another kind added by weavers to the end of a cloth, to be removed when the garment is washed before being worn for the first time.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Fibers which remain at the washer’s,” etc. Abba Hoshaia from Tireh<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">A frequent place name.</i> was a washer. He made himself a tunic<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In the word, ד is not a safe reading. The Venice printer read איסטרכין. Brill reads איסטכרין, Greek στιχάριον, “variegated tunic”.</i> of a single kind of wool lest people say: He wears our property<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Bava Kamma (Lieberman) 11:14" href="/Tosefta_Bava_Kamma_(Lieberman).11.14">Tosephta, 11:14</a>, it is noted that even he took black and white fibers following the Mishnah. This Abba Hoshaia cannot be the fourth generation Amora mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Kohelet Rabbah 1:5" href="/Kohelet_Rabbah.1.5"><i>Eccl. rabba</i> 1:5</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The tailor<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">He receives all materials from the consignor.</i> who had enough thread left to sew with it, and cloth three by three finger breadths<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Which can be used as a handkerchief.</i>, has to return if to its owners. What the woodworker<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">He receives all materials from the consignor.</i> takes off with a small plane is his, with a large one belongs to the consignor; if he works at the latter’s place even the chips are the consignor’s.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The tailor who had enough thread left,” etc. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: A needle full<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Enough thread to start working.</i>. The House of Rebbi Ḥiyya explained: Twice a needle full<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 119b" href="/Bava_Kamma.119b">Babli, 119b</a> (in the name of Rav Assi): Twice the length of the needle.</i>. Bar Qappara stated: The stitching with a needle<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">Enough thread to sew a few permanent stitches of a garment.</i>. The House of Rebbi Yannai explained: twice the stitching of a needle. Do they disagree? What Rebbi Ḥiyya stated, a needle full, the House of Rebbi Ḥiyya explained: Twice a needle full. What Bar Qappara stated: the stitching with a needle, the House of Rebbi Yannai explained: twice the stitching of a needle.
